The Spread of Islam and the Birth of Intellectual Debate
Historical narrative on Islam's spread and resulting debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rapid spread of Islam naturally led to theological discussions that required a disciplined science like kalam.

Core Idea
Islam's rapid expansion sparked debates that demanded a new science of reasoning.

This passage sets the stage for the entire discipline of kalam. Imam al-Maturidi begins by recounting how Islam spread with extraordinary speed after the Prophet's mission, bringing Muslims into contact with diverse beliefs. This historical reality gave rise to intellectual debates—both between Islam and other faiths, and within the Muslim community itself. Understanding this context is essential because kalam was born precisely to address these debates with logical rigor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's description of Islam's spread and the debates that followed.
The OCR may have minor errors; focus on the overall meaning.
2
Identify two types of debate
The passage mentions debates between Islam and other religions, and also debates among Muslims. Mark these in the text.
3
Connect to the need for kalam
Why would these debates lead to a new discipline? Think about the need for a shared method of argument to resolve theological disputes.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the neutral tone
Notice that the passage does not condemn debate; it presents it as a natural historical development. This is important for understanding the role of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد أرسل الله محمدا لله ، إلى البشرية كلها، فبلغ الوحي الإلهي الأخير لها قبل أربعة عشر قرنا ، ثم انتقل إلى جوار ربه ؛ وهذا هو منهج جميع الرسل الذين كانوا دائما في خدمة البشرية لسعادتها وفلاحها إن الإسلام أو الحلقة الأخيرة لدين الله الخالد قد ولد في شبه الجزيرة العربية وانتشر في بلاد مجاورة - لها أديانها وعقائدها وفلسفاتها العديدة - بسرعة عة لم نشاهد مثلها في التاريخ. وهذه الظاهرة التاريخية قد كانت سببًا رئيسيا لظهور جدل ونقاش فكري بين الإسلام وبين الأديان السماوية والثقافات والحضارات الأخرى، كما أصبح المجتمع الإسلامي بمرور الزمن أيضاً مسرحا لهذا الجدل بين نظريات إسلامية عديدة.
Indeed, Allah sent Muhammad, to all of humanity, and he conveyed the final divine revelation to them fourteen centuries ago, then he passed away to his Lord; and this is the way of all messengers who were always in the service of humanity for its happiness and success. Indeed, Islam, or the final link of God's eternal religion, was born in the Arabian Peninsula and spread to neighboring lands - which had their many religions, creeds and philosophies - with a speed we have not seen the like in history. And this historical phenomenon was a main cause for the emergence of intellectual debate and discussion between Islam and the heavenly religions, cultures and other civilizations, and over time the Muslim society also became a stage for this debate among numerous Islamic theories.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a historical observation that sets the stage for the development of kalam. It recounts that Allah sent Muhammad to all humanity with the final revelation. After his death, the pattern of all messengers continued: they serve humanity's happiness. Islam, as the final link of God's eternal religion, was born in Arabia and spread to neighboring regions with unprecedented speed. This rapid expansion brought Islam into contact with various religions, creeds, and philosophies, naturally giving rise to intellectual debates. Moreover, over time, the Muslim community itself became a forum for internal debates among diverse Islamic theories. This sets up the need for a discipline that can adjudicate these discussions—namely, 'ilm al-kalam, which is introduced next.

Key Terms
الوحي الإلهي الأخير (al-waḥy al-ilāhī al-akhīr)
الجدل والنقاش الفكري
نظريات إسلامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage views these debates as negative or problematic.
Correction: The passage presents the debates as a natural historical development, not as something to be condemned.

Checkpoint
What does the passage identify as a main cause for the emergence of intellectual debate?
A) The spread of Islam with great speed
B) The death of the Prophet
C) The birth of Islam in Arabia
D) The number of Islamic theories

Key Takeaway
The rapid expansion of Islam created a pluralistic environment where intellectual debate flourished, and this is the historical backdrop for the development of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief or philosophy different from your own, do you react with the same spirit of intellectual curiosity that early Muslims had, or do you avoid the challenge? What would change if you leaned into the debate?
?
The passage says that debate emerged naturally from contact with other civilizations. Look at your own life: where are you avoiding necessary conversations about faith with people who see things differently? What is one step you can take this week to engage responsibly?
?
If you were a Muslim in the 7th century faced with a foreign philosophy, would you have the courage to defend your faith with reason, or would you retreat into blind assertion? What does your answer reveal about how you approach your beliefs today?

Early Kalam Schools: Mu'tazila, Hanafi, Maturidi, Ash'ari
Introduces four early kalam schools: Mu'tazila, Abu Hanifa, Maturidi, Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila were the first to adopt the kalam method for defending creed, and that later scholars like Abu Hanifa, Maturidi, and Ash'ari engaged with this method.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila pioneered kalam; later scholars continued the method.

This passage from a Maturidi text sets the historical stage: it affirms that the Mu'tazila were the first systematic kalam school, but immediately notes that Abu Hanifa, Maturidi, and Ash'ari also used or adopted the kalam method. This shows the continuity of theological reasoning across early Islamic schools, despite their differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. The original text is incomplete, but the main ideas are clear.
The passage cuts off at 'الذي تبنى' – meaning 'who adopted'.
2
Identify the key claim about Mu'tazila
The passage states without doubt that the Mu'tazila are the first kalam school to use the kalam method for defending the creed.
3
Notice the three additional figures
The text mentions Abu Hanifa, Maturidi, and Ash'ari. Note how each is described in relation to the development of kalam.
Abu Hanifa lived during the emergence of kalam; Maturidi was born about 100 years after Abu Hanifa's death; Ash'ari adopted – the text cuts off.
4
Consider the historical significance
Think about why the author highlights this sequence. It situates Maturidi within a broader tradition of kalam, showing that the method was not limited to one school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما لا شك فيه أن المعتزلة هي أولى مدرسة كلامية لجأت إلى المنهج الكلامي في الدفاع عن العقيدة؛ ومع ذلك، فإن أبا حنيفة الذي كان يعيش أثناء نشأة هذا العلم، وأبا منصور الماتريدي الذي ولد تقريبًا بعد مائة عام من وفاة الإمام، وأبا الحسن الأشعري الذي تبنى
And there is no doubt that the Mu'tazila are the first kalam school that resorted to the kalam method in defending the creed; nevertheless, Abu Hanifa, who lived during the emergence of this science, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who was born approximately one hundred years after the death of the Imam, and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who adopted...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a brief historical sketch of early kalam schools. It states unequivocally that the Mu'tazila were the first to use the kalam method for defending Islamic creed. Then it mentions three other key figures: Abu Hanifa (founder of the Hanafi school) who lived during the time kalam was emerging; Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (the subject of this book) who was born about a century after Abu Hanifa's death; and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who adopted (the text cuts off here). The sentence is incomplete, but the intended contrast is that although the Mu'tazila pioneered kalam, these later figures also engaged in or adopted kalam methodology. This sets up the importance of Maturidi's contribution.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa)
أبو منصور الماتريدي (Abu Mansur al-Maturidi)
أبو الحسن الأشعري (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari)
المنهج الكلامي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Abu Hanifa was a full-fledged kalam theologian like the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The passage only notes that Abu Hanifa lived during the emergence of kalam, not that he practiced it as a method; his relationship to kalam is historical rather than doctrinal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school is described as the first to use the kalam method for defending the creed?
A) The Ash'ari school
B) The Maturidi school
C) The Mu'tazila school
D) The Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila were the first to apply kalam to creedal defense, and later scholars like Maturidi continued this intellectual tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila risked being accused of innovation by using rational discourse to defend faith. Are you willing to seek truth even if it means going against popular opinion or facing criticism from your community? What would it take for you to courageously defend what you believe, even when it's uncomfortable?
?
Abu Hanifa lived during the rise of kalam but did not extensively engage in theological debate himself. Is there an area of your faith where you have avoided deep reflection or study because it seemed too challenging or controversial? Will you commit to exploring one such topic this month, seeking guidance from reliable scholars?
?
Maturidi and Ash'ari came after the Mu'tazila and built upon the kalam method to preserve orthodox creed. What legacy are you building for those who come after you? Are you merely consuming knowledge without contributing, or are you actively seeking to understand and share the truth so that others may benefit from your efforts?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Rafidah on Imamate
Lists Maturidi's book refuting some Rafidah on Imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which work Maturidi authored in refutation of certain Rafidah on the doctrine of Imamate and who the likely target of that refutation was.

Core Idea
Maturidi refuted al-Rawandi on Imamate.

This brief passage from Maturidi's biographical tradition informs us of one of his lost works: a refutation of the Rafidah's book on Imamate. Scholars have worked to identify the intended recipient of this refutation, and the passage presents the conclusion that Maturidi was responding to the Mu'tazili-leaning scholar Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Rāwandī. Let us examine the details and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the names mentioned: al-Nasafi, al-Maghribi, Ayyub Ali, and al-Rawandi.
2
Identify the lost work
The passage mentions a book titled 'Refutation of the Book of Imamate of Some Rafidah'. This is a lost work by Maturidi. Understand that 'Rafidah' is a term for certain Shia groups.
The work is known only through references like this one.
3
Understand the scholarly identification
Professor Ayyub Ali concluded that the refutation is directed against Abū al-Ḥusayn Aḥmad ibn Yaḥyā al-Rāwandī, a scholar with Mu'tazili and Shia leanings. This shows Maturidi's engagement with sectarian debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹ - رد كتاب الإمامة لبعض الروافض ذكره النسفي بهذا الاسم دون وصف له كما ذكره كثير من الباحثين المعاصرين ؛ وقد أشار إليه على عبد الفتاح المغربي باسم كتاب رد الإمامة لبعض الروافض ، وقد حاول الأستاذ أيوب علي التعرف على المقصود بالمشار إليه في الكتاب، فرجح أنه يرد فيه على أبي الحسين أحمد بن يحيى الراوندي
9 - Refutation of the Book of Imamate of Some Rafidah. Al-Nasafi mentioned it by this name without describing it, as did many contemporary researchers. And ‘Alī ‘Abd al-Fattāḥ al-Maghribī referred to it as the Book of Refutation of the Imamate of Some Rafidah. Professor Ayyūb ‘Alī tried to identify the intended reference in the book and concluded that he refutes Abū al-Ḥusayn Aḥmad ibn Yaḥyā al-Rāwandī.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage lists another of al-Maturidi's theological works, a refutation of certain Rafidah (a term for some Shia groups) regarding the doctrine of Imamate. It notes that al-Nasafi mentioned the book without description, and contemporary researchers also mention it. The passage further explains that ‘Alī ‘Abd al-Fattāḥ al-Maghribī refers to it with a slightly different title. Finally, it mentions the attempt of Professor Ayyūb ‘Alī to identify the target of the refutation, concluding that Maturidi was refuting Abū al-Ḥusayn Aḥmad ibn Yaḥyā al-Rāwandī, a known Mu'tazili or Shia scholar.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الروافض
الراوندي
النسفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This refutation was directed against all Shia or against the Rafidah in general.
Correction: It is specifically a response to a particular book on Imamate by some Rafidah, and scholars have identified the author of that book as al-Rawandi, a Mu'tazili with Shia sympathies.

Checkpoint
Whom does Maturidi likely refute in his work 'Refutation of the Book of Imamate of Some Rafidah' according to Ayyūb ‘Alī?
A) Al-Ka'bi
B) Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Rāwandī
C) Abū al-Qāsim al-Balkhī
D) Al-Nasafi

Key Takeaway
Maturidi wrote a refutation of a Rafidah book on Imamate, directed against al-Rawandi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows Maturidi actively refuting a book on Imamate. When have you encountered a belief that you knew was wrong but did not have the courage or knowledge to challenge? What prevented you from speaking up or writing a rebuttal? Are you willing to prepare yourself now so that you can defend the truth when needed?
?
al-Rawandi was a scholar of kalām who adopted some Shia ideas. Are there areas in your own understanding where you have mixed truth with falsehood without realizing it? What active steps will you take this week to examine your beliefs against the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Maturidi's work is now lost to history, yet his effort is remembered. If you were to write something today that defends orthodox Islam, what would it be about and who would it help? What is stopping you from starting today?

Maturidi's Work 'Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila'
Lists Maturidi's book and its alternate titles in sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Maturidi authored a work called 'Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila' and how it is referenced with a slight title variation in different scholarly sources.

Core Idea
One book, two titles: Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila and Bayān Awhām al-Mu'tazila.

This lesson introduces another work by Imam al-Maturidi, dedicated to exposing errors in Mu'tazilite thought. The passage notes how different sources refer to it with slight variations in its title. Understanding these citations helps us trace the transmission of Maturidi's writings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the English translation. Notice the different names and sources mentioned.
The passage is a bibliographical note, not doctrinal.
2
Identify the book titles
Find the two versions of the title: 'Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila' and 'Kitāb Bayān Awhām al-Mu'tazila'. Note the difference in wording.
'Wahm' means error, 'Awhām' means illusions.
3
Note the sources
Identify the three sources that mention the first title (al-Nasafi, al-Baghdadi, Brockelmann) and the one source that uses the variant (al-Jawāhir al-Muḍiyya).
4
Reflect on title variations
Consider why manuscripts sometimes have slight title changes. This could be due to scribal errors or regional differences.
This is common in Islamic manuscript traditions.
5
Connect to broader study
Recognize that such details help scholars authenticate works and understand their transmission history.
This passage is a small piece of a larger scholarly puzzle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۰ - بيان وهم المعتزلة ذكره أبو المعين النسفي في تبصرة الأدلة والبغدادي في هدية العارفين بهذا الاسم، كما ورد اسمه أيضاً هكذا عند بروكلمان؛ غير أنه ذُكر في الجواهر المضية باسم كتاب بيان أوهام المعتزلة.
10 - Bayān Wahm al-Mu‘tazila (Exposition of the Error of the Mu‘tazila). Abū al-Mu‘īn al-Nasafī mentioned it in Tabṣirat al-Adilla and al-Baghdādī in Hadiyyat al-‘Ārifīn by this name, and its name also appears thus in Brockelmann; however, it was mentioned in al-Jawāhir al-Muḍiyya with the name Kitāb Bayān Awhām al-Mu‘tazila (Book of Exposition of the Illusions of the Mu‘tazila).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage lists a third theological work by al-Maturidi, titled 'Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila' (Exposition of the Error of the Mu'tazila). It notes the sources that mention the book: al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla and al-Baghdadi's Hadiyyat al-'Arifin use this title, and Brockelmann also records it under this name. However, the work is referred to in al-Jawāhir al-Muḍiyya with a slightly different name: 'Kitāb Bayān Awhām al-Mu'tazila' (Book of Exposition of the Illusions of the Mu'tazila), indicating a variation in the title among sources.

Key Terms
بيان وهم المعتزلة
تبصرة الأدلة
هدية العارفين
الجواهر المضية
بيان أوهام المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the different titles refer to different books.
Correction: They are likely the same work with a minor variation in wording.

Checkpoint

B) Kitāb Bayān Awhām al-Mu'tazila
C) Kitāb al-Jadal

Key Takeaway
The work 'Bayān Wahm al-Mu'tazila' is also known as 'Kitāb Bayān Awhām al-Mu'tazila' in some sources, illustrating typical title variations in Islamic manuscript traditions.

Khawarij and Mu'tazila on Major Sins
Maturidi enumerates sectarian views on major sins and announces refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila regarding the status of a believer who commits a major sin, and Maturidi's initial critique of these views.

Core Idea
Major sins do not necessarily expel one from faith entirely.

Maturidi begins by outlining the views of two major sectarian groups on the issue of major sins. Understanding these positions is essential for grasping his subsequent critique and positive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sectarian views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to identify the two groups' positions on major sins.
Notice how both groups agree that major sins remove one from faith, but differ on whether the sinner becomes an unbeliever.
2
Identify Maturidi's refutation approach
Maturidi states that he will refute these views with severe criticism. Observe his characterization of these groups as opposing text, reason, and reality.
This sets the stage for his arguments in the following lessons.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this issue matters in Islamic theology. The classification of major sins affects community boundaries and the concept of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد أفاد الماتريدي بأن البعض من الخوارج يعتقدون أن الذنوب كلها، صغيرها وكبيرها، تخرج المؤمن عن دائرة الدين كما أشار إلى أن أغلب الخوارج ومتكلمي المعتزلة يعتقدون بأن ارتكاب الكبيرة يخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان فمرتكب الكبيرة عند الخوارج يكفر، وعند المعتزلة لا يكفر رغم خروجه عن الإيمان. والبعض يرى أنه منافق' . فالماتريدي يشير إلى جميع تلك الآراء ويذكر أدلتهم، ثم يوجه إليهم انتقادات شديدة اللهجة ، فيصف منتحليهم بأنهم معارضون للنص والعقل والواقع وأنهم غير منصفين. فالماتريدي الذي يحلل الحالة النفسية للمؤمن الذي يعرض أحوالاً قبيحة أو الإهمال السيئ، فهو من ناحية أولى يشير إلى مطابقته للواقع في المجتمع الإسلامي، ومن ناحية أخرى يقدم أمثلة للأخلاق النبوي الموصوف بأنه رؤف رحيم للمؤمنين ﴾ [التوبة، ۱۲۸/۹]. ويعني ذلك في نظره أنه ما من أحد يعصى الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو في حالة ارتكاب الكبيرة مكتسب الطاعة نفسيا ؛ فإنه يحمل في داخل نفسه أحاسيس الخوف من العقاب الإلهي، والفزع عن مقته تعالى، والرجاء برحمته والثقة بكرمه. فهذه الأحاسيس التي تظهر في صورة خيرات في نفسه فهي الأحاسيس التي قد تكون غالبة على الذنوب التي ارتكبها بغلبة الأسباب الدافعة إلى تلك السيئات . فعلينا في هذا النطاق أن نتذكر ببعض ما أنعم الله على رتكب الكبيرة من النعم ؛ فقد أحسن الله إليه بوضع حب الله تعالى وحب أوليائه في قلبه، كما أعطى له الإحساس باحترام الحق الإلهي وحقوق أوليائه. فالله تعالى يؤمّن خلقه بأن لا يضيع جميع ما أكرمهم به من هذه النعم. وفى الحقيقة فإن الناس جميعًا، وإن اختلفت أديانهم التي اعتقدوها، يصممون أن يقفوا صادقين أمام معتقداتهم، وينفرون عن معاملات تبعدهم من أديانهم، فلن تتغير النتائج وإن كانوا اعتقدوها عن طريق الاستدلال العقلى أو المعجزة أو التقليد .
Al-Maturidi informed that some of the Khawarij believe that all sins, small and great, remove the believer from the circle of religion, and he indicated that most of the Khawarij and the theologians of the Mu'tazila believe that committing a major sin removes its doer from faith, so the committer of a major sin among the Khawarij becomes an unbeliever, and among the Mu'tazila he does not become an unbeliever despite his removal from faith. And some consider him a hypocrite. Al-Maturidi refers to all these opinions and mentions their proofs, then directs severe criticisms to them, describing their claimants as opposed to the text, reason, and reality, and as unfair. Al-Maturidi, who analyzes the psychological state of the believer who exhibits ugly states or bad neglect, on the one hand indicates its correspondence to reality in Islamic society, and on the other hand presents examples of the prophetic character described as kind and merciful to the believers [At-Tawbah 9:128]. That means in his view that there is no one who disobeys God with any major sin except polytheism, but he, while committing the major sin, is psychologically acquiring obedience; for he carries within himself feelings of fear of divine punishment, dread of His anger, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These feelings, which appear as good things in his soul, are the feelings that may overcome the sins he committed by the predominance of the causes driving to those evils. So in this context we should remember some of the blessings that God has bestowed upon the committer of a major sin: God has favored him by placing love of God and love of His friends in his heart, and gave him the sense of respect for divine right and the rights of His friends. God guarantees His creation that He will not waste all that He has honored them with from these blessings. And in truth, all people, even if their religions differ, are determined to stand sincerely before their beliefs, and they are averse to actions that distance them from their religions, and the results will not change even if they hold them through rational inference or miracle or imitation.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's critique of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions on the status of a believer who commits a major sin. It begins by stating that the Khawarij believe all sins remove the believer from the fold of religion, and that most Khawarij and Mu'tazila theologians hold that a major sin removes one from faith, with the difference that Khawarij declare him an unbeliever while Mu'tazila do not consider him an unbeliever but still out of faith. Some even consider him a hypocrite. Maturidi acknowledges these opinions and states that he will refute them with severe criticism, describing their proponents as opposing scripture, reason, and reality. He then shifts to analyzing the psychological state of the believer who commits sins: such a person, even when sinning, still harbors fear of God's punishment, dread of His displeasure, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These positive feelings may overcome the sins. Maturidi then reminds us of God's blessings on the sinner: God has placed love of Him and His saints in his heart, and given him a sense of respect for divine rights. God guarantees that He will not waste these blessings. The passage concludes with a general observation that people from all religions are sincere to their beliefs and averse to actions that distance them from their religion, regardless of how they adopted their beliefs.

Key Terms
الخوارج
المعتزلة
الكبائر
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing a major sin automatically makes a person an unbeliever.
Correction: While Khawarij hold this view, Maturidi and others reject it. The Mu'tazila say he leaves faith but is not an unbeliever, and Maturidi offers a more nuanced position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the Khawarij and Mu'tazila regarding the committer of a major sin?
A) The Khawarij consider him a hypocrite, the Mu'tazila consider him an unbeliever.
B) The Khawarij consider him an unbeliever, the Mu'tazila consider him out of faith but not an unbeliever.
C) Both consider him an unbeliever.
D) Both consider him a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij and Mu'tazila agree that major sins remove one from faith, but differ on whether the sinner is an unbeliever; Maturidi criticizes both views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone who struggles with a major sin, do you ever distance yourself from them in your heart, thinking they are somehow less of a believer? How does this lesson force you to check that impulse?
?
If you have ever committed a sin you considered 'major', did you lose hope in your own faith? Does Maturidi's rejection of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila views give you a reason to hold onto iman even after falling short?
?
Are there any 'small' sins you ignore, thinking they don't affect your faith? This passage shows that some Khawarij considered all sins, even small ones, as removing from religion. Does that extreme make you reconsider your own neglect of minor sins?

The Sinner's Inner Faith: Psychological Analysis
Maturidi analyzes the psychological state of the sinner believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's psychological argument that a believer who commits a major sin still possesses inner faith manifested as fear of God, hope, and trust, which constitute obedience.

Core Idea
Even in sin, the believer carries seeds of faith that outweigh the sin.

Maturidi moves from critique to a constructive argument. He analyzes the internal state of a believer who falls into major sin, highlighting the positive emotions that remain. This serves as evidence that the person is not entirely devoid of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the psychological analysis
Read the Arabic passage and translation describing the sinner's inner feelings: fear, dread, hope, and trust.
Notice that Maturidi calls these feelings 'acquiring obedience' even during sin.
2
Understand the argument
Maturidi argues that these positive feelings can overcome the sins because of the predominance of causes driving to evil. The sinner still has goodness in his soul.
This is a psychological defense of the sinner's faith status.
3
Relate to the broader issue
Consider how this argument counters the Khawarij and Mu'tazila claim that major sins remove faith entirely. Maturidi shows remnants of faith persist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد أفاد الماتريدي بأن البعض من الخوارج يعتقدون أن الذنوب كلها، صغيرها وكبيرها، تخرج المؤمن عن دائرة الدين كما أشار إلى أن أغلب الخوارج ومتكلمي المعتزلة يعتقدون بأن ارتكاب الكبيرة يخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان فمرتكب الكبيرة عند الخوارج يكفر، وعند المعتزلة لا يكفر رغم خروجه عن الإيمان. والبعض يرى أنه منافق' . فالماتريدي يشير إلى جميع تلك الآراء ويذكر أدلتهم، ثم يوجه إليهم انتقادات شديدة اللهجة ، فيصف منتحليهم بأنهم معارضون للنص والعقل والواقع وأنهم غير منصفين. فالماتريدي الذي يحلل الحالة النفسية للمؤمن الذي يعرض أحوالاً قبيحة أو الإهمال السيئ، فهو من ناحية أولى يشير إلى مطابقته للواقع في المجتمع الإسلامي، ومن ناحية أخرى يقدم أمثلة للأخلاق النبوي الموصوف بأنه رؤف رحيم للمؤمنين ﴾ [التوبة، ۱۲۸/۹]. ويعني ذلك في نظره أنه ما من أحد يعصى الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو في حالة ارتكاب الكبيرة مكتسب الطاعة نفسيا ؛ فإنه يحمل في داخل نفسه أحاسيس الخوف من العقاب الإلهي، والفزع عن مقته تعالى، والرجاء برحمته والثقة بكرمه. فهذه الأحاسيس التي تظهر في صورة خيرات في نفسه فهي الأحاسيس التي قد تكون غالبة على الذنوب التي ارتكبها بغلبة الأسباب الدافعة إلى تلك السيئات . فعلينا في هذا النطاق أن نتذكر ببعض ما أنعم الله على رتكب الكبيرة من النعم ؛ فقد أحسن الله إليه بوضع حب الله تعالى وحب أوليائه في قلبه، كما أعطى له الإحساس باحترام الحق الإلهي وحقوق أوليائه. فالله تعالى يؤمّن خلقه بأن لا يضيع جميع ما أكرمهم به من هذه النعم. وفى الحقيقة فإن الناس جميعًا، وإن اختلفت أديانهم التي اعتقدوها، يصممون أن يقفوا صادقين أمام معتقداتهم، وينفرون عن معاملات تبعدهم من أديانهم، فلن تتغير النتائج وإن كانوا اعتقدوها عن طريق الاستدلال العقلى أو المعجزة أو التقليد .
Al-Maturidi informed that some of the Khawarij believe that all sins, small and great, remove the believer from the circle of religion, and he indicated that most of the Khawarij and the theologians of the Mu'tazila believe that committing a major sin removes its doer from faith, so the committer of a major sin among the Khawarij becomes an unbeliever, and among the Mu'tazila he does not become an unbeliever despite his removal from faith. And some consider him a hypocrite. Al-Maturidi refers to all these opinions and mentions their proofs, then directs severe criticisms to them, describing their claimants as opposed to the text, reason, and reality, and as unfair. Al-Maturidi, who analyzes the psychological state of the believer who exhibits ugly states or bad neglect, on the one hand indicates its correspondence to reality in Islamic society, and on the other hand presents examples of the prophetic character described as kind and merciful to the believers [At-Tawbah 9:128]. That means in his view that there is no one who disobeys God with any major sin except polytheism, but he, while committing the major sin, is psychologically acquiring obedience; for he carries within himself feelings of fear of divine punishment, dread of His anger, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These feelings, which appear as good things in his soul, are the feelings that may overcome the sins he committed by the predominance of the causes driving to those evils. So in this context we should remember some of the blessings that God has bestowed upon the committer of a major sin: God has favored him by placing love of God and love of His friends in his heart, and gave him the sense of respect for divine right and the rights of His friends. God guarantees His creation that He will not waste all that He has honored them with from these blessings. And in truth, all people, even if their religions differ, are determined to stand sincerely before their beliefs, and they are averse to actions that distance them from their religions, and the results will not change even if they hold them through rational inference or miracle or imitation.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's critique of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions on the status of a believer who commits a major sin. It begins by stating that the Khawarij believe all sins remove the believer from the fold of religion, and that most Khawarij and Mu'tazila theologians hold that a major sin removes one from faith, with the difference that Khawarij declare him an unbeliever while Mu'tazila do not consider him an unbeliever but still out of faith. Some even consider him a hypocrite. Maturidi acknowledges these opinions and states that he will refute them with severe criticism, describing their proponents as opposing scripture, reason, and reality. He then shifts to analyzing the psychological state of the believer who commits sins: such a person, even when sinning, still harbors fear of God's punishment, dread of His displeasure, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. These positive feelings may overcome the sins. Maturidi then reminds us of God's blessings on the sinner: God has placed love of Him and His saints in his heart, and given him a sense of respect for divine rights. God guarantees that He will not waste these blessings. The passage concludes with a general observation that people from all religions are sincere to their beliefs and averse to actions that distance them from their religion, regardless of how they adopted their beliefs.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق بالقلب
الشفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who commits a major sin has no good in them and is purely evil.
Correction: Maturidi argues that even a sinner retains positive spiritual feelings like fear of God and hope, which constitute obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does a person who commits a major sin (excluding polytheism) still carry within himself?
A) Only fear of punishment
B) Feelings of fear, dread, hope, and trust in God
C) Complete disbelief
D) Hypocrisy

Key Takeaway
The inner feelings of fear and hope in God that a sinner possesses prove that faith is not completely lost, and these feelings can outweigh the sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you recognize within yourself the fear of God's punishment and hope for His mercy, or have these feelings grown cold? What does that reveal about the state of your faith?
?
You carry love of God and His friends in your heart—do you actively nurture these feelings, or do you take them for granted while continuing in sin?
?
Maturidi says these inner feelings may outweigh your sins. Is your life evidence of this balance, or are your sins overwhelming the good feelings? What will you change today to tip the scale?

Universality of Sincerity to Beliefs
Maturidi observes universal sincerity to beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concluding observation that people from all religions are sincere to their beliefs and averse to actions that contradict them, regardless of how they adopted their beliefs.

Core Idea
Sincere adherence to belief is universal across religions.

Maturidi closes his argument with a broader anthropological observation. He notes that all people, irrespective of how they came to their faith, are naturally inclined to be sincere to their beliefs and shun actions that distance them from it. This reinforces the idea that a Muslim sinner still has fundamental loyalty to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding observation
Read the final part of the passage and its translation. Notice the universal statement about all people.
Maturidi includes those who believe through rational inference, miracle, or imitation.
2
Analyze the argument
Maturidi claims that this sincerity is universal and the results do not change based on how belief was acquired. This supports his view that inner faith persists.
This counters the idea that only certain types of belief are valid.
3
Connect to the main theme
Reflect on how this observation strengthens Maturidi's overall defense of the sinner's faith. If even non-Muslims are sincere to their beliefs, a Muslim sinner's inner faith is even more profound.

?

Why 'Attribute' is Preferable to 'Accident'
Maturidi argues terminological preference and refutes al-Ka'bī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the term 'attribute' (ṣifa) is preferable to 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) for describing non-body entities, and why naming in kalam is conventional.

Core Idea
Names in theology are conventions, not rational deductions.

Imam Maturidi turns from proving the existence of accidents to a terminological debate: what should we call them? Some scholars say 'accidents', others 'attributes'. Maturidi argues that the name is a matter of convention, not reason, and that 'attribute' aligns better with Quranic usage. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and his critique of al-Ka'bī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi begins by stating that the distinction is established and then introduces the disagreement.
2
Identify the two positions and Maturidi's principle
The passage presents two views: calling the entity 'accident' or 'attribute'. Find Maturidi's principle that names are conventional and not derived by reason or analogy.
He says 'the correct view is to submit to what is conventionally used'.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence and conclusion
Maturidi then refutes al-Ka'bī by showing that the term 'accident' in the Qur'an means 'worldly goods', not the technical kalam accident. Therefore, 'attribute' is closer to Islamic nomenclature.
The Quranic verses are from Surah al-Anfal (8:67) and Surah al-Tawba (9:42).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قد ثبت التغاير ، واختلف أهل الكلام في مائية اسم ذلك. فمنهم من يسميه عرضاً، ومنهم من يسمّيه صفة وحق هذه المسألة التسليم لما يجرى الاصطلاح به، لما يراد بالتسمية التعريف وإفهام المراد ، فأي شيء يعمل ذلك كفى، ولا يُغرف الاسم بالعقل والقياس. كذلك قولنا في تخطئة قول الكعبي : إنه لما ثبت أنه ليس بجسم بان أنه (عرض . {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } وإنما يجب ذلك إذا سلم أنّ ما سوى الجسم من الخلق عرض، وفي كتاب الله تسمية العرض على إرادة أعين الأشياء، كقوله تعالى: ﴿تريدون عرض الدنيا ﴾ [الأنفال ، ٦٧/٨] ، وقوله : ولو كان عرضاً قريبا ﴾ [التوبة، ٤٢/٩]، فعلى هذا تسمية ذلك صفة أقرب إلى الأسماء الإسلامية . ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then distinction has been established, and the people of kalam differed regarding the quiddity of its name. Some of them call it 'accident' (ʿaraḍ), and some of them call it 'attribute' (ṣifa). The correct view in this matter is to submit to what is conventionally used, because the purpose of naming is definition and conveying intended meaning; so anything that achieves that suffices, and the name is not known by reason and analogy. Similarly, our statement in refuting the saying of al-Kaʿbī: since it is established that it is not a body, it becomes clear that it is an accident. {Abū Manṣūr said:} That is only necessary if it is conceded that what is other than the body from creation is an accident, while in the Book of God the term 'accident' is used to mean the goods of things, as His saying: {You desire the accident of this world} [al-Anfāl 8:67], and His saying: {If it had been a near accident} [al-Tawba 9:42]. According to this, calling that [attribute] a 'ṣifa' is closer to Islamic nomenclature. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the correct terminology for the entities distinct from the body (i.e., accidents/attributes). Maturidi notes that scholars disagree on whether to call them 'accidents' (aʿrāḍ) or 'attributes' (ṣifāt). He argues that the name is conventional and should be accepted as per usage, since the purpose is communication, not rational deduction. He then criticizes al-Kaʿbī for claiming that whatever is not a body is necessarily an accident. Maturidi shows that the term 'accident' in the Qur'an refers to worldly goods, not to the philosophical accidents of kalam. Therefore, using 'attribute' (ṣifa) is more appropriate for Islamic discourse. He concludes that the choice is conventional, but 'ṣifa' is preferable.

Key Terms
عرض
صفة
تغاير
اصطلاح
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the term 'accident' is rationally necessary for entities that are not bodies.
Correction: Maturidi argues that names are conventional and that the Qur'an uses 'accident' for worldly goods, so 'attribute' is preferable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is calling accidents 'attributes' (ṣifa) more appropriate than calling them 'accidents' (ʿaraḍ)?
A) Because the term 'accident' implies impermanence while 'attribute' implies permanence.
B) Because in the Qur'an, 'accident' refers to worldly goods, not the technical accidents of kalam.
C) Because al-Ka'bī insisted on calling them accidents.
D) Because reason dictates that attributes are not accidents.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that theological terminology is based on convention and Quranic usage, not pure reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's attributes, do you choose your words carefully based on the Qur'an and the way of the Salaf, or do you rely on terms that are intellectually fashionable? How can you develop deeper reverence for the names you use for your Creator?
?
Maturidi says 'the name is not known by reason and analogy.' In your own life, have you ever assumed that your rational conclusions about God are automatically correct without checking them against divine revelation? What would it take for you to submit your theological vocabulary to the Book of God?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' Do you truly believe that even your ability to use correct terminology comes only from Him? In what specific ways can you cultivate a constant sense of dependence on God when you learn or teach matters of faith?

Refuting the Manichaean Claim of the World's Eternity
Maturidi refutes the Manichaean dualist view of the eternal world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Manichaean dualist view by exposing internal contradictions in their narrative, particularly the temporality of the mixture and the implications for light's power and knowledge.

Core Idea
Dualism's own logic proves the world is temporal, not eternal.

Imam Maturidi takes on the Manichaean claim that the world is eternal and composed of two eternal principles: light and darkness. He exposes internal contradictions in their narrative, showing that their story of a temporal mixture logically implies a temporal world. This lesson walks through his incisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist claim
The Manichaeans claim the world is eternal and made from the mixture of two eternal principles: light and darkness. They assert these were originally separate, then mixed to form the world.
This sets the stage for Maturidi's critique.
2
Identify Maturidi's first argument
Maturidi points out that the mixture itself is temporal (حادث), as it came after the state of separation. Since the world arises from this mixture, it cannot be eternal; it must be temporal.
This is a direct refutation of the world's eternity from their own premises.
3
Analyze Mani's specific narrative
Maturidi focuses on Mani's version: light saw darkness, was attacked, and created the world to free its trapped parts. This implies light was powerless to prevent the attack and ignorant of when it would happen.
Contradicts light's supposed perfection.
4
See the final absurdity
If light is the originator of the world, why is most of the world evil? Light would have done evil to escape evil, which is contradictory. This shows the incoherence of dualism.
Maturidi concludes that dualists are ignorant and far from truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم مَنْ يقول بالاثنين - الظُّلْمة والنور - بقدم العالم. وأحقِّ مَنْ يأبى ذلك من يقول بهذا؛ إذ من قولهم : إنهما كانا متباينين فامتزجا فكان العالم من امتزاجهما. ومعلوم أن الامتزاج كان حادثا، إذ التباين كان هو المتقدم ولم يكونا يُلقبان بالعالم. إلا أن يقولوا: النور والظلمة جوهران اختلفا، كانا في الأصل بمكانها، فكان مكان النور نور كله وخير، ومكان الظلمة ظلمة كلها وشر، فيبطل القول بقدم العالم الممتزج وبخاصة قول الماني حيث زعم أن النور لما رأى الظلمة قَدَحَتْ فيه ومازجت به أحدث هذا العالم ليتخلّص بذلك أجزاء النور من أجزاء الظلمة، فصار العالم على هذا القول بعد الامتزاج المحدث؛ فيكون بعد المحدَث قديما، وذلك هو التجاهل. فأوجبوا عجز النور وقت كونه في سلطانه بجميع أعوانه من الخيرات وأنصاره من الحسنات، حيث لم يقدر على الامتناع من قدح الظلمة وأحد أجزائه عنه، وجهلوه بوقت القدح فيه ليتخلص عنه ، ثم زعموا أنه أحدث هذا العالم ليخلّص أجزاءه منها بعد أن صار في وثاقها. هيهات ما أبْعَدَهم عن ذلك، وما أجهل مَن يقدمونه ويجعلون له كل خير؛ وأول كل خير علم ، وقد جهل ما ذُكِر ؛ وعِظَم كل خير بقوة، وقد عجز من حفظه في أقوى أحواله. ثم إذ كان هو المنشئ للعالم كيف صار أكثر العالم شراً ؟ فهو إذن فَعَل الشر ليتخلص به من وثاق الشر، فكأنه أعان الشر والظلمة، إذ هو عمل ذلك. ثم قد زاد من أجزائها في أجزاء النور
Then those who say by the two (things) - darkness and light - claimed the eternity of the world. And the most rightful of those who reject that is the one who says this; for from their statement is that they were separate, then mixed, and the world came from their mixture. And it is known that the mixture was temporal, because separation was preceding and they were not called the world. Unless they say: light and darkness are two substances that differed, originally in their place, so the place of light was all light and good, and the place of darkness all darkness and evil; so the statement of the eternity of the mixed world is invalid, especially the statement of Mani, who claimed that when light saw darkness, it attacked it and mixed with it, and he brought about this world to thereby free the parts of light from the parts of darkness. So the world according to this statement came after the temporal mixture; then it would be after the temporal thing eternal, and that is ignorance. So they necessitated the inability of light at the time of its being in its domain with all its aids of good things and its supporters of good deeds, as it was not able to prevent the attack of darkness and one of its parts from it, and they made it ignorant of the time of the attack on it to free itself from it. Then they claimed that it brought about this world to free its parts from it after it had become in its shackles. How far are they from that! And how ignorant is the one whom they advance and make for him all good; and the first of all good is knowledge, and he was ignorant of what was mentioned; and the greatness of all good is power, and he was powerless to protect himself in his strongest state. Then when he is the originator of the world, how did most of the world become evil? So then he did evil to free himself from the shackles of evil, as if he aided evil and darkness, for he did that. Then he increased from its parts in the parts of light
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists (الثنوية), specifically the Manichaeans, who claim that the world is eternal and composed of two eternal principles: light and darkness. Maturidi first points out that the dualists themselves say the world arose from the mixture of light and darkness after they were separate. Since mixture is temporal (حادث), the world is temporal, not eternal. He then focuses on Mani's specific view: light saw darkness, was attacked, and then created the world to free itself from darkness. This implies that light was powerless and ignorant at the time of the attack, contradicting its supposed goodness and power. Further, if light is the originator of the world, why is most of the world evil? It would have done evil to escape evil, which is contradictory. Maturidi exposes the absurdity in their reasoning using their own premises.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism provides a coherent explanation of the world's origin.
Correction: Maturidi shows dualism leads to contradictions about the nature of light, making it incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw Maturidi finds in Mani's account?
A) The mixture is temporal, so the world cannot be eternal
B) Light is both good and evil
C) Darkness created itself
D) The world is composed of only one substance

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by demonstrating that its own narrative forces the conclusion that the world is temporal and that the good principle is neither all-powerful nor all-knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil or suffering in your own life, do you ever find yourself questioning whether God is truly all-powerful or all-knowing? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Manichaean contradiction—that their 'good principle' was ignorant and powerless—help you reaffirm your trust in Allah's perfect attributes? This week, when a trial strikes, catch yourself and consciously recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' to reject any dualistic thought.
?
The Manichaeans believed that light had to do evil (create the world) to rescue itself from darkness. In what areas of your life do you rationalize a 'small sin' because you think it will lead to a greater good or solve a problem? Confront that excuse today with the truth that Allah does not need evil to bring about good, and repent if you have compromised.
?
Maturidi points out that if light is the originator of the world, then most of the world being evil means light caused evil. Do you sometimes blame God for the evil in the world as if He is not fully in control of good and evil? This lesson demands that you submit to the fact that all evil is permitted by Allah's wisdom, and your role is not to judge His decree but to respond with faith and patience. Write down one instance where you can practice this submission today.

General Refutation of Dualists on Pre-Eternity of the World
Maturidi refutes dualist claim that the world is pre-eternal by showing mixture is contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualist claim of a pre-eternal world by demonstrating that the mixture of light and darkness is contingent, thus the world cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
The world cannot be pre-eternal because its origin (mixture) is contingent.

This passage presents Imam al-Maturidi's concise argument against dualist cosmologies that posit two eternal principles. By focusing on the logical status of the mixture that supposedly produces the world, he demonstrates that a composite world cannot be pre-eternal. The argument is a powerful example of kalam reasoning on divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist claim
Read the passage and note what the dualists assert: the world arises from the mixture of two eternal principles, light and darkness.
The dualists claim the world is pre-eternal because its components are eternal.
2
Recognize the refutation
Maturidi points out that the mixture itself is contingent (ḥādith) because the state of separation preceded it. Since the mixture is not eternal, the world resulting from it cannot be eternal either.
Contingency of the cause implies contingency of the effect.
3
Consider the dualist objection
The dualists might argue that light and darkness originally occupied distinct places. Maturidi shows that even if true, this does not affect the contingency of the mixture.
The objection does not save the claim of pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أقاويل الثنوية في قدم العالم وغيره] ثم زعم مَنْ يقول بالاثنين - الظُّلْمة والنور - بقدم العالم. وأحق مَنْ يأبى ذلك من يقول بهذا؛ إذ من قولهم : إنهما كانا متباينين فامتزجا فكان العالم من امتزاجهما. ومعلوم أن الامتزاج كان حادثا، إذ التباين كان هو المتقدم ولم يكونا يُلقبان بالعالم. إلا أن يقولوا: النور والظلمة جوهران اختلفا، كانا في الأصل بمكانها، فكان مكان النور نور كله / وخير، ومكان الظلمة ظلمة كلها وشر، فيبطل القول بقدم العالم الممتزج
[Sayings of the dualists concerning the pre-eternity of the world and other matters] Then he who says with the two (principles) – darkness and light – claimed the pre-eternity of the world. And the most rightful of those who reject that is the one who says this; for it is from their saying: They were separate then mixed, and the world came to be from their mixture. And it is known that the mixture was contingent (ḥādith), since the separation was what preceded, and they were not called the world. Unless they say: Light and darkness are two substances that differed, they were originally in their place, so the place of light was all light and good, and the place of darkness was all darkness and evil, so the statement of the pre-eternity of the mixed world is invalidated.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a general refutation of the dualist claim that the world is pre-eternal because it originates from two eternal principles, light and darkness. Maturidi begins by stating the dualist position: they claim the world is pre-eternal because light and darkness were originally separate, then mixed to form the world. He then presents the refutation: the mixture is contingent (ḥādith) because the state of separation preceded it, and before mixture, the two principles were not called 'the world'. Therefore the world, being a product of mixture, is contingent. He then considers a possible counterargument from the dualists: that light and darkness originally occupied distinct places, one all light and good, the other all darkness and evil. Even if that is granted, it does not affect the conclusion that the mixture is contingent, and thus the mixed world cannot be pre-eternal. The argument is straightforward: contingency of the cause implies contingency of the effect.

Key Terms
قدم
حادث
امتزاج
تباين
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is only criticizing a specific dualist sect, but the argument applies to any claim of a pre-eternal composite world.
Correction: The argument is general: any world composed from pre-existing elements cannot be pre-eternal if the composition (mixture) is a contingent event.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for Maturidi's refutation of the dualists' claim that the world is pre-eternal?
A) Light and darkness cannot mix
B) The mixture is contingent because separation preceded it
C) The world is evil
D) The dualists contradict themselves

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be pre-eternal because its composition (mixture) is a contingent event, and contingency of the cause implies contingency of the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that the world's composition (mixture) points to its contingency. When you look at the world around you—its order, complexity, and constant change—do you truly see it as dependent on the Creator every moment, or do you unconsciously treat it as self-sufficient? What would it look like to live today with the awareness that everything is contingent on Allah?
?
The dualists attached 'eternity' to something other than Allah. Are there things in your life—possessions, relationships, status—that you treat as if they were eternal? How does this argument from contingency expose the folly of relying on anything besides the Eternal? Write down one attachment you need to re-evaluate.
?
If the very origin of the world requires a temporal beginning, then what does that demand of you regarding your own beginning and end? How does this reality shape your priorities? Will you live today as if this world is temporary and what comes after is eternal?

Refutation of Mani's Dualism
Maturidi refutes Mani's theory that light created the world to rescue its parts from darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of Mani's theory that light created the world to rescue its parts from darkness.

Core Idea
Mani's theory of light and darkness is self-contradictory and fails rationally.

Maturidi presents Mani's claim that light created the world to rescue its parts from darkness, then systematically dismantles it. He shows that this theory implies light is ignorant, powerless, and actually worsens its own situation. This lesson explores the logical arguments Maturidi uses to refute dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the rhetorical tone: 'How far they are from that!'
2
Identify the contradictions
List the contradictions Maturidi points out: (1) world cannot be both created and pre-eternal, (2) light was powerless despite helpers, (3) light was ignorant of the attack, (4) most of the world is evil yet light created it, (5) creation increased darkness's hold.
3
Reflect on the attributes of light
Consider how Maturidi uses the attributes of knowledge and power (which dualists ascribe to light) to show that light cannot be both perfectly good and lacking these.
This connects to Islamic theology about God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبخاصة قول الماني حيث زعم أن النور لما رأى الظلمة قَدَحَتْ فيه ومازجت به أحدث هذا العالم ليتخلّص بذلك أجزاء النور من أجزاء الظلمة، فصار العالم على هذا القول بعد الامتزاج المحدث؛ فيكون بعد المحدَث قديما، وذلك [هو ] التجاهل. فأوجبوا عجز النور وقت كونه في سلطانه بجميع أعوانه من الخيرات وأنصاره من الحسنات، حيث لم يقدر على الامتناع من قدح الظلمة وأحد أجزائه عنه، وجهلوه بوقت القدح فيه ليتخلص عنه ، ثم زعموا أنه أحدث هذا العالم ليخلّص أجزاءه منها بعد أن صار في وثاقها. هيهات ما أبْعَدَهم عن ذلك، وما أجهل مَن يقدمونه ويجعلون له كل خير؛ وأول كل خير علم ، وقد جهل ما ذُكِر ؛ وعِظَم كل خير بقوة، وقد عجز من حفظه في أقوى أحواله. ثم إذ كان هو المنشئ للعالم كيف صار أكثر العالم شراً ؟ فهو إذن فَعَل الشر ليتخلص به من وثاق الشر، فكأنه أعان الشر والظلمة، إذ هو عمل ذلك. ثم قد زاد من أجزائها في أجزاء النور بإحداث العالم، [فزاد أجزاؤها] في أجزاء العالم ، فازداد له حبسا وهلاكًا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And especially the saying of Mani, where he claimed that when light saw that darkness had struck it and mixed with it, it originated this world in order to thereby rescue the parts of light from the parts of darkness. So the world according to this saying came after the originated mixture; thus after the originated it would be pre-eternal, and that is ignorance. So they necessitated the incapacity of light at the time it was in its dominion with all its helpers from goods and its supporters from virtues, when it was unable to prevent the striking of darkness and one of its parts from it, and they made it ignorant of the time of the striking in order to be rescued from it. Then they claimed that it originated this world to rescue its parts from it after they had come into its bonds. How far they are from that! And how ignorant is the one they put forward and make for him every good; and the foremost of every good is knowledge, and he was ignorant of what was mentioned; and the greatness of every good is power, and he was incapable of preserving himself in his strongest state. Then, since he is the originator of the world, how did most of the world become evil? So he therefore did evil to rescue himself from the bonds of evil, as if he aided evil and darkness, since he did that. Then he increased from its parts in the parts of light by originating the world, so he increased its parts in the parts of the world, so he increased for himself imprisonment and destruction. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a specific refutation of Mani's version of dualism. Maturidi first presents Mani's theory: light saw that darkness struck and mixed with it, so light created the world to extract its trapped parts. Maturidi points out a logical contradiction: if the world is created after the mixture, it cannot be pre-eternal; calling it pre-eternal is ignorance. He then criticizes the implication that light was powerless to prevent the attack despite having all its helpers, and ignorant of the time of attack. This contradicts the perfections of knowledge and power that the dualists attribute to light. Furthermore, if light is the creator of the world, why is most of the world evil? This suggests that light either willed evil (which is inconsistent with it being all good) or was compelled. By creating the world, light actually increased the mixture and thus its own imprisonment, contradicting the goal of rescue. Maturidi concludes with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah,' emphasizing reliance on God's power alone.

نور
ظلمة
عجز
جهل
خلاص

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is attacking light worship or dualism as a whole, but his aim is to show the internal inconsistency of Mani's specific system.
Correction: Maturidi refutes Mani's version by exposing logical contradictions within the theory itself, not by a general rejection of light as a principle.

Checkpoint

A) Light is good but created an evil world
B) Light was powerless before darkness despite being divine
C) The world is pre-eternal but also created
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Mani's dualism fails because it attributes ignorance and powerlessness to light, contradicts its goodness, and fails to explain evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Mani's theory failed because it attributed ignorance and weakness to light. Have you ever, even unknowingly, attributed imperfection to Allah by complaining that a situation is 'too much' for Him to handle? How can you replace that thought with humble reliance on His power?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is all-knowing and all-powerful, how would your response to personal trials change starting today? Write one specific action you will take to actively remind yourself of His perfect attributes when you are tempted to despair.

The Dualists' Disagreements on Mixture
Maturidi reports dualist disagreements on mixture and dismisses them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi dismisses the dualists' conflicting explanations of the mixture of light and darkness as baseless nonsense.

Core Idea
The dualists cannot agree on which principle caused the mixture — their confusion shows the weakness of their theory.

The dualists claim that light and darkness are two eternal principles, but they cannot even agree on how these two mix. Some say darkness spreads and light penetrates it; others say the opposite. Maturidi points out their internal disagreements and dismisses all such talk as incoherent. This lesson examines their arguments and Maturidi's rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualists' conflicting views
Read the passage where Maturidi reports the dualists' different opinions on which principle caused the mixture. Notice how they contradict each other.
2
Understand Maturidi's dismissal
See how Maturidi rejects all these views as 'nonsense' (هذيان). Ask: why does he consider them baseless?
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider that if the dualists cannot agree on such a basic point, their entire system is questionable.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واختلفت الثنوية في الامتزاج . فمنهم من يجعله للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا ؛ فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل، ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دَرَاك فيقع فيها، فوقع الامتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور. لكنه كله هذيان؛ ما يُدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزء والتبعض، والحسن والقبح، والطيب / والخبث وكل شيء سواه . فان كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فهما يحدثان بحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منهما ألوهية لظهور العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم هو دليل قوى عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة. وبعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منهما قدر أن ينشئ فعلاً يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان. ومما يبيّن أنهما فعل الواحدٍ ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره خير حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر حتى لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا؛ ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed concerning the mixture. Some of them attribute it to darkness, but they differed: some affirm action for it, and some reject that and see it like the naturally spreading, and it is dense and covering, and light is thin and penetrating, so it falls into it, so the mixture occurred by that. And some of them attribute that to light. But all of it is nonsense; what informs them of that? The principle in it is that darkness and light are subject to change and transformation, and subject to division and partition, and good and evil, and the pleasant and the foul, and everything else. For if they go back to the parts of the world, then they come to be with its coming to be and perish with its perishing. Then it is not permissible that either of them has divinity because of the appearance of incapacity and ignorance in them, while the world is an evidence of a powerful, knowing, wise (being), so they are in that aggregate. After that, since neither of them was able to originate an act that indicates it, it is established that they are effects, not agents. And among what clarifies that they are the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world that is by its essence good such that no evil ever comes from it, nor evil such that no good ever comes from it; it is established that denying the like of this from the One is not possible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists' disagreements about which principle caused the mixture, and a proof that light and darkness are contingent and cannot be gods. Maturidi first notes the internal disagreements among dualists: some say darkness caused the mixture, others say light. He dismisses all such talk as nonsense. Then he lays down the principle: light and darkness are subject to change, transformation, division, good and evil, etc. If they are part of the world (i.e., they compose it), then they are contingent and perishable like the world. Therefore they cannot be divine, because divinity requires power and knowledge, but both show incapacity and ignorance. The world itself is evidence of a powerful, knowing, wise creator, so light and darkness are part of that world. They cannot create any act that would indicate they are agents; rather, they are effects. Finally, he argues that nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil; everything can produce both. This shows that the world cannot be the product of two opposite principles, but must be the act of one God, who creates both good and evil contingently.

Key Terms
امتزاج
هذيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the dualists who say mixture is caused by darkness disagree among themselves about what?
A) whether darkness is dense or thin
B) whether darkness has action or is passively spreading
C) whether light is older
D) whether mixture is eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that dualists cannot even agree on which principle caused the mixture, making their theory self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists had no reliable source for their beliefs, so they fell into confusion. When you face questions about Allah and His actions, do you rely on revelation or your own reasoning? Where do you tend to speculate without guidance?
?
If you were challenged to defend your belief in one attribute of Allah, would you be able to point to a clear proof from the Quran or Sunnah, or would you fumble like the dualists? What would you do to strengthen that weak spot?
?
Maturidi calls the dualists' views 'nonsense' because they have no knowledge. Is there any area of your faith where you hold a belief without a solid basis from revelation? Are you willing to abandon it if you cannot find evidence?

Light and Darkness Are Contingent, Not Divine
Maturidi argues for the contingency of light and darkness and refutes their divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that light and darkness are contingent creatures, not eternal gods, based on their attributes and inability to create.

Core Idea
Light and darkness show incapacity and ignorance — they cannot be gods, and the world points to one powerful Creator.

Maturidi now turns from the dualists' confusion to a rational argument. He establishes a principle: light and darkness are subject to change, division, and other qualities. Since they are part of the world, they are contingent. Moreover, they cannot create any act, so they are effects, not agents. Finally, he adds that nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil, refuting the idea of two opposite principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle about light and darkness
Identify Maturidi's starting point: light and darkness are subject to change, division, good and evil, etc.
2
Follow the contingency argument
If they are part of the world, they come to be with the world and perish. So they cannot be divine.
3
Consider the evidence of the world
The world itself is evidence of a powerful, knowing, wise being, so light and darkness are part of that aggregate.
4
Understand the final proof
Nothing is purely good or evil; everything can produce both. This shows the world is from one creator, not two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واختلفت الثنوية في الامتزاج . فمنهم من يجعله للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا ؛ فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل، ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دَرَاك فيقع فيها، فوقع الامتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور. لكنه كله هذيان؛ ما يُدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزء والتبعض، والحسن والقبح، والطيب / والخبث وكل شيء سواه . فان كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فهما يحدثان بحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منهما ألوهية لظهور العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم هو دليل قوى عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة. وبعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منهما قدر أن ينشئ فعلاً يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان. ومما يبيّن أنهما فعل الواحدٍ ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره خير حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر حتى لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا؛ ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed concerning the mixture. Some of them attribute it to darkness, but they differed: some affirm action for it, and some reject that and see it like the naturally spreading, and it is dense and covering, and light is thin and penetrating, so it falls into it, so the mixture occurred by that. And some of them attribute that to light. But all of it is nonsense; what informs them of that? The principle in it is that darkness and light are subject to change and transformation, and subject to division and partition, and good and evil, and the pleasant and the foul, and everything else. For if they go back to the parts of the world, then they come to be with its coming to be and perish with its perishing. Then it is not permissible that either of them has divinity because of the appearance of incapacity and ignorance in them, while the world is an evidence of a powerful, knowing, wise (being), so they are in that aggregate. After that, since neither of them was able to originate an act that indicates it, it is established that they are effects, not agents. And among what clarifies that they are the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world that is by its essence good such that no evil ever comes from it, nor evil such that no good ever comes from it; it is established that denying the like of this from the One is not possible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists' disagreements about which principle caused the mixture, and a proof that light and darkness are contingent and cannot be gods. Maturidi first notes the internal disagreements among dualists: some say darkness caused the mixture, others say light. He dismisses all such talk as nonsense. Then he lays down the principle: light and darkness are subject to change, transformation, division, good and evil, etc. If they are part of the world (i.e., they compose it), then they are contingent and perishable like the world. Therefore they cannot be divine, because divinity requires power and knowledge, but both show incapacity and ignorance. The world itself is evidence of a powerful, knowing, wise creator, so light and darkness are part of that world. They cannot create any act that would indicate they are agents; rather, they are effects. Finally, he argues that nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil; everything can produce both. This shows that the world cannot be the product of two opposite principles, but must be the act of one God, who creates both good and evil contingently.

Key Terms
تغير
تجزء
ألوهية
عجز
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument only applies to the specific dualist view he mentions.
Correction: His argument uses universal principles about contingency and the absence of pure essences, thus refuting any dualism of good and evil principles.

Checkpoint
What reason does Maturidi give for saying light and darkness cannot be divine?
A) They are material
B) They show incapacity and ignorance
C) They are not mentioned in scripture
D) They are older than the world

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that light and darkness are contingent, dependent on a single creator, and cannot be gods because they lack power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways do you treat certain created things (like light, darkness, success, or hardship) as if they have independent power, forgetting that they are merely contingent effects? How will you remind yourself daily that only Allah is the true agent?
?
Maturidi says nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil. Think of something you consider 'pure good' in your life (e.g., health, wealth, a relationship). Can you see how it might also bring about evil? How does this realization affect your attachment to it?
?
If light and darkness display incapacity and ignorance, how much more do the lesser things you rely on for guidance or strength? Are there any 'gods' (objects, people, or systems) in your heart that you look to for power or knowledge that Allah alone possesses? Will you identify and uproot them today?
