The Good and Evil Argument for Contingency
Maturidi enumerates three possibilities for the mixture's moral quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the moral categories of good and evil to refute the dualist view and argue for the contingency of light and darkness.

Core Idea
If mixture is good, it cannot come from evil darkness; if evil, light would produce evil — both contradict dualism.

Maturidi now turns to a logical puzzle: the mixture of light and darkness must be either good or evil. By examining each possibility, he shows that none can be consistently attributed to both principles, thereby refuting the dualist claim that light and darkness are eternal and independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note how Maturidi begins with a principle about the mixture's moral quality.
The Arabic uses conditional structures: 'if it is good...' and 'if it is evil...'.
2
Understand the first conditional (good mixture)
If the mixture is good, it cannot originate from darkness (evil). Yet darkness is said to be a principle. This contradicts the dualist claim that good and evil are separate.
3
Understand the second conditional (evil mixture)
If the mixture is evil, then light (good) must have participated in it, making light capable of evil. This also contradicts dualism.
4
Consider the third possibility (mixture from light alone)
If the mixture comes from light alone, then light produces both good and evil, again inconsistent with its pure nature.
Maturidi concludes that all possibilities refute dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الامتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرًا أو خيرا. فان كان خيرا لا يخلو من يكون من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير وبطل قولهم بالاثنين من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر؛ وإن كان شراً فقد شاركه الخير في القبول فصار شراً؛ وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه . مع ما إذ كانا غير ممتزجين فامتزجا، لا يخلو امتزاجها من أن يكون بأنفسهما فيكونان ممتزجين بالجوهر متباينين به، وذلك متناقض . ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكونا متحركين بأنفسهما ساكنين، حتين ميتين، قاعدين قائمين مع ما يفسد أن يكون التباين لنفسه يقع، ثم امتزاج بما كان به التباين ألا ترى أن الأحوال التي تتغير بالأعيان لم يجز وجودها إلا بغير، فكذلك التباين والامتزاج؛ فثبت أنهما بغيرهما امتزجا وبغيرهما كانا متباينين، وذلك يوجب حدثهما .
Then the principle is that the mixture is not free from being either evil or good. If it is good, it is not free from being from darkness, so good would come from it, and their statement of the two (principles) is invalidated since evil does not produce good nor good produce evil. And if it is evil, then good shared in its acceptance, so it became evil. And if that is from light, then both aspects apply to it. Moreover, when they were unmixed and then mixed, their mixture is not free from being by themselves, so they would be mixed in substance and separate in substance, and that is contradictory. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible for them to be moving by themselves and stationary, living and dead, sitting and standing, while it is invalid that separation occurs by itself, then mixture by what caused the separation. Do you not see that states that change in entities are not possible except by another? So likewise separation and mixture. So it is established that they mixed by another and were separate by another, and that necessitates their contingency.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage contains two further arguments for the contingency of light and darkness. First, Maturidi examines the moral quality of the mixture. If the mixture is good, it cannot be from darkness, because darkness is evil and good cannot come from evil. If it is evil, then light (which is good) participated in it, making light evil. If the mixture is from light, then light produces both good and evil, which contradicts its pure goodness. So in all cases, the dualist position fails. Second, he argues about the nature of mixture and separation. If light and darkness were unmixed and then mixed, the mixture must have been caused by themselves or by another. If by themselves, they would be both mixed and separate at the same time, which is contradictory. If it were possible for them to mix and separate by themselves, then other contradictory states would be possible, like being moving and stationary simultaneously. But in reality, states like change occur only through an external agent. Therefore, mixture and separation must be caused by another, implying that light and darkness are not eternal but contingent beings that are acted upon.

Key Terms
خير
شر
امتزاج
اثنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument about good and evil is a moral claim about the world's ethical nature.
Correction: It is a logical argument about causal relationships: good cannot come from evil, and evil cannot come from good if essences are pure. It is not a statement about moral values per se.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if the mixture is good?
A) It must come from darkness, which is impossible.
B) It must come from light, which is consistent.
C) It can come from both equally.
D) It proves the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The mixture's moral quality forces a contradiction for dualism: good cannot come from evil, and evil cannot involve pure good, so the principles must be contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience something good in your life, do you truly recognize it as coming from the One God alone, or do you unconsciously attribute it to some other source? Pause and examine a recent blessing: where did your heart place its origin?
?
Consider a time when you faced a moral struggle. Did you ever feel as if there were two equal powers pulling you, one for good and one for evil? In light of this argument, how does the unity of God reshape your understanding of that inner battle?
?
This argument shows that if two independent principles existed, they would inevitably lead to contradictions. In your own life, are there areas where you still hold dualistic assumptions—acting as if good and evil come from separate, equally powerful sources? What change is required of you after seeing this?

Mixture and Separation: Proving Contingency
Maturidi presents a logical proof from the nature of mixture and separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that mixture and separation cannot be caused by the essences themselves, proving light and darkness are contingent.

Core Idea
If light and darkness caused their own mixture, they would be contradictory; therefore an external cause exists.

Maturidi now turns to the logical problem of how two unmixed entities come to be mixed. He argues that if they caused their own mixture, they would simultaneously be both mixed and separate, which is absurd. Since such contradictions do not occur in reality, mixture and separation must be caused by another, implying the entities themselves are not eternal but contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with focus on the contradiction
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the phrase 'mixed in substance and separate in substance' which is the core contradiction.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
If mixture were by themselves, they would have to be both mixed and separate simultaneously, which is impossible.
Maturidi uses examples like moving and stationary to show absurdity.
3
Recognize the analogy with other changing states
Just as changes like motion require an external cause, so do mixture and separation.
4
Conclude contingency
Since they are acted upon by another, they cannot be eternal; they are contingent and created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الامتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرًا أو خيرا. فان كان خيرا لا يخلو من يكون من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير وبطل قولهم بالاثنين من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر؛ وإن كان شراً فقد شاركه الخير في القبول فصار شراً؛ وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه . مع ما إذ كانا غير ممتزجين فامتزجا، لا يخلو امتزاجها من أن يكون بأنفسهما فيكونان ممتزجين بالجوهر متباينين به، وذلك متناقض . ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكونا متحركين بأنفسهما ساكنين، حتين ميتين، قاعدين قائمين مع ما يفسد أن يكون التباين لنفسه يقع، ثم امتزاج بما كان به التباين ألا ترى أن الأحوال التي تتغير بالأعيان لم يجز وجودها إلا بغير، فكذلك التباين والامتزاج؛ فثبت أنهما بغيرهما امتزجا وبغيرهما كانا متباينين، وذلك يوجب حدثهما .
Then the principle is that the mixture is not free from being either evil or good. If it is good, it is not free from being from darkness, so good would come from it, and their statement of the two (principles) is invalidated since evil does not produce good nor good produce evil. And if it is evil, then good shared in its acceptance, so it became evil. And if that is from light, then both aspects apply to it. Moreover, when they were unmixed and then mixed, their mixture is not free from being by themselves, so they would be mixed in substance and separate in substance, and that is contradictory. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible for them to be moving by themselves and stationary, living and dead, sitting and standing, while it is invalid that separation occurs by itself, then mixture by what caused the separation. Do you not see that states that change in entities are not possible except by another? So likewise separation and mixture. So it is established that they mixed by another and were separate by another, and that necessitates their contingency.

Commentary
This passage contains two further arguments for the contingency of light and darkness. First, Maturidi examines the moral quality of the mixture. If the mixture is good, it cannot be from darkness, because darkness is evil and good cannot come from evil. If it is evil, then light (which is good) participated in it, making light evil. If the mixture is from light, then light produces both good and evil, which contradicts its pure goodness. So in all cases, the dualist position fails. Second, he argues about the nature of mixture and separation. If light and darkness were unmixed and then mixed, the mixture must have been caused by themselves or by another. If by themselves, they would be both mixed and separate at the same time, which is contradictory. If it were possible for them to mix and separate by themselves, then other contradictory states would be possible, like being moving and stationary simultaneously. But in reality, states like change occur only through an external agent. Therefore, mixture and separation must be caused by another, implying that light and darkness are not eternal but contingent beings that are acted upon.

B) That mixture and separation require an external cause

Refutation of Dualists' Disagreement on Mixing
Maturidi refutes dualists' views on mixing and proves light and darkness are created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument against dualist claims about light and darkness, and why they must be created rather than divine.

Core Idea
Light and darkness are created, not creators.

Maturidi confronts one of the most persistent challenges to monotheism: dualism. In this passage, he reports the dualists' own disagreements about how light and darkness mix, then dismantles their position by showing that both are subject to change and part of the world. This lesson will walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first, Maturidi reports the dualist views, then he refutes them.
2
Identify the dualist claims
Pay attention to the different opinions among dualists: some attribute mixing to darkness, others to light, with further subdivisions. Maturidi dismisses all as 'raving'.
3
Understand the principle
Maturidi establishes that both light and darkness are subject to change, division, and moral qualities. This is key: anything subject to change cannot be eternal or divine.
4
See the conclusion
Since light and darkness are part of the world and show incapacity, they must be created. The world itself points to a single wise creator, so they are acted upon, not actors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واختلفت الثنوية في الامتزاج . فمنهم من يجعله للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا ؛ فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل، ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دَرَاك فيقع فيها، فوقع الامتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور. لكنه كله هذيان؛ ما يُدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزء والتبعض، والحسن والقُبح، والطيب والخُبث وكلِّ شيء سواه . فان كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فهما يحدثان بحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منهما ألوهية لظهور العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم هو دليل قوى عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة. وبعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منهما قَدَرَ أن ينشئ فعلاً يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed concerning the mixing. Some of them attribute it to darkness, but they differed; some of them affirm action for it, and some of them refuse that and consider it like that which spreads by nature, and it is dense and a veil, while light is fine and penetrating, so it falls into it, so mixing occurred by that. And some of them attribute that to light. But all of it is raving; what makes them know that? The principle concerning it is that darkness and light are subject to possibility of change and transformation, possibility of division and partition, and good and evil, and pleasant and foul, and everything else. So if they both refer to the parts of the world, then they both arise with its arising and perish with its perishing. Then it is not permissible for either of them to have divinity due to the appearance of incapacity and ignorance in them, and the world is a proof of a powerful, knowing, wise [being], so they are included in that total. Moreover, since neither of them was able to originate an act that indicates it, it is established that they are both acted upon, not actors.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualists' theories about the mixing (امتزاج) of light and darkness. Imam Maturidi first reports their differing views: some attribute mixing to darkness, some to light, with further subdivisions. He then dismisses these as nonsense (هذيان) and establishes a principle: both light and darkness are subject to change, division, and moral qualities, so they must be part of the created world, arising and perishing with it. Therefore, neither can be divine, as they show incapacity and ignorance. The world itself demonstrates a powerful, knowing, and wise creator, so light and darkness are merely created things (مفعولان), not creators (فاعلان).

ظلمة

جهل
مفعول
فاعل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is a rational alternative to monotheism, with light and darkness as coeternal principles.
Correction: Maturidi shows that light and darkness are subject to change and part of the world, proving they are created and cannot be divine.

Checkpoint

B) Because they are subject to change and division

Key Takeaway

Maturidi dismisses the dualists’ views as ‘raving.’ Have you ever dismissed a truth claim about Allah because it was uncomfortable? What belief about God are you holding that, if you were honest, might be just as baseless?

Refutation of Dualists on Sacrifices
Maturidi refutes dualist justification of sacrifices via logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualists' justification of sacrifices by exposing internal contradictions.

Core Idea
Dualists' own principles lead to evil coming from light, contradicting their core doctrine.

In this passage, Maturidi takes on the dualist claim that sacrifices are permissible because they separate the luminous spirit from the dark body. He shows that their own admission of confessing murder as a sin forces them to accept that evil can come from light, thereby undermining their foundational belief that light and good are one. This lesson analyzes that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand dualist justification for sacrifices
Read the opening lines where dualists claim sacrifices are lawful because they separate dark body and luminous spirit. Note their description of light as fine and penetrating.
2
Identify the contradiction in their admission of sin
Maturidi points out that they affirm confession of murder as a sin. If this confession comes from a source other than light, it is a lie (evil); if from light, then light produces evil (contradicting their principle).
This is a key logical move.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that inability to perceive wisdom in creating evil does not force one to accept dualism. The dualist position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق مَنْ يُحل هم؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضيئ، وبين النور الجلي والظُّلمة الستارة، وبذلك وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالرّوح ذلك، لا بالظلمة، فيجب به حل الذبح . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير، والخير من جوهر الشر؛ هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين. ثم قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل وبما هو عندهم معصية، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالإقرار بالمعصية . ثبت أن العجز عن إدراك الحكمة في خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين؛ لما فيه تحقيقه أيضاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الامتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم؛ لأن قولهم: «إن جوهر النور لا يجيئ منه شر قط» ، والجهل شر. فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم بجوهره حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما. وإن كان من جوهر الشر فانه لا ينجع فيه ؛ لأنه بجوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير . وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم الحكمة والعلم . لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت مع جوهر النور كان هو عالما قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها. ولو كانت مع جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا مستمع له فهو عبث. فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر كل منهما ليصح ذلك المعنى، وفي ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ؛ وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين، فبطل بحمد الله . والأصل فيه أن التكلّم منهم بالحكمة لا يعدو إما أن تكلموا بجوهرهم وهو يعلم، فيخرج مخرج العبث ؛ أو يجهله ولا يقبله؛ وأيهما كان ففيه ثبات الأمرين من واحد؛ أو تكلموا من غير جوهرهم ، فإنه لا يخلو أيضاً من قبول أو عبث، وأيهما كان ففي ذلك ما قلنا . ولا قوة إلا بالله
Moreover, they claim that sacrifices are prohibited, and the most entitled to permit them are they themselves, because by them is the separation between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the veiling darkness, and thereby they described light as fine and penetrating, and the spirit likewise, not by darkness, so the permissibility of slaughter is necessitated. And there is no power except with God. The origin of this is that they deny evil from the substance of good, and good from the substance of evil; this is what led them to say dualism. Then they have affirmed confession of murder and what is according to them disobedience. So if it is from other than that from which murder comes, then he has lied, and that is evil; and if it is from it, then he has spoken truth in confessing disobedience. It is established that inability to perceive the wisdom in creating evil does not necessitate dualism, because of what is also its realization. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation of refraining from speaking of wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their statement: 'The substance of light never produces evil at all,' while ignorance is evil. So if it is from that substance, then he is knowing by his substance, wise by it, not susceptible to ignorance or foolishness, and learning and seeking wisdom are the right of those ignorant of them. And if it is from the substance of evil, then it does not benefit him, because by his substance he does not accept or admit good. And if that is so, their disputation and their claim of wisdom and knowledge are invalidated. Because their disputation in that, if it were with the substance of light, he would be knowing before the disputation, so it would have no meaning. And if it were with the substance of darkness, he would be not receptive nor listening, so it is futility. So it is necessary to realize ignorance and knowledge in the substance of each of them for that meaning to be sound, and in that is the combination of the two attributes in one of them; and that meaning compelled them to dualism, so it is invalidated, praise be to God. The principle in this is that their speaking of wisdom does not exceed either that they speak by their substance while it knows, so it comes out as futility; or it is ignorant and does not accept it; and whichever it is, in it is the establishment of the two matters from one; or they speak from other than their substance, then it also does not lack acceptance or futility, and whichever it is, in it is what we said. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes two aspects of dualist doctrine: their view on sacrifices and their claim that light cannot produce evil. First, Maturidi notes that dualists permit sacrifices because they separate dark body from luminous spirit, but then argues that their own principle that evil comes only from darkness leads to contradictions when they admit to confessing murder (a sin). Second, he turns to their claim that the substance of light never produces evil. But ignorance is evil, so if they speak from light, they are already wise and need not seek knowledge; if from darkness, they cannot receive good. Therefore, their disputation is either meaningless or futile. This shows that their dualism collapses, as it requires both good and evil to be present in one substance. Maturidi concludes that their entire position is invalid.

Key Terms
ذبائح
جسد مظلم
روح مضيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe their view on sacrifices is consistent with their principles.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their admission of sin as evil forces them to accept evil from light or good from darkness, contradicting their dualism.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Maturidi identify in the dualist position regarding the confession of murder?
A) Confession of murder is not a sin according to dualists.
B) If confession comes from light, it is a lie (evil from light); if from darkness, it is truth (good from darkness).
C) Dualists do not allow confession of murder.
D) Confession comes from a third substance.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualists by showing that their own admission of sin forces them to accept that evil can come from light or good from darkness, undermining dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself committing a sin, do you ever mentally blame your environment, upbringing, or circumstances as if they are separate from you? How does Maturidi's argument force you to confront that good and evil can come from the same source—your own soul—and that you alone are accountable? What one sin will you stop excusing today and confess fully to Allah?
?
Maturidi shows that not understanding God's wisdom does not justify false belief. Is there a command of Allah that you have resisted or doubted because you don't see the wisdom in it? What would it cost you to submit to it anyway, trusting that His wisdom is greater than your understanding? Name one specific act of obedience you will perform this week in silent surrender to that wisdom.
?
The dualists' error was denying that evil can originate from good, leading them to attribute evil to a separate source. In what ways do you subtly separate your good deeds from your bad deeds, as if they belong to different 'selves'—your 'spiritual self' and your 'worldly self'? How can you integrate both under the single sovereignty of Allah, owning both your obedience and your disobedience as coming from the same created soul? What concrete step will you take to stop compartmentalising your faith?

Refutation of Dualists on Knowledge and Disputation
Maturidi critiques dualist epistemology showing dilemma on knowledge and disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the dualists' claim that light cannot produce evil makes their pursuit of knowledge and disputation either meaningless or futile.

Core Idea
If light cannot produce evil, then ignorance (evil) cannot come from it, so dualists are either already wise or incapable of learning.

Maturidi now turns to the dualists' assertion that the substance of light never produces evil. By noting that ignorance is evil, he forces a dilemma: if dualists speak from light, they are already wise; if from darkness, they cannot receive good. Hence their disputation is either pointless or hopeless. This lesson unpacks that devastating critique.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the dualist claim that light produces no evil
Read the section where dualists state 'the substance of light never produces evil at all.' Note that Maturidi classifies ignorance as evil.
2
Identify the dilemma
If dualists speak from the substance of light, they are already knowledgeable; disputation is meaningless. If from darkness, they cannot accept good (knowledge); disputation is futile.
This is a classic logical dilemma.
3
Conclude the invalidation
Maturidi concludes that their claim to wisdom and knowledge is invalidated. Their whole position collapses.

?

What would it mean to truly submit to the fact that knowledge comes from Allah and requires humility?
?

This lesson warns against a worldview that destroys the possibility of growth. Examine your own heart: do you live as if some part of you is 'beyond' needing guidance or 'unable' to receive it?

Dualist View on Sacrifice and Light
Maturidi presents the dualist argument on sacrifice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist claim about sacrifice and how they describe light and darkness.

Core Idea
Dualists use sacrifice to separate light from darkness, but their reasoning is flawed.

Maturidi begins by presenting the dualist argument that sacrifices are forbidden and only they can permit them, because sacrifice distinguishes the dark body from the luminous spirit. They describe light as subtle and penetrating, while darkness is covering. This sets the stage for his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Dualist Claim
Read the Arabic passage that states the dualists' view on sacrifices. Notice how they link sacrifice to the distinction between light and darkness.
2
Identify Their Description of Light
Pay attention to how they describe light as 'subtle and penetrating' and connect this to the spirit. Why do they think this justifies their permissibility of slaughter?
They believe light is active and darkness is passive.
3
Ask: Why is this relevant?
Consider why Maturidi starts with this point. He is laying out their position before refuting it. Understanding their claim is essential for seeing the flaws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق مَنْ يُحل هم؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضيئ، وبين النور الجلي والظُّلمة الستارة، وبذلك وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالرّوح ذلك، لا بالظلمة، فيجب به حل الذبح . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير، والخير من جوهر الشر؛ هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين. ثم قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل وبما هو عندهم معصية، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالإقرار بالمعصية . ثبت أن العجز عن إدراك الحكمة في خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين؛ لما فيه تحقيقه أيضاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الامتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم؛ لأن قولهم: «إن جوهر النور لا يجيئ منه شر قط» ، والجهل شر. فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم بجوهره حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما. وإن كان من جوهر الشر فانه لا ينجع فيه ؛ لأنه بجوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير . وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم الحكمة والعلم . لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت مع جوهر النور كان هو عالما قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها. ولو كانت مع جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا مستمع له فهو عبث. فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر كل منهما ليصح ذلك المعنى، وفي ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ؛ وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين، فبطل بحمد الله .
And after that, they (the dualists) say that sacrifices are forbidden, and the most deserving to permit them are they themselves, because through them (sacrifices) there is distinction between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the covering darkness. And by that they described light as subtle and penetrating, and the spirit likewise, not darkness, so the permissibility of slaughter is necessitated. And there is no power except with Allah. Its root is that they deny evil from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil; this is what led them to say (there are) two. Then they have affirmed the confession of killing and that which is according to them a sin. So if it (the confession) was from other than that from which the killing (came), then he (the confessor) has lied, and it (the lie) is evil. And if it was from it (the same essence as the killing), then he has spoken truthfully by confessing the sin. It is established that inability to perceive wisdom in creating evil does not compel one to say (there are) two; because of that which is also its reality. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation to refrain from speaking of wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their saying: 'The essence of light never produces any evil' – and ignorance is evil. So if it (the request for wisdom) is from that essence, then it (the essence) is knowing by its essence, wise by it, it does not admit ignorance or foolishness, and learning and seeking wisdom are the right of the ignorant regarding them. And if it is from the essence of evil, then it does not avail in it, because by its essence it does not accept nor admit good. And if it is so, their disputation and their claim of wisdom and knowledge become void. Because their disputation in that, if it were with the essence of light, then it (light) is knowing before the disputation, so there is no meaning to it. And if it were with the essence of darkness, it (darkness) is not receptive nor listening, so it is futile. So there must be a realization of ignorance and knowledge in the essence of each of them for that meaning to be valid, and in that there is combining of the two attributes in one of them; and that meaning necessitated their saying (there are) two, so it is invalidated, praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) view that there are two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi begins by stating the dualists' claim that sacrifices are forbidden (or permissibility determined by them) because sacrifice distinguishes between the dark body and the luminous spirit. They hold that light is subtle and penetrating, and so only sacrifice is permissible. The root of their error is that they deny that evil can come from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil, which leads them to claim two principles. Then Maturidi points out an inconsistency: the dualists affirm that a murderer can confess his crime (which is a sin). If that confession comes from a different essence than the one that produced the murder, then the confessor has lied (since the murder is evil, but the confession comes from a different good essence, so it is a lie), and lying is evil. If the confession comes from the same essence as the murder, then it is a truthful confession of a sin, but that would mean the essence produces both good (truthfulness) and evil (murder), contradicting their claim that light only produces good. Thus, inability to understand the wisdom in creating evil does not force one to accept dualism; indeed, the dualists' own reasoning refutes them. Then Maturidi argues that the dualists are the last people who should speak of wisdom or seek knowledge, because they claim light never produces evil, yet ignorance is evil. If they seek wisdom from the essence of light, it already knows, so the seeking is futile. If they seek from the essence of darkness, it does not accept good, so the seeking is futile. Their entire disputation is either with light (which already knows) or with darkness (which cannot accept), so it is meaningless. Therefore, the dualists' position forces them to combine knowledge and ignorance in the same essence, which is the very thing they deny, so their position collapses.

Key Terms
جوهر (essence)
النور (light)
الظلمة (darkness)
الذبائح (sacrifices)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dualists have a valid argument for sacrifice based on the distinction between light and darkness.
Correction: Maturidi will later show that this argument leads to contradictions; here he simply states it.

Checkpoint
According to the dualists, what does sacrifice distinguish?
A) Good and evil
B) Light and darkness, and body and spirit
C) Wisdom and ignorance
D) Knowledge and action

Key Takeaway
The dualists justify sacrifice as a means to separate light from darkness, but this is only the starting point of Maturidi's refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists claimed they alone were entitled to permit sacrifices based on their understanding of reality. In what area of your life do you assert a special privilege based on your own reasoning rather than submitting to Allah's command? Will you repent and surrender that area to Him today?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' How does your reliance on your own intellect prevent you from experiencing true dependence on Allah? What step will you take this week to consciously depend on Him rather than your own understanding?

The Root Error and Internal Contradiction
Maturidi identifies the root denial and exposes contradiction via confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' root error and the internal contradiction in their position regarding confession of sin.

Core Idea
Dualists deny that good and evil can come from each other's essences, but their own view on confession exposes a flaw.

Maturidi now identifies the root cause of dualism: they deny that evil can come from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil. He then presents a powerful argument using their own acceptance of confession of murder to show that their system collapses into contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Root Error
Read the passage that states the root cause: they deny that evil comes from good's essence and good from evil's essence. This leads them to believe in two separate principles.
This is the core of their mistake.
2
Understand the Confession Argument
Maturidi points out that dualists accept that a murderer can confess his crime. Follow his reasoning: if the confession comes from a different essence than the murder, it is a lie (evil); if from the same essence, it is truthful confession of sin, mixing good and evil.
Pay attention to the logic: they cannot avoid contradiction.
3
Draw the Conclusion
Maturidi concludes that inability to understand wisdom in creating evil does not force dualism. The dualists' own reasoning refutes them.

Source Text

وبعد، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق مَنْ يُحل هم؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضيئ، وبين النور الجلي والظُّلمة الستارة، وبذلك وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالرّوح ذلك، لا بالظلمة، فيجب به حل الذبح . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير، والخير من جوهر الشر؛ هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين. ثم قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل وبما هو عندهم معصية، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالإقرار بالمعصية . ثبت أن العجز عن إدراك الحكمة في خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين؛ لما فيه تحقيقه أيضاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الامتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم؛ لأن قولهم: «إن جوهر النور لا يجيئ منه شر قط» ، والجهل شر. فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم بجوهره حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما. وإن كان من جوهر الشر فانه لا ينجع فيه ؛ لأنه بجوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير . وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم الحكمة والعلم . لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت مع جوهر النور كان هو عالما قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها. ولو كانت مع جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا مستمع له فهو عبث. فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر كل منهما ليصح ذلك المعنى، وفي ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ؛ وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين، فبطل بحمد الله .
And after that, they (the dualists) say that sacrifices are forbidden, and the most deserving to permit them are they themselves, because through them (sacrifices) there is distinction between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the covering darkness. And by that they described light as subtle and penetrating, and the spirit likewise, not darkness, so the permissibility of slaughter is necessitated. And there is no power except with Allah. Its root is that they deny evil from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil; this is what led them to say (there are) two. Then they have affirmed the confession of killing and that which is according to them a sin. So if it (the confession) was from other than that from which the killing (came), then he (the confessor) has lied, and it (the lie) is evil. And if it was from it (the same essence as the killing), then he has spoken truthfully by confessing the sin. It is established that inability to perceive wisdom in creating evil does not compel one to say (there are) two; because of that which is also its reality. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation to refrain from speaking of wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their saying: 'The essence of light never produces any evil' – and ignorance is evil. So if it (the request for wisdom) is from that essence, then it (the essence) is knowing by its essence, wise by it, it does not admit ignorance or foolishness, and learning and seeking wisdom are the right of the ignorant regarding them. And if it is from the essence of evil, then it does not avail in it, because by its essence it does not accept nor admit good. And if it is so, their disputation and their claim of wisdom and knowledge become void. Because their disputation in that, if it were with the essence of light, then it (light) is knowing before the disputation, so there is no meaning to it. And if it were with the essence of darkness, it (darkness) is not receptive nor listening, so it is futile. So there must be a realization of ignorance and knowledge in the essence of each of them for that meaning to be valid, and in that there is combining of the two attributes in one of them; and that meaning necessitated their saying (there are) two, so it is invalidated, praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) view that there are two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi begins by stating the dualists' claim that sacrifices are forbidden (or permissibility determined by them) because sacrifice distinguishes between the dark body and the luminous spirit. They hold that light is subtle and penetrating, and so only sacrifice is permissible. The root of their error is that they deny that evil can come from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil, which leads them to claim two principles. Then Maturidi points out an inconsistency: the dualists affirm that a murderer can confess his crime (which is a sin). If that confession comes from a different essence than the one that produced the murder, then the confessor has lied (since the murder is evil, but the confession comes from a different good essence, so it is a lie), and lying is evil. If the confession comes from the same essence as the murder, then it is a truthful confession of a sin, but that would mean the essence produces both good (truthfulness) and evil (murder), contradicting their claim that light only produces good. Thus, inability to understand the wisdom in creating evil does not force one to accept dualism; indeed, the dualists' own reasoning refutes them. Then Maturidi argues that the dualists are the last people who should speak of wisdom or seek knowledge, because they claim light never produces evil, yet ignorance is evil. If they seek wisdom from the essence of light, it already knows, so the seeking is futile. If they seek from the essence of darkness, it does not accept good, so the seeking is futile. Their entire disputation is either with light (which already knows) or with darkness (which cannot accept), so it is meaningless. Therefore, the dualists' position forces them to combine knowledge and ignorance in the same essence, which is the very thing they deny, so their position collapses.

Key Terms
جوهر (essence)
الخير (good)
الشر (evil)
القتل (killing)
الإقرار (confession)
معصية (sin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dualism is a reasonable response to the problem of evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their own position leads to contradictions, so it is not reasonable.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the root cause of dualism?
A) Their view on sacrifices.
B) Their denial that evil comes from good's essence and good from evil's essence.
C) Their description of light as subtle.
D) Their belief in two creators.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' denial of interchange between good and evil leads to a contradiction when applied to their own acceptance of confession of murder.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or witness evil, do you ever find yourself doubting that Allah alone is in control? How does Maturidi's exposure of the dualist's contradiction confront the hidden pride in your heart that wants to question Allah's wisdom?
?
The dualists refused to accept that both good and evil could come from the same source. Think about your own life: do you attribute your successes to Allah but blame your failures on circumstances or others? How does the confession argument force you to admit that every act—good or bad—ultimately traces back to a single Creator, and what does that demand from you in repentance?
?
If you cannot fully understand why Allah allows evil, does that drive you to trust Him more or to resent Him? This lesson shows that rejecting His oneness leads to even greater absurdities. What specific step will you take today to surrender your confusion to Allah and affirm that He alone is worthy of worship, even when His wisdom is hidden?

The Futility of Dualist Claims to Wisdom
Maturidi refutes dualist claims to wisdom and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that dualists are the last who should claim wisdom or seek knowledge.

Core Idea
Dualists cannot consistently seek wisdom because their own principles make such seeking either redundant or impossible.

Maturidi continues his refutation by attacking the dualists' claim to wisdom and knowledge. He points out that according to their own assertion that light never produces evil, and since ignorance is evil, their pursuit of wisdom is either futile or contradictory. He then demonstrates that their entire disputation is meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the Dualist Principle
Read the passage that states the dualist claim: 'The essence of light never produces any evil.' Note that Maturidi adds: 'and ignorance is evil.'
2
Analyze the Two Scenarios
Maturidi presents two possibilities for the dualists' pursuit of wisdom. First, if they seek wisdom from the essence of light, it is already knowing, so seeking is futile. Second, if they seek from the essence of darkness, it cannot accept good, so seeking is also futile.
This shows their inconsistency.
3
Understand the Conclusion
Since both possibilities lead to futility, their disputation is invalid. Moreover, their position forces them to combine knowledge and ignorance in a single essence, which contradicts their original denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) view that there are two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Maturidi's argument forces you to examine the consistency of your own worldview. Is there any belief you hold that, if followed to its logical conclusion, would make your search for truth meaningless? What step will you take this week to align your beliefs with the reality of a single, all-wise Creator?

Maturidi's Argument Against Dualists on Wisdom and Attributes
Maturidi's analysis of dualists' speech regarding wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes an internal contradiction in the dualists' claim by analyzing their speech about wisdom.

Core Idea
Dualists cannot speak about wisdom without combining two attributes in one essence.

Maturidi presents a concise logical argument that targets the very act of the dualists' disputation. By examining how they could possibly speak about wisdom, he shows that their position forces them to attribute both knowledge and ignorance to the same essence, thereby undermining the foundation of their dualism. This passage demonstrates the power of dialectical reasoning in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure (إما... أو).
The passage is a single logical argument, not a list.
2
Identify the two main scenarios
The dualists either speak by their essence or by something else. For each scenario, Maturidi derives contradictory outcomes.
Focus on how each scenario leads to futility or acceptance, which implies combination.
3
Trace the conclusion
In both scenarios, the dualists must admit that two opposite attributes (like knowledge and ignorance) come from one entity. This refutes their claim of two separate principles.
The conclusion is that dualism is self-defeating.
4
Reflect on the method
Maturidi uses the dualists' own framework to show inconsistency. This is a common tactic in kalam arguments.
Consider how this argument differs from simply asserting tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل فيه أن التكلّم منهم بالحكمة لا يعدو إما أن تكلموا بجوهرهم وهو يعلم، فيخرج مخرج العبث ؛ أو يجهله ولا يقبله؛ وأيهما كان ففيه ثبات الأمرين من واحد؛ أو تكلموا من غير جوهرهم ، فإنه لا يخلو أيضاً من قبول أو عبث، وأيهما كان ففي ذلك ما قلنا . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The principle in that is that the dualists' speaking about wisdom does not exceed either that they speak by their essence and it knows, so it comes out as futility; or it is ignorant of it and does not accept it; and whichever of the two (cases) it is, in it there is establishment of the two things from one; or they speak from other than their essence, then it also does not lack either acceptance or futility, and whichever it is, in that is what we have said. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a succinct argument showing that the dualists cannot consistently claim to speak about wisdom. Maturidi reduces their speech to two possibilities: either they speak by their own essence (i.e., the essence of light or darkness) or by something else. If they speak by their essence and it knows, then the speech is futile because the essence already knows the wisdom. If they speak by their essence and it is ignorant of the wisdom and does not accept it, then again the speech is futile. In both cases, this requires that the same essence possess both knowledge (or action of speaking) and ignorance (or rejection), thus combining two opposite attributes in one essence, which defeats their dualism. Alternatively, if they speak by something other than their essence, then that other thing must either accept the wisdom (so it becomes good) or reject it (futility), again requiring combination of attributes in one entity. Therefore, their position inevitably forces them to admit a single source that has contradictory attributes, refuting their claim of two separate principles.

Key Terms
التكلّم (speaking)
الحكمة (wisdom)
العبث (futility)
القبول (acceptance)
الجوهر (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is just a logical trick and doesn't disprove dualism.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is substantive: it shows that any attempt to speak about wisdom requires a single subject with knowledge, contradicting the dualist's claim of two independent principles.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if the dualists speak about wisdom by their own essence and it knows, what is the result?
A) The speech is valid and meaningful.
B) The speech is futile because the essence already knows.
C) The essence does not accept wisdom.
D) It proves the existence of two principles.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that dualists cannot consistently claim to speak about wisdom without positing a single essence that both knows and speaks, thereby refuting their core doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about wisdom, do you examine the consistency between your words and your inner state? Maturidi shows that dualists cannot speak without contradiction; how often do you speak about faith while harboring doubts or contradictions in your heart? What will you do to align your speech with your essence?
?
Consider a time you argued against a belief you didn't fully understand. Did you, like the dualists, assume your speech was neutral? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the source of your own arguments—are they from sincere conviction or merely from external influence?
?
Maturidi exposes that dualism leads to futility in speech. What areas of your life do you hold dualistic tendencies—separating faith from action, or truth from practice? How can you repent from such hypocrisy and seek unity of purpose?

Refutation of Dualist Claim
Maturidi argues against the dualist premise by showing it is self-refutational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that one being cannot produce both good and evil by exposing the self-contradiction in their own position.

Core Idea
The dualists' own actions refute their principle.

Maturidi now turns to address the dualist sects who posit two eternal principles, one good and one evil. Their core claim is that a single source cannot produce opposites. In this passage, he exposes the logical flaw in that claim by turning their own argument against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the dualist premise
The dualists claim that one being cannot produce both good and evil. Mark where this premise appears in the passage.
The phrase 'لا يجوز أن يكون واحد يجيئ منه خير وشر' states their premise.
3
See how Maturidi turns it against them
Maturidi asks: if each of the two principles can only produce one type, how can the world (which contains both) arise from them? This exposes the contradiction.
He uses their own logic to question the origin of the mixed world.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical conclusion
Maturidi ends with rhetorical questions about foolishness and ignorance. Why? Because the dualists' own disputation (an action that contains both good and evil) comes from a single being (themselves).
This shows their premise is self-refuting.
5
Connect to tawhid
The argument reinforces Islamic tawhid: only Allah, who is one, can create both good and evil as part of a wise plan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذ القول بأن لا يجوز أن يكون واحد يجيئ منه خير وشر، ومن هذا قوله كيف كان في رأيه منها العالم الذي توجد فيه الثنوية وكل واحد منهم هذا وصفه ؟ فينقض عليهما فعلهما ما لذلك ادعى لهما ذلك. أترى سفها أعظم مما عملاهما بأنفسهما أو جهلاً أبين من ذلك ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then it is said to them: Since the saying that it is not possible that one (being) produce from it good and evil – and from this (premise) is his saying: how is it in his view from them (the two principles) the world in which dualism exists, and each one of them is described this way? So their own action contradicts them, not that which they claimed for them. Do you see any foolishness greater than what they both did to themselves, or ignorance more evident than that? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the dualist dogma that one being cannot produce both good and evil. Maturidi asks: if the dualists claim that no single source can produce both good and evil, then how do they explain the existence of the world, which contains both? They say the world comes from two principles, one good and one evil. But then each of those principles, according to their own claim, can only produce one type of effect. So how can the world, which is a mixture, arise from them? In fact, the dualists' own actions (their disputation and claims) contradict their premise. They are engaged in discourse that involves both good and evil, yet they come from a single being (the dualist himself). Thus, they demonstrate foolishness and ignorance. The conclusion is that their position is self-refuting.

Key Terms
خير وشر (good and evil)
واحد (one)
العالم (the world)
الثنوية (dualism)
سفه (foolishness)
جهل (ignorance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One being cannot produce both good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the dualists themselves engage in actions that contain both good and evil, so their premise is false. In Islamic tawhid, Allah alone creates both good and evil as part of His wisdom.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Maturidi point out in the dualist position?
A) They claim one being cannot produce both good and evil, yet the world from their principles contains both.
B) They claim light is good, but it produces evil.
C) They claim darkness is evil, but it produces good.
D) They claim two principles, but the world is one.

Key Takeaway
The dualist position is self-refuting because their own actions contradict their premise that one being cannot produce both good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold that your own daily actions contradict? Take a moment to identify one such inconsistency—what is it, and why do you cling to the belief despite the contradiction?
?
When you argue with others, do you ever unknowingly violate your own principles? Think of a recent disagreement: did your method or tone betray the very truth you were defending?
?
The dualists were blind to their own self-refutation. What blind spot might you have in your understanding of tawheed—where does your life suggest you believe in two sources (one for good, one for bad) rather than submitting it all to Allah?

intermediate
6 min
Refuting Dualism: Wisdom Beyond Human Comprehension
Maturidi refutes dualism and defines divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the dualist claim that good and evil originate from two separate principles, and his explanation of divine wisdom as placing everything in its proper place, even if human intellect cannot fully grasp it.

Core Idea
What appears as foolishness from God is wisdom beyond human grasp.

Maturidi now takes on one of the most persistent challenges to divine unity: the dualist idea that good and evil must come from two different sources. This lesson unpacks his logical dismantling of that claim and his profound redefinition of wisdom. As you read, notice how he turns the dualists' own assumptions against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without getting lost in details. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument unfold: the initial refutation, the objection, and his response.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question 'أترى سفها أعظم...' as it highlights the absurdity of the dualist position.
2
Analyze the refutation argument
Identify why Maturidi says the dualist claim is self-refuting. Notice how he uses their own premise (each principle produces only its own kind) to show that the existence of both good and evil in the same world contradicts that premise.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the definition of wisdom
Maturidi defines wisdom as 'placing everything in its proper place' (al-?i?aba). He argues that what seems like foolishness from God is actually wisdom beyond human understanding. Reflect on how this answers the objection and protects divine transcendence.
Compare this definition with common notions of wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذ القول بأن لا يجوز أن يكون واحد يجيئ منه خير وشر، ومن هذا قوله كيف كان منها العالم الذي [توجد فيه الثنوية] وكل واحد منهم هذا وصفه فينقض عليهما فعلهما ما لذلك ادعى لهما ذلك. أترى سفها أعظم مما عملاهما بأنفسهما أو جهلاً أبين من ذلك؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله. فإن قال قائلهم : كيف زعمتم أنه يجوز أن يكون من الحكيم يجيئ فعل السفه؟ قلنا : هذا لا يجيئ ممن هو حكيم بذاته، إنما يجيئ ممن يجهل كما قلتم في النور من الجهل بعمل الظلمة ونحو ذلك. فأما الله سبحانه يتعالى عن ذلك. لكن قد يجوز أن يكون فعل حكمة لا يبلغها عقل البشر، وإلا فهو يجل عن ذلك. وما الحكمة إلا الإصابة: أن يوضع كل شيء موضعه، ويعطى كل ذي حظ حظه، ولا يبخس بأحد حقه. وإنما أبى من يظن بالله أو لا يعلم بما يضيف إليه الموحدون ذلك، لجهلهم بحدود الحكمة ومبلغ الحظوظ، وإيجابهم الحقوق لمن ليست لهم. وسنذكره إن شاء الله في موضع هو أملك به من هذا.
Then it is said to them: For the statement that it is not permissible that one [principle] should bring forth good and evil, and from this is His saying: how was from it the world in which dualism exists, and each one of them this is its description? So their action refutes both of them, so that is why he claimed that for them. Do you see any foolishness greater than what they both did to themselves, or ignorance more evident than that? And there is no power except with Allah. Then if their questioner says: How do you claim that it is permissible for an act of foolishness to come from the Wise? We say: This does not come from one who is wise by essence; it only comes from one who is ignorant, as you said about the light being ignorant of the work of darkness and similar. As for Allah, exalted is He above that. But it may be that He did an act of wisdom that the intellect of humans does not reach; otherwise, He is too exalted for that. And wisdom is nothing but correctness: that everything be placed in its proper place, and every possessor of a portion be given his portion, and no one be deprived of his right. But only he refuses who thinks of Allah or does not know what the monotheists attribute to Him, due to their ignorance of the limits of wisdom and the extent of portions, and their imposing rights on those who have none. And we will mention it, God willing, in a place more fitting for it than this.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) claim that good and evil originate from two separate principles (light and darkness, or God and an opposing deity). Maturidi begins by pointing out the inconsistency in their position: if each principle produces only its own kind (good from light, evil from darkness), then how can the world contain both? Each principle's action contradicts the other, so the claim is self-defeating. He then asks rhetorically if there is any greater foolishness or ignorance than what they attribute to their principles. Next, he addresses an objection: 'How can you claim that an act of foolishness (like creating evil) can come from a wise being?' Maturidi replies that this cannot come from one who is wise by essence; it comes only from one who is ignorant. But they themselves attribute ignorance to the light (i.e., the good principle) when it allows darkness to act. However, Allah is exalted above such ignorance. God's actions may be wisdom that human intellect cannot fully grasp; He is too exalted for foolishness. Wisdom is defined as placing everything in its proper place and giving each its due. Those who deny this do so out of ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom and what is due to whom. Maturidi promises to address this more fully elsewhere.

Key Terms
ثنوية
حكمة
سفه
حكيم بذاته
إصابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that good and evil come from two separate principles (e.g., God and an evil deity) is logically coherent.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this claim is self-refuting because the world's mixture of good and evil contradicts the premise that each principle produces only its own kind. Moreover, attributing foolishness to God is based on a misunderstanding of divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, why can't the dualist claim that one principle produces both good and evil?
A) Because both principles are inherently incapable of producing good.
B) Because the actions of the two principles would contradict each other, refuting the claim.
C) Because evil cannot exist at all, so the argument is moot.
D) Because the dualist has no proof for their claim.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing its internal contradiction and redefines wisdom as placing everything in its proper place, thereby preserving God's perfection even when His actions appear mysterious to finite minds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or meaningless, do you silently accuse God of foolishness? How can you actively surrender your limited understanding to His perfect wisdom, even when it hurts?
?
Do you ever secretly wish for a second 'principle' to blame—like shaytan or your circumstances—for your own sins? How does Maturidi's refutation of dualism call you to take full responsibility before the One God?
?
If true wisdom is 'placing everything in its proper place,' what in your life is out of place because you have demanded your 'rights' from God instead of trusting His allocation? Will you repent and realign today?

Metaphor vs Reality in Divine Attributes and Warning Against Mu'tazila
Objection-answer on attribute predication and critique of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī distinction between metaphorical and real attribute predication and the gravity of the Mu'tazilī error.

Core Idea
Denying real attributes leads to theological ignorance and spiritual ruin.

This passage from Imam Māturīdī's 'Kitāb al-Tawḥīd' engages with a Mu'tazilī objection about divine attributes. It clarifies that statements like 'redness is an attribute of red' are permissible only metaphorically, not as real predication in the theological sense. The passage concludes with a stark warning against following such erroneous views. (Note: the source has high OCR risk, so read with careful attention.)

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Do not analyze yet; just get a feel for the flow.
The beginning 'إلى آخر يصف طوله' may refer to a prior argument.
2
Identify the core claim about attributes
Notice the author's statement that saying 'redness is an attribute of red' is not denied but is only metaphorical (majāz) and not real (ḥaqīqa) in the sense used for divine attributes.
This distinction is key to the Māturīdī position.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection is that if attributes can be described, an infinite regress follows. The answer limits description to the act of speaking—it does not imply a real attribute lying in another attribute.
The answer uses a pragmatic condition: 'as long as the describer is speaking'.
4
Reflect on the jurist's critique
The jurist al-faqīh condemns the Mu'tazila for following someone of such shallow understanding that even an ignorant monotheist would be shocked. This shows the seriousness of the error.
Consider why the author chose such strong language.
5
Consider the eschatological warning
The passage ends with a warning that such a leader will lead his followers to Hell. This underscores that theological error has real consequences.
This is a call to adhere to sound 'aqīda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى آخر يصف طوله، يجوز أن يُقال: وصفه لنا في كتابه زعم أن هذا واضح. ثم قال: وإنّا لا ننكر جواز إطلاق القول بأن الحمرة صفة الأحمر، والرحمة صفة الفعل، لكن على المجاز والحقيقة ما ذكرت. ثم عورض بأنه يجوز إذا أن يكون للصفة صفة. قال: نعم، بمعنى أنها توصف، لكن ذلك إنما يوجد ما دام الواصف به قائلاً، فإذا أمسك لا. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: تأملوا عظيم منزلة المعتزلة بالائتمام بهذا الذي هذا مبلغ علمه بالصفة والموصوف والمجاز والحقيقة مما لو قرف به أجهل أهل توحيد الله لاستعظمه. ثم يقدم قومه يوم القيامة فيوردهم المورد الذي هذا وصف سبيله. نسأل الله العصمة.
To the end, he describes its length, it is permissible to say: his description of it in his book he claims that this is clear. Then he said: And we do not deny the permissibility of applying the statement that redness is an attribute of the red, and mercy is an attribute of action, but metaphorically and as a reality what I mentioned. Then it was objected that it is permissible then for the attribute to have an attribute. He said: Yes, in the sense that it is described, but that only exists as long as the describer is speaking with it, and when he stops, no. The jurist (al-faqīh), may Allah have mercy on him, said: Reflect on the great status of the Mu'tazila by following this person whose knowledge of attribute and described, metaphor and reality is such that if a most ignorant of the people of tawḥīd were accused of it, he would consider it great. Then he will lead his people on the Day of Resurrection and bring them to the place whose description is this. We ask Allah for protection.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer exchange regarding the reality of divine attributes, followed by a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila. The discussion begins with a claim that describing God's attributes (like length) is permissible. The author then states that while they do not deny the permissibility of saying 'redness is an attribute of red' as a linguistic expression, it is metaphorical (majāz) and not a real (ḥaqīqa) attribution in the sense the Mu'tazila intend. An objection is raised: if attributes can be described, then an attribute might itself have an attribute, leading to infinite regress. The answer is that such description is only valid while the speaker is actively describing; when speech ceases, the attribution does not persist in reality. Then the faqīh (likely the author's teacher) condemns the Mu'tazila for following a person of such shallow understanding of the distinction between attribute (ṣifa) and described (mawṣūf) and between metaphor (majāz) and reality (ḥaqīqa), suggesting that even an ignorant monotheist would be shocked by his error. He warns that such a leader will lead his followers to Hell on Judgment Day. The passage ends with a prayer for divine protection from such error.

Key Terms

موصوف
مجاز
حقيقة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilī position on divine attributes is merely a terminological dispute with no serious consequences.
Correction: This passage shows that the Mu'tazilī view is based on a fundamental confusion between metaphorical and real predication, leading to ignorance of tawḥīd and ultimately to punishment in Hell.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition does the opponent give for an attribute to have an attribute?
A) The attribute must be eternal.
B) The describer must be actively speaking.
C) The attribute must be a divine action.
D) The attribute must be metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī denial of real divine attributes is not a minor difference but a profound error that leads to ignorance and eschatological peril.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'Merciful' or 'Powerful,' do you truly believe these attributes are real descriptions of His essence, or do you treat them as poetic metaphors? What would change in your worship if you realized they are merely figurative?
?
This passage warns that a misguided leader will drag his followers to Hell. Whose teachings about Allah's attributes are you following? Have you critically examined whether your understanding of al-Ṣifāt aligns with the Qur'an and the way of the Salaf? If not, what step will you take today to verify?
?
The jurist says that even the most ignorant person of tawḥīd would be shocked at the Mu'tazilī's confusion. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that would shock a simple believer from the early generations? Name one, and commit to tracing it back to the sources—or discarding it.
