The Mu'tazila and Dualists on Origination
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila to Dualists on world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila's view on the world's origination with that of the Dualists, highlighting the absence of a distinct creator.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and Dualists both deny a distinct act of creation.

Maturidi begins this passage by drawing a striking parallel between the Mu'tazila and an ancient sect, the Dualists, on how the world came to be. He wants to show that their reasoning leads to a denial of a distinct Creator. Read carefully to see the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi quotes the Dualists' view that things emerge from non-existence without a separate act of bringing into existence.
Notice the language: 'without there being any bringing into existence'.
2
Identify the parallel
See how Maturidi says 'and likewise is said by the Mu'tazila' — he directly equates their position.
This is a critique, not a compliment.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means if the world comes to be without an external creator. How does this conflict with Islamic theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا من مضاهاتهم في هذا قول الثنوية أن الأشياء كانت معدومة ثم وجدت من غير أن كان ثَمّ إيجاد غير خروجها من العدم. وقالت الثنوية: كان النور والظلمة متباينين فامتزجا، فكان هذا العالم من غير أن كان ثَمّ تباين غير أو امتزاج غير، فصار العالم عالما بنفسه بعد أن لم يكن عالما، إذ لا غير هنالك أوجب ذلك . وكذا قيل المعتزلة على ما ذكرنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .

واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن محدث؛ ولم يكن دليلهم على سوى وجود العالم ، فأوجبوا حدثه وقالوا في ذلك بمحدث. ثم قالت المعتزلة في الله سبحانه : أن كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهو اليوم كذلك. فصيّروه في أول أحوال ما وقع للخلق به العلم غير خال عن الحوادث، كالعالم الذي وجدوه بالحس غير خال عنها ؛ فصار السبب الذي عرفوا حدث العالم به هو الذي به عرفوا حدث الخالق الرحمن.

والله قديم لم يزل.

ثم بعد هذا وجهان أحدهما أنه إذا لزم القول في جملة العالم بالحدث - وإن لم نشهده بوجودنا ما شهدنا منه غير خالٍ من الأحداث للزم ذلك في الصانع الخالق، لوجودنا له ما به نُسمّيه حدثًا. والثاني أنه قد وجب قدم ذاته، مع ما لا يُعلم وجوده إلا بحوادث، لم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم وإن كان غير خالٍ عن الحوادث ؟ وبعد، فإن معرفة احتمال الحوادث فيما لا يُحس من العالم بما أضيف إليه من الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون. فالله على قولهم يضاف إليه الرحمة والصنع والإبداع
And also from their approximations in this is the saying of the Dualists that things were non-existent then came to be without there being any bringing into existence other than their coming out of non-existence. And the Dualists said: light and darkness were distinct, then they mixed, so this world came to be without there being any other distinctness or other mixture, so the world became a world by itself after it was not a world, since there is no other there that necessitated that. And likewise is said by the Mu'tazila as we mentioned. And there is no power except by God.

And the Mu'tazila and others used as proof for the world's temporality that it is not free of a temporal originator; and their proof was based only on the existence of the world, so they necessitated its temporality and said concerning that [it has] a temporal originator. Then the Mu'tazila said about God: that He was not a creator nor merciful nor compassionate, and He is today such. So they made Him in the first of the states that came to the creation with knowledge, not free of temporal events, like the world which they found by sense perception not free of them. So the cause by which they knew the world's temporality became that by which they knew the temporality of the Creator, the Merciful.

And God is eternal, never ceasing.

Then after this, two aspects: one of them is that if the statement about the entirety of the world is necessitated by temporality – even if we do not witness by our existence what we witnessed of it not free of temporal events, then the same would be necessitated about the Maker, the Creator, because we find in Him that by which we call it temporal. And the second is that it is necessary that His essence is eternal, along with what is not known to exist except through temporal events – why is it not necessary to say that the entirety of the world is eternal even if it is not free of temporal events? Moreover, the knowledge of the possibility of temporal events in what is not perceived of the world, by what is attributed to it of gathering and separating and motion and stillness. So God, according to their saying, is attributed with mercy, action, and origination.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage extends the critique by comparing the Mu'tazila's view to that of the Dualists (الثنوية). Maturidi first notes that the Dualists claim things emerge from non-existence without an external act of bringing into existence; they give the example of light and darkness mixing to produce the world. The Mu'tazila, according to him, hold a similar position. Then he turns to the Mu'tazila's standard proof for the world's temporality: the world is never free of temporal events, so it must have a temporal originator. However, Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila also claim that God in pre-eternity was not Creator or Merciful, implying He acquires these attributes later. Thus, by the same reasoning, God would also be subject to temporal events, making Him temporal. Maturidi states categorically that God is eternal. He then presents two aspects (وجهان) of the argument. First, if the world's temporality is proven by its not being free of events, then the same reasoning would apply to God because we find in Him occurrences (e.g., creating after not creating). Second, if God's essence is eternal despite being known through temporal events, why can't the world be eternal despite being associated with events? He concludes by noting that the properties of the world (gathering, separating, motion) indicate temporal possibility, but the Mu'tazila's attribution of mercy, action, and origination to God implies temporality in Him, which is inconsistent with His eternity.

Key Terms
الثنوية
العدم
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila affirm a distinct Creator like mainstream Islam.
Correction: Maturidi shows they effectively deny a distinct act of creation, equating them with Dualists.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim the Mu'tazila share with the Dualists?
A) Belief in one God
B) Denial of a distinct act of bringing into existence
C) Belief that light and darkness created the world
D) Affirmation of eternal attributes

Key Takeaway
Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila's view on the world's origination with that of the Dualists, both lacking a distinct creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if the world runs on its own, without needing God's constant act of creation? In what areas of your life do you act like the Dualists or Mu'tazila, attributing outcomes to natural processes rather than to Allah's power?
?
When you see something come into being, do you pause to acknowledge that it existed only because Allah brought it into existence? Or do you take the act of creation for granted, as if things simply happen?
?
The Mu'tazila believed things come from non-existence by themselves. Do you ever feel self-sufficient, as if your successes are your own doing, forgetting that there is no power except by Allah? What would change if you truly believed that every moment of your existence is sustained by a distinct act of Allah's will?

Critique of the Contingency Argument
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila proof for world's temporality as leading to God's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique that the Mu'tazila's proof for the world's temporality also implies God's temporality, which is absurd.

Core Idea
The same reasoning proves God temporal if applied consistently.

Maturidi now turns to the standard Mu'tazila argument for the world's temporality: the world is never free of temporal events, so it must have a temporal originator. He exposes a fatal flaw: the same logic can be applied to God, leading to the conclusion that God is also temporal. This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila proof
Read how the Mu'tazila argue that the world is temporal because it is not free of temporal events.
Their proof is based on observing the world's change.
2
Follow the reductio
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila also say God was not Creator in pre-eternity, so He acquired that attribute. Thus, by the same reasoning, God is not free of temporal events.
This makes God temporal.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that their proof backfires. The cause that proved the world's temporality also proves God's temporality.
Therefore their argument is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا من مضاهاتهم في هذا قول الثنوية أن الأشياء كانت معدومة ثم وجدت من غير أن كان ثَمّ إيجاد غير خروجها من العدم. وقالت الثنوية: كان النور والظلمة متباينين فامتزجا، فكان هذا العالم من غير أن كان ثَمّ تباين غير أو امتزاج غير، فصار العالم عالما بنفسه بعد أن لم يكن عالما، إذ لا غير هنالك أوجب ذلك . وكذا قيل المعتزلة على ما ذكرنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .

واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن محدث؛ ولم يكن دليلهم على سوى وجود العالم ، فأوجبوا حدثه وقالوا في ذلك بمحدث. ثم قالت المعتزلة في الله سبحانه : أن كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهو اليوم كذلك. فصيّروه في أول أحوال ما وقع للخلق به العلم غير خال عن الحوادث، كالعالم الذي وجدوه بالحس غير خال عنها ؛ فصار السبب الذي عرفوا حدث العالم به هو الذي به عرفوا حدث الخالق الرحمن.

والله قديم لم يزل.

ثم بعد هذا وجهان أحدهما أنه إذا لزم القول في جملة العالم بالحدث - وإن لم نشهده بوجودنا ما شهدنا منه غير خالٍ من الأحداث للزم ذلك في الصانع الخالق، لوجودنا له ما به نُسمّيه حدثًا. والثاني أنه قد وجب قدم ذاته، مع ما لا يُعلم وجوده إلا بحوادث، لم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم وإن كان غير خالٍ عن الحوادث ؟ وبعد، فإن معرفة احتمال الحوادث فيما لا يُحس من العالم بما أضيف إليه من الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون. فالله على قولهم يضاف إليه الرحمة والصنع والإبداع
And also from their approximations in this is the saying of the Dualists that things were non-existent then came to be without there being any bringing into existence other than their coming out of non-existence. And the Dualists said: light and darkness were distinct, then they mixed, so this world came to be without there being any other distinctness or other mixture, so the world became a world by itself after it was not a world, since there is no other there that necessitated that. And likewise is said by the Mu'tazila as we mentioned. And there is no power except by God.

And the Mu'tazila and others used as proof for the world's temporality that it is not free of a temporal originator; and their proof was based only on the existence of the world, so they necessitated its temporality and said concerning that [it has] a temporal originator. Then the Mu'tazila said about God: that He was not a creator nor merciful nor compassionate, and He is today such. So they made Him in the first of the states that came to the creation with knowledge, not free of temporal events, like the world which they found by sense perception not free of them. So the cause by which they knew the world's temporality became that by which they knew the temporality of the Creator, the Merciful.

And God is eternal, never ceasing.

Then after this, two aspects: one of them is that if the statement about the entirety of the world is necessitated by temporality – even if we do not witness by our existence what we witnessed of it not free of temporal events, then the same would be necessitated about the Maker, the Creator, because we find in Him that by which we call it temporal. And the second is that it is necessary that His essence is eternal, along with what is not known to exist except through temporal events – why is it not necessary to say that the entirety of the world is eternal even if it is not free of temporal events? Moreover, the knowledge of the possibility of temporal events in what is not perceived of the world, by what is attributed to it of gathering and separating and motion and stillness. So God, according to their saying, is attributed with mercy, action, and origination.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage extends the critique by comparing the Mu'tazila's view to that of the Dualists (الثنوية). Maturidi first notes that the Dualists claim things emerge from non-existence without an external act of bringing into existence; they give the example of light and darkness mixing to produce the world. The Mu'tazila, according to him, hold a similar position. Then he turns to the Mu'tazila's standard proof for the world's temporality: the world is never free of temporal events, so it must have a temporal originator. However, Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila also claim that God in pre-eternity was not Creator or Merciful, implying He acquires these attributes later. Thus, by the same reasoning, God would also be subject to temporal events, making Him temporal. Maturidi states categorically that God is eternal. He then presents two aspects (وجهان) of the argument. First, if the world's temporality is proven by its not being free of events, then the same reasoning would apply to God because we find in Him occurrences (e.g., creating after not creating). Second, if God's essence is eternal despite being known through temporal events, why can't the world be eternal despite being associated with events? He concludes by noting that the properties of the world (gathering, separating, motion) indicate temporal possibility, but the Mu'tazila's attribution of mercy, action, and origination to God implies temporality in Him, which is inconsistent with His eternity.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
المحدث
الخالق
الرحمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's proof for the world's temporality is sound and does not affect God's eternity.
Correction: Maturidi shows it leads to the absurd conclusion that God is temporal, so the proof is flawed.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim is the consequence of applying the Mu'tazila's reasoning to God?
A) God becomes eternal
B) God becomes temporal
C) The argument becomes invalid
D) The world becomes eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila's proof for the world's temporality, if consistently applied, would also prove God temporal, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that because something in your life is changing or has a beginning, it must be invalid or temporary? How does this passage challenge you to think more carefully about the nature of the Eternal?
?
In your own understanding of God, do you ever unconsciously imagine that God's attributes only become real when they relate to creation? How does the Maturidi critique make you accountable for a more perfect understanding of God's eternity?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's oneness, but their logic led to a contradiction. Are there any areas in your faith where you confidently argue a position without seeing where it leads? Will you examine your own beliefs in light of this lesson?

Two Arguments Against the Mu'tazila
Maturidi presents two arguments against the Mu'tazila's reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments (wajhan) Maturidi presents to show inconsistencies in the Mu'tazila's position on temporality and eternity.

Core Idea
If God is eternal despite events, the world could be too.

After presenting his reductio, Maturidi lays out two formal arguments (وجهان) against the Mu'tazila position. The first argues that if the world's not-being-free-of-events proves its temporality, then God's not-being-free-of-events (by their view) would also prove His temporality. The second argues that if God's essence can be eternal while known through temporal events, why can't the world be eternal despite its events? This challenges the Mu'tazila's criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read the two aspects
Read the passage from 'then after this, two aspects' until the end. Identify the two distinct arguments.
They are marked by 'one of them' and 'the second'.
2
Understand the first argument
The first says: if the world's temporality is proven by its events, then the same applies to God because we find in Him events (like creating).
This is a parallel to the earlier reductio.
3
Understand the second argument
The second says: if God's essence is eternal despite being known through temporal events, then why can't the world be eternal despite its events?
This challenges the criterion used.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends by noting that the Mu'tazila's attribution of mercy, action, etc. to God implies temporality in Him, which is inconsistent.

?

?

In your own life, where do you assume that change implies imperfection? Will you let God be both unchanging in essence and actively involved in your changing circumstances without demanding He fit your mental categories?
?

Refutation of Dualist and Mu'tazili Views on Origin
Maturidi criticizes dualist and Mu'tazili claims about the world's origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that the world came into being spontaneously from light and darkness mixing without a Creator.

Core Idea
No world can come into existence by itself; every effect requires a cause.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid tackles an ancient debate about the world's origin. Some groups claimed the world emerged on its own, without need for a Creator. Maturidi exposes the logical flaw in such claims, emphasizing that existence cannot arise from nothing without an external agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of Maturidi's style and argument. Then read the translation.
Notice that Maturidi uses a concluding phrase of dependence on Allah.
2
Identify the dualist claims
The passage presents two related dualist claims: things emerging from non-existence without an agent, and light and darkness mixing spontaneously to form the world. Underline these claims in the text.
3
Understand Maturidi's critique
Maturidi points out that if the world came about by itself, it would be self-caused, which is absurd. He then links the Mu'tazila to a similar faulty reasoning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' reaffirms that all existence depends on God, countering the dualist denial of a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل اسم سني، لو قلب اسم العالم لكان أعذر، ولكن ذا آية عواقب السوء. وأيضا من مضاهاتهم في هذا قول الثنوية أن الأشياء كانت معدومة ثم وجدت من غير أن كان ثَمّ إيجاد غير خروجها من العدم. وقالت الثنوية: كان النور والظلمة متباينين فامتزجا، فكان هذا العالم من غير أن كان ثَمّ تباين غير أو امتزاج غير، فصار العالم عالما بنفسه بعد أن لم يكن عالما، إذ لا غير هنالك أوجب ذلك. وكذا قيل المعتزلة على ما ذكرنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Every noble name, if the name of the world were turned, it would be more excusable, but this is a sign of evil consequences. And also from their imitations in this is the statement of the Dualists that things were non-existent then came into existence without there being any bringing into existence other than their emergence from non-existence. And the Dualists said: Light and darkness were separate then they mixed, so this world came to be without there being any other separation or other mixing, so the world became a world by itself after it was not a world, since there was no other that necessitated that. And likewise it is said about the Mu'tazila according to what we mentioned. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. It begins by stating a general principle: even if one were to manipulate the names of things, it would not justify the error. Then it criticizes the dualists (al-Thunawiyya) who claim that things emerged from non-existence without a creator, simply by themselves. The dualists also claim that light and darkness originally were separate, then mixed spontaneously, producing the world without any external agent. Maturidi points out that this would mean the world became a world by itself, which is absurd. He then links the Mu'tazila to a similar faulty reasoning, concluding with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' to affirm dependence on God.

Key Terms
ثنوية
إيجاد
نور
ظلمة
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dualists believed in a creator; this passage shows they denied any external cause.
Correction: The dualists explicitly claimed that things emerged from non-existence or mixed spontaneously without any external agent, thus denying a Creator.

Checkpoint

A) They were created by God
B) They were separate and then mixed to form the world
C) They are eternal and never change
D) They are illusions

Key Takeaway
The world cannot bring itself into existence; genuine creation requires a Creator.

?

What specific step can you take today to consciously affirm your dependence on Allah as the only true cause of your existence?

Commit to one specific gratitude dua today to acknowledge Allah's role in that event.
?
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' Write down one specific action you will take this week to consciously invoke 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' in that situation.

The Mu'tazila and Magian Doctrine of Will
Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila doctrine of God's will to the Magian evil thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critical comparison between the Mu'tazila doctrine of God's will and the Magian belief in an evil thought, and why he considers them essentially similar.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila will is no different from Magian evil thought — both arise without choice.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi draws a striking parallel between the Mu'tazila concept of God's will and the Zoroastrian idea of an evil thought. He argues that both doctrines posit an unoriginated, necessitated source of creation or evil. By linking them, Maturidi exposes what he sees as a theological error. Let's examine his reasoning and the hadith he cites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation carefully. Notice the parallel structure Maturidi uses.
2
Identify the key claims
Find the Mu'tazila claim about God's will and the Magian claim about the evil thought. List their similarities.
Both arise from God without prior will or choice.
3
Understand the analogy and hadith
Maturidi equates the two doctrines. Then he cites the hadith that Qadariyya are the Magians of this community. Ask: why does this apply to Mu'tazila?
The common misunderstanding is that the hadith only applies to the Qadariyya; Maturidi extends it to the Mu'tazila will theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : كان الله سبحانه، ثم حدث منه إرادة كان بها العالم، من غير أن كان منه إياها إحداث أو إرادة، أو لها اختيار؛ إذ لا غير لها سوى ذاته، وقد كان ذاك قبلها. وكذلك قالت المجوس أن كان الله سبحانه، فحدثت فكرة رديئة كان منها الشيطان؛ وهو الذي كان به كل شر، فسمّت المجوس تلك فكرة والمعتزلة إرادة واختيارًا. وكان حدوثها لا باختيار وإرادة، فهي بالفكرة أشبه؛ ثم لم تكن هي غير الشر، ولا الإرادة عند المعتزلة غير العالم. فهذا - والله أعلم - معنى قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة».
And the Mu'tazila said: Allah the Exalted existed, then from Him originated a will (irāda) by which the world came to be, without Him having originated it by any bringing into existence or will, and without it having choice; because there is no other besides His essence, and that existed before it. And likewise the Magians said: Allah the Exalted existed, then an evil thought (fikra radiyya) originated from which came Satan; and he is the one by whom every evil came, so the Magians called that a thought and the Mu'tazila called it will and choice. And its origination was not by choice or will, so it is more similar to a thought; then it was nothing other than evil, and the will according to the Mu'tazila is nothing other than the world. So this – and Allah knows best – is the meaning of the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison. Maturidi reports that the Mu'tazila claim God brought forth a will from Himself without any prior act of creating or willing, and that will itself had no choice but necessarily produced the world. He then compares this to the Magian (Zoroastrian) belief that an evil thought originated from God, producing Satan and all evil. The Magians called it a thought; the Mu'tazila call it will and choice. Maturidi points out that both originate without any prior will or choice, hence they are more akin to a thought than a genuine will. He then cites the hadith that the Qadariyya (those who deny predestination, often including Mu'tazila) are the Magians of this community, concluding that the two doctrines are essentially similar.

Key Terms
إرادة
اختيار
فكرة
مجوس
قدرية
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community' refers only to the Qadariyya sect, not the Mu'tazila.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the Mu'tazila doctrine of will is analogous to the Magian evil thought, and he cites the hadith to support this, showing that the Mu'tazila are included in the condemnation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the Magians claim originated from God?
A) A good thought
B) An evil thought that produced Satan
C) A will that created the world
D) Light and darkness

Key Takeaway
Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila notion of an unoriginated will with the Magian evil thought, both arising without choice, and uses the hadith to condemn them as the Magians of this community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that any belief about God's will that falls short of the truth may align you with the Magians, does your heart feel the urgency to ensure your understanding is exactly as the Quran and Sunnah teach? What specific step will you take this week to verify your understanding of divine attributes?
?
Maturidi reveals that a doctrine can appear to be about God's will but in reality be closer to a 'thought' without choice. Are there any beliefs you hold about God's actions—perhaps about how He creates or decrees—that, if examined, might similarly reduce His will to a mere impulse? What will you do to purify your creed from such subtle errors?
?
The hadith 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community' warns that theological deviation can place one in the camp of falsehood. How does that warning make you feel about the responsibility of learning ʿaqīdah correctly? Will you commit to studying from sound sources and abandoning speculative theology?

Mu'tazila and Majus: Parallel Origins of Evil
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila and Majus origins of evil, citing a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila and Majus views on the origin of evil and what this reveals about the hadith identifying Qadariyya as the Majus of this community.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila will and the Majus thought both arise without divine choice, making them akin to dualist heresy.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi draws a striking parallel between two seemingly different groups: the rationalist Mu'tazila and the dualist Majus (Zoroastrians). Both, he argues, attribute the origin of evil—or even the world itself—to something that arises from God without His choice or will. This comparison leads to a profound reading of the prophetic hadith that calls the Qadariyya 'the Majus of this community.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure between the Mu'tazila and Majus claims.
The passage begins with a reference to re-creation and accidents, but the main focus is on the two groups.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
Maturidi reports that the Mu'tazila say God existed, then a will (irada) arose from Him by which the world came to be, without God having any further will or choice over that will.
Note that this will is said to arise without choice—it is not freely chosen by God.
3
Understand the Majus claim
Similarly, the Majus say God existed, then an evil thought (fikra radi'a) arose, from which came Satan and all evil.
The thought also arises without choice, making it analogous to the Mu'tazila will.
4
Identify the parallel and the hadith
Maturidi equates the two: the Mu'tazila will is like the Majus thought, and then he cites the hadith that the Qadariyya (those who deny divine decree) are the Majus of this community.
This shows that Maturidi sees the Mu'tazila position as a form of dualism.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this comparison is significant: it affirms that God is the sole creator and that everything, including evil, occurs by His will and decree, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Majus views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإعادة، وكل هذا عندهم حوادث، فيجب القول فيه بما يجب في العالم؛ ويكون لمن هو بهذا الوصف خالق صانع خارج من ذلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
وقالت المعتزلة : كان الله سبحانه، ثم حدث منه إرادة كان بها العالم، من غير أن كان منه إياها إحداث أو إرادة، أو لها اختيار ؛ إذ لا غير لها سوى ذاته، وقد كان ذاك قبلها وكذلك قالت المجوس أن كان الله سبحانه، فحدثت فكرة رديئة كان منها الشيطان؛ وهو الذي كان به كل شر، فسمّت المجوس تلك فكرة والمعتزلة إرادة واختيارًا. وكان حدوثها لا باختيار وإرادة، فهي بالفكرة أشبه ؛ ثم لم تكن هي غير الشر، ولا الإرادة عند المعتزلة غير العالم فهذا - والله أعلم - معنى قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة».
And re-creation, and all this according to them are accidents, so it is necessary to say about it what is necessary about the world; and the one described as such must have a creator and maker outside of that. And there is no power except with God. And the Mu'tazila said: God, glorified be He, existed, then from Him arose a will by which the world came to be, without there being from Him an origination of it or a will, nor did it have choice; for there is nothing other than His essence, and His essence was before it. And likewise the Majus said that God, glorified be He, existed, then an evil thought arose, from which came Satan; and it was he through whom every evil came to be. So the Majus called that a thought, and the Mu'tazila called it will and choice. And its coming into existence was not by choice or will, so it is more similar to a thought. Then it was not other than evil, and the will according to the Mu'tazila is not other than the world. This — and God knows best — is the meaning of the saying of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace: 'The Qadariyya are the Majus of this community.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage compares the Mu'tazila and Majus (Zoroastrian) views on the origin of evil and then cites a hadith. Maturidi first notes that re-creation (al-i'ada) and similar phenomena are considered accidents (hawadith) by these groups, necessitating a creator external to the world. He then states the Mu'tazila claim: God existed, then a will (irada) arose from Him by which the world came into being, without God having any further will or choice regarding that will, nor did that will itself have choice. This will is not other than God's essence, but it arose after His essence. Similarly, the Majus claim that an evil thought (fikra radi'a) arose from God, from which Satan came, bringing all evil. The Majus called this a thought, while the Mu'tazila called it will and choice. Since both arose without will or choice, the Mu'tazila concept is more akin to the Majus thought. The Mu'tazila will is not other than the world, just as the thought is not other than evil. Maturidi then cites the hadith identifying the Qadariyya (those who deny divine decree) as the Majus of this community, linking the Mu'tazila view to Zoroastrian dualism.

Key Terms
الإعادة (al-i'ada)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
المجوس (al-Majus)
إرادة (irada)
فكرة (fikra)
القدرية (al-Qadariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila will (irada) is a separate entity with its own choice, but the passage clarifies that it arises without any choice or will.
Correction: The Mu'tazila will is not an independent agent; it arises from God without choice, similar to how the Majus thought arises.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila and Majus both claim arises from God without choice or will?
A) The world and Satan respectively
B) A will and a thought respectively
C) An accident and a substance respectively
D) Good and evil respectively

Key Takeaway
Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila theory of a self-originating will with the Zoroastrian belief in an evil thought, viewing both as undermining divine omnipotence and foreshadowing the hadith on Qadariyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself attributing good or evil to your own will apart from Allah's decree, how are you inadvertently echoing the Mu'tazila or Majus? Will you repent today and affirm that all events, even your thoughts, are by Allah's will?
?
The hadith calls Qadariyya the Majus of this community. Are there any areas where you subconsciously think that some evil originates independently of Allah? How will you fight this belief starting now?
?
Maturidi argues that an uncreated will is like a dualist thought. Do you truly believe that no will or thought arises without Allah's choice, or do you still cling to a sense of autonomy? What would it take to surrender that completely?

Dualist and Mu'tazilite Views on Power: A Comparison
Maturidi compares dualist and Mu'tazilite doctrines on power, showing a parallel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both dualists and Mu'tazila divide power over actions between two agents, and why Maturidi sees a parallel.

Core Idea
Both dualists and Mu'tazila split power over actions between two beings.

Maturidi begins by describing the dualist notion of two gods, each with exclusive power over good or evil. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite doctrine that divides power between God and human: the human has acquisition (al-kasb) and God has creation (al-ījād). He points out that in both systems, each agent lacks power over the other's domain, making them structurally similar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation to grasp the overall comparison.
2
Identify the dualist view
Find the statement that the world is the act of two, each with exclusive power over good or evil.
Dualists include zanadiqa and Majus.
3
Identify the Mu'tazilite view
Notice the division: servant has acquisition (kasb), God has creation (ijad); neither has power over the other's domain.
4
Observe the parallel
See how both systems create a dual division of power, each agent lacking power over the other's sphere.
5
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila resemble the dualists in this structural division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و مذهب الزنادقة أن العالم كان فعل اثنين، ليس لأحدهما في فعل آخر صنع ولا تدبير ولا قدرة، وأن كل واحد منهما ينفرد بنوع من الفعل من الشر والخير لا يقدر عليه الآخر. وكذلك مذهب المجوس. وعلى مذهب الاعتزال أن العبد له قدرة على نوع من الفعل وهو الكسب، وللمعبود نوع وهو الإيجاد، وليس الله على ما للعبد قدرة ولا صنع، ولا للعبد على ما الله؛ وعلى هذين الأمرين دار تدبير العالم. فضاهاوا به من ذكرت في التحصيل. ثم ازداد مذهب هؤلاء قبحا من حيث جعلوا منه قدرة على ما للعبد من السكون والحركة فلما أقدر الله العبد عليهما ذهبت عنه القدرة. ولا نرى الثنوية تزيل قدرة واحد منهما بالتمكين من الآخر. ليُعلم أن معنى القدرة في الذي أضيف إليه الربوبية عند الثنوية أحق أن يكون بنفسه منه عند المعتزلة، وفي ذلك إزالة القدرة الله أن يكون بذاته، وهو أقبح قول. والثاني كذبهم، إذ ثبتوا للعبد في نوع فعله ما ثبتوا للصانع، ولم يثبتوا له اسم الألوهية الذي عُرف الله به من فعل الإنشاء، وكذلك اسم الخالق. جميع مع ما كانت المعتزلة يزيدون في رتبة قدرة العبد القادر على قدرة الله، لأنهم يقولون: إن الله لا يقدر بالموعود أن يكون وبما كان الوفاء فعله مع الوصف بالقدرة. وذلك نحو ما يقول الله تعالى في تمام الآية: قال اخرج منها مذؤما مدحورًا لمن تبعك منهم لأملأن جهنم منكم أجمعين.
And the doctrine of the zanadiqa (heretics) is that the world was the act of two, neither of them having creation (ṣunʿ), arrangement (tadbīr), or power (qudra) in the act of the other, and that each of them is uniquely occupies a type of action from evil and good which the other cannot do. Likewise the doctrine of the Majus (Zoroastrians). And upon the doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that the servant has power over a type of action, which is acquisition (al-kasb), and the worshipped has a type, which is bringing into existence (al-ījād), and God has no power over nor creation of what is for the servant, nor does the servant have power over what is for God; and upon these two matters the arrangement of the world revolves. So they resemble those whom I mentioned in the analysis. Then the doctrine of these became uglier in that they made for Him power over what is for the servant of rest and motion, but when God empowered the servant over them, the power (from God) left. And we do not see the dualists removing the power of one of them by enabling the other. So that it may be known that the meaning of power in that to which lordship is attributed among the dualists is more rightful to be by itself than in the Mu'tazila, and in that is the removal of God's power being by His essence, which is the ugliest statement. And the second is their lie, for they affirmed for the servant in his type of action what they affirmed for the Maker, but they did not affirm for him the name of divinity (al-ulūhiyya) by which God is known from the act of creation (al-inshāʾ), and likewise the name of creator (al-khāliq). All this while the Mu'tazila increase the rank of the power of the capable servant over God's power, because they say: God is not able for what is promised to be, and since the fulfillment of His action was with the description of power. And that is similar to what God Almighty says in the complete verse: 'He said: Get out of it, despised and banished. Whoever follows you among them, I will surely fill Hell with all of you.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage compares the dualist (zanadiqa, Majus) doctrine with the Mu'tazilite doctrine on power and lordship. It begins by describing the dualist view: the world is the work of two separate beings, each with exclusive power over a type of action (good or evil), with no interference. The Mu'tazilite view, Maturidi explains, divides power between God and man: the servant has power over acquisition (al-kasb), and God has power over creation (al-ījād). Neither has power over what belongs to the other. Maturidi says this parallels the dualist division. He then declares that the Mu'tazilite position is even uglier: they attribute to God a power over human actions like rest and motion, but when God gives power to the servant over those actions, God's power departs—something dualists do not claim. Thus, the dualist concept of power is more consistent with divinity than the Mu'tazilite one, because the dualists at least affirm that each god's power is essential. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, strip God of essential power while granting the servant a power that rivals God's, yet they deny the servant the title of divinity. Finally, Maturidi accuses them of lying: they affirm for the same type of action what they affirm for the Creator, but refuse to call the servant 'god' or 'creator'. They also claim that God cannot fulfill His promises, citing the verse about filling Hell as an example of God's inability to do what He promised.

Key Terms
زنادقة
مجوس
كسب
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila are entirely different from dualists because they believe in one God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that despite monotheism, the Mu'tazilite division of power into distinct domains (human acquisition, divine creation) structurally parallels the dualist division, making them resemble polytheism.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the similarity between the dualist and Mu'tazilite doctrines of power?
A) Both believe in two creators.
B) Both divide power between two agents, each lacking power over the other's domain.
C) Both claim that human servants have no power.
D) Both affirm that God has power over all actions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite division of power between God and man structurally resembles dualist polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you unconsciously act as if your decisions are outside Allah’s control, as if you have an independent power like the dualist or Mu’tazilite claim?
?
Does it disturb you that the Mu’tazilite view—which many modern Muslims find reasonable—structurally resembles polytheism? How does that affect your trust in Allah’s complete sovereignty?
?
If you truly believed that every good and evil action depends entirely on Allah’s creation and your own acquisition is only a means, would that change how you pray for guidance and strength? What will you do about it today?

Maturidi's Refinement: Mu'tazila Are Worse than Dualists
Maturidi refines the comparison, arguing Mu'tazila are worse than dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite position on power is more problematic than the dualists' because it strips God of essential power while granting the servant divine-like ability.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila make God's power vanish after empowering the servant, a problem dualists avoid.

After showing the structural parallel, Maturidi argues that Mu'tazila are actually worse than dualists. He points out that they attribute to God a power over human actions that disappears once the servant is empowered, whereas dualists keep power inherent. He then highlights additional absurdities: Mu'tazila affirm for the servant what they affirm for God but deny him the titles, and they claim God cannot fulfill His promises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the first criticism
Find where Maturidi says that Mu'tazila make God's power over rest/motion leave when servant is empowered.
Dualists do not remove power from one god when enabling the other.
3
Understand the implication
This shows that the dualist concept of power is more deserving of being essential to divinity than the Mu'tazilite one.
4
Identify the second criticism
Mu'tazila lie by affirming for the servant the same type of power as for God but refusing to call the servant a god or creator.
5
Identify the third criticism
Mu'tazila increase the servant's power rank over God's by claiming God cannot fulfill His promises, citing the Hell verse.

Thus, the dualist concept of power is more consistent with divinity than the Mu'tazilite one, because the dualists at least affirm that each god's power is essential. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, strip God of essential power while granting the servant a power that rivals God's, yet they deny the servant the title of divinity. Finally, Maturidi accuses them of lying: they affirm for the same type of action what they affirm for the Creator, but refuse to call the servant 'god' or 'creator'. They also claim that God cannot fulfill His promises, citing the verse about filling Hell as an example of God's inability to do what He promised.

Key Terms
قدرة
ألوهية
خالق
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is worse than Mu'tazilism because it is polytheistic.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite position is actually more problematic due to its internal contradictions regarding power and divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one reason the Mu'tazilite position is uglier than the dualist one?
A) Dualists believe in two gods, while Mu'tazila believe in one.
B) Mu'tazila make God's power vanish after empowering the servant, while dualists keep power inherent.
C) Mu'tazila deny that humans have any power.
D) Dualists claim that God cannot fulfill His promises.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite division of power leads to greater theological absurdity than dualism, as it strips God of essential power and makes incoherent affirmations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that every action you perform is within God's power, do you ever act as if you are the ultimate decider? How does Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila expose your own subtle tendency to claim independence from God? Repent of any moment today where you forgot His sovereignty.
?
The Mu'tazila affirmed for humans the same kind of power they affirmed for God, yet they refused to call the servant a god. Are you guilty of living as though you are self-sufficient while professing belief in God alone? Identify one habit where you rely solely on your own strength, and commit to praying for God's help before doing it.
?
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila elevated the servant's power above God's by denying God's ability to fulfill His promise. Do you truly trust that God will fulfill every promise He has made to you, or do your worries and anxieties reveal a deeper doubt about His power? Write down one promise of God you struggle to believe, and today ask Him to strengthen your certainty in His absolute ability.

Zanadiqa and Mushabbiha: Denials of Creation and Divine Nature
Al-Maturidi presents the denials of the Zanadiqa and Mushabbiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the denials of the Zanadiqa and Mushabbiha regarding creation from nothing and the nature of God.

Core Idea
Some sects deny God's creative power and incorporeality due to limited imagination.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his refutation of heretical sects by addressing the Zanadiqa (materialists) who deny creation ex nihilo, and the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) who attribute a body to Allah. Their denial stems from restricting divine attributes to what is imaginable. This lesson examines their claims and the reasons behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two groups mentioned and the reason for their denial.
The Zanadiqa and Mushabbiha are distinct groups with different denials.
2
Identify the denials
The Zanadiqa deny creation from nothing; the Mushabbiha say God is a body. Find how each is described.
3
Understand the reasoning
Both denials are based on what is conceivable in imagination. Why is that a flawed criterion?
Divine power is not limited by human imagination.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Al-Maturidi is implicitly refuting these views by stating them as erroneous. Think about how he would counter them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأنكرت الزنادقة كون شيء يحدث لا من شيء، لما لا يتصوّر مثله في الوهم وكذلك المشبهة في قولهم بالجسم. وأنكرت المعتزلة خلق أفعال العباد، لما ليس بقائم في العقول ولا متوهم في الأوهام. وزعمت القدرية أن الله لا يدع الغاية من الخير الذي يقدر عليه إلا فعله . وكذلك قول الزنادقة أنه يفعل غاية الخير وأن خالق الشر غيره. وكذلك قول القدرية أن الله لا يقدر على شيء موجود من الشر، وأنه كله فعل العباد. ويقول المعتزلة : إن الله لا يريد كون الشر لأحد ومن أحد، ويريده الشيطان، ثم يكون ذلك وإن لم يكن ما يريده الله كما قالت الزنادقة في كون ذلك من الشيطان وخالق الشر وإن لم يرده الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The Zanadiqa denied that something comes into existence from nothing, because its like is not conceivable in the imagination, and likewise the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) [denied] in their saying [that He is] a body. And the Mu'tazila denied the creation of the actions of servants, because it is not established in the intellects nor imagined in the imaginations. And the Qadariyya claimed that God does not leave undone the utmost good that He is capable of, but does it. And likewise the saying of the Zanadiqa that He does the utmost good, and that the creator of evil is other than Him. And likewise the saying of the Qadariyya that God is not capable of any existing evil, and that all of it is the actions of servants. And the Mu'tazila say: God does not will the occurrence of evil for anyone or from anyone, and Satan wills it, then it occurs even if that which God wills does not occur, just as the Zanadiqa said concerning that it is from Satan and the creator of evil even though God did not will it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the erroneous denials of various heretical sects. Al-Maturidi begins by stating the denials of the Zanadiqa (materialists/atheists) who deny creation ex nihilo because they cannot imagine it. Similarly, the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) deny that God is not a body. Then he mentions the Mu'tazila, who deny that God creates the actions of servants because they cannot conceive it rationally. Next, the Qadariyya claim that God always does the utmost good and cannot refrain from it. The Zanadiqa also claim that God does the utmost good and that the creator of evil is someone else. The Qadariy further claim that God cannot create any existing evil, so all evil is from human actions. Finally, the Mu'tazila claim that God does not will evil from anyone, but Satan wills it, and it occurs contrary to God's will, similar to the Zanadiqa view about evil originating from Satan. Al-Maturidi presents these views as rejections of God's absolute power and will, concluding with a prayer for strength.

Key Terms
الزنادقة
المشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something cannot be imagined, it cannot be true.
Correction: Al-Maturidi refutes this by showing that the Zanadiqa's denial is based on imagination, which is not a valid criterion for divine actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Zanadiqa deny creation from nothing?
A) Because they believed God is a body
B) Because they could not imagine it
C) Because they thought it is illogical
D) Because they followed the Mushabbiha

Key Takeaway
The Zanadiqa and Mushabbiha reject divine truths based on the limits of human imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What Islamic belief do you find hardest to accept because it doesn't fit your imagination? How will you train yourself to submit your imagination to revelation?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture Him with human-like traits? What specific attribute do you struggle to conceive without a mental image, and what step will you take today to purify your tawhid?
?
The Zanadiqa rejected what they couldn't imagine. Where in your life have you rejected a command of Allah because it seemed 'unimaginable' or unrealistic for you? What is one such command you will commit to obeying starting now?

Mu'tazila Denial of Divine Creation of Human Actions
Al-Maturidi states Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila deny that God creates human actions and the reasoning behind their denial.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila deny God's creation of actions because they cannot conceive it rationally.

Imam al-Maturidi next addresses the Mu'tazila, who deny that God creates the actions of human beings. Their denial is based on a claim that such creation is not established in intellects nor imagined in imaginations. This lesson explores their position and its flaws.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation focusing on the Mu'tazila denial.
The phrase 'خلق أفعال العباد' is key.
2
Identify the reason

They rely on rational and imaginative evidence.
3
Critique the reasoning
Ask: Is it valid to deny something simply because it is not rationally established? How does this limit God's power?
Al-Maturidi implicitly refutes this by listing it as an error.

Qadariyya and Mu'tazila on Good, Evil, and Divine Will
Al-Maturidi presents the Qadariyya and Mu'tazila views on good, evil, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya and Mu'tazila positions on God's role in good and evil, and how they limit divine power.

Core Idea
Some sects claim God must do the utmost good and cannot create evil, restricting His absolute will.

Imam al-Maturidi then discusses the Qadariyya and Mu'tazila, who impose limits on God's power regarding good and evil. The Qadariyya claim God always does the utmost good and cannot create evil, while the Mu'tazila say God does not will evil. This lesson examines these views and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic text on Qadariyya and Mu'tazila. Note the claims about good, evil, and will.
The passage connects Qadariyya and Zanadiqa.

Qadariyya say God does the utmost good, cannot refrain from it, and cannot create evil. Find these points.
3
Identify Mu'tazila claim
Mu'tazila say God does not will evil; Satan wills it and it occurs against God's will.
This resembles the Zanadiqa view.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Maturidi lists these as denials of divine power. How do these claims limit God?
They imply God is not all-powerful or all-willing.

?

Refutation of the Batiniyya on Divine Names
Maturidi refutes the Batiniyya's allegorical interpretation of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Batiniyya's allegorical interpretation of divine names leads to ta'til (divesting God of attributes) and ultimately to polytheism.

Core Idea
Allegorizing divine names to avoid anthropomorphism can end in worshipping multiple gods.

Maturidi here confronts a subtle threat to tawhid: the Batiniyya sect. They claimed to protect God's transcendence by interpreting names like 'Allah' as referring to the first intellect, not to God Himself. But in doing so, Maturidi shows, they inadvertently strip God of all attributes and end up with a belief system that worships multiple entities. This passage is a powerful warning that overcorrection in avoiding tashbih can destroy tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the Batiniyya's claims about the Intellect and Soul.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand their doctrine.
2
Identify Maturidi's critique
Focus on the consequences Maturidi draws: ta'til (stripping God of attributes) and polytheism (worshipping multiple entities under God's names). Notice how he shows that their rejection of names leads to a god without knowledge, power, or will.
Maturidi argues that if God does not know or will creation, He is no different from a natural force.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: Why does this matter for understanding divine names? What is the correct balance between affirming attributes and avoiding anthropomorphism?
This lesson shows that negating all names to protect tawhid can actually destroy it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الباطنية ، وهم الذين يصرفون المذكور من الأسماء إلى المبدع الأول والثاني نحو العقل والنفس، ويجعلون كل العالم مبروزاً في العقل، تستمد منه النفس فيمد الهيولى. يقولون: كان العقل بالإبداع والإبداع علّته، مبروز فيه كل شيء يكون . ومحال أن يُبرزه بالإبداع من لم يعلم ما يكون، أو من لا يقدر على أن يُبرزه، أو من لا يريد أن يكون مبروزاً ؛ فيخرج الإبداع منه خروج فعل ذي طبع من حيث لا يشعر به ولا يعلمه ولا يوصف بالقدرة [عليه ] ؛ فيكون الله تعالى عنده - في نفي الصفات عنه والأسماء كراهية التشبيه - يصير في حد التعطيل، ويصير بحيث لا شيء عليه بدليل، ويحصل القول منه التقليد، وذلك بعيد . والله الموفق. مع على ما يقال: «الله» اسمه أو هو اسم غيره؟ فيرجع في الحقيقة إلى أنه اسم العقل، و«الرحمن» اسم النفس ؛ وعلى هذا مذهبهم. وأبوا الاسم كراهة التشبيه، ثم جعلوا المعبود باسم الإله والرحمن والرحيم أغيارًا لا يُحصى عددهم، وأجزاء يصعب إحصاؤهم. فكأن الرسل جاءوا عندهم بعبادة العدد لا بالتوحيد والله المستعان . ثم يقال لهم عند قولهم ليس له اسم ما تعنون بقولكم : ليس له اسم ذاتي ولا صفة
And the Batiniyya said—and they are those who divert the mentioned names to the first and second originator, such as the Intellect and the Soul, and they make the whole world manifested in the Intellect, from which the Soul draws and then extends to matter. They say: The Intellect was through origination (ibdā') and origination is its cause, and everything that will be is manifested in it. And it is impossible that one who does not know what will be, or who cannot manifest it, or who does not will that it be manifested, should manifest it through origination; thus origination from Him would be like the action of a natural agent, without Him being aware of it or knowing it or being described as having power over it. So Allah in their view—by negating attributes and names from Him out of dislike for tashbīh—falls into the limit of ta'tīl (divesting God of attributes), and becomes such that nothing can be proved about Him by evidence, and speech from Him becomes mere imitation, and that is far [from truth]. And Allah is the granter of success. Moreover, concerning what is said: 'Allah' is His name or is it the name of another? In reality it reverts to being the name of the Intellect, and 'al-Rahman' is the name of the Soul; and this is their doctrine. And they rejected the name out of dislike for tashbīh, then they made the object of worship under the names 'al-Ilah', 'al-Rahman', 'al-Rahim' into distinct entities whose number cannot be counted, and parts difficult to enumerate. So it is as if the messengers came according to them to worship multiplicity, not tawhid. And Allah is the Succorer. Then it is said to them regarding their statement that He has no name: What do you mean by your saying, 'He has no essential name nor attribute'?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage refutes the Batiniyya sect, who allegorize divine names and attributes. Maturidi describes their doctrine: they divert names like 'Allah' to the first intellect and 'al-Rahman' to the soul, claiming the world is manifested in the intellect. He then criticizes this: if their god creates without knowledge, power, or will, it becomes like a natural force, not a personal God. By negating attributes to avoid tashbīh, they fall into ta'tīl (stripping God of attributes) and make religious knowledge mere imitation. Furthermore, they end up worshipping multiple entities (intellect, soul, etc.) under the names of God, thus turning tawhid into polytheism. The passage ends by questioning their claim that God has no name, implying that they contradict themselves.

Key Terms
الباطنية
المبدع الأول
العقل
النفس
الإبداع
التعطيل
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating all names and attributes from God preserves tawhid and avoids anthropomorphism.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this leads to ta'til (stripping God of attributes) and, when names are applied elsewhere, to polytheism. True tawhid requires affirming names and attributes as revealed, without tashbih or ta'til.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the consequence of the Batiniyya's negation of names and attributes?
A) They achieve true tawhid.
B) They end up in ta'til (divesting God of attributes) and ultimately polytheism.
C) They properly avoid anthropomorphism.
D) They become monotheists like others.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that true tawhid requires affirming divine names and attributes as revealed, without tashbih or ta'til.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself uncomfortable with a divine name or attribute and tried to 'interpret' it away to make it more palatable? What does Maturidi's critique of the Batiniyya say about that impulse, and how might it be a subtle form of ta'til in your own heart? Will you resolve to embrace all of Allah's names as they are revealed, trusting His wisdom over your comfort?
?
The Batiniyya thought they were protecting tawhid by rejecting names, but they ended up worshipping multiple entities. What 'names' or concepts about God are you tempted to hold onto so tightly that you might be worshiping your own idea of Him rather than Him as He has described Himself? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your worship is directed to Allah alone, as He is, not as you imagine Him?
?
Maturidi says that without attributes, nothing can be proved about God by evidence. When you struggle to understand a divine attribute, do you ever feel like giving up on trying to know Him? How does this lesson challenge you to seek knowledge of Allah through His names, rather than abandoning them? What is one name of Allah you will study deeply to strengthen your tawhid?

The Batiniyya Interpretation of Divine Names and Its Refutation
Maturidi presents the Batiniyya doctrine of Intellect and Soul and refutes its denial of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Batiniyya doctrine of the Intellect and Soul, and Maturidi's argument that their denial of divine attributes leads to complete negativism (ta'til).

Core Idea
Denying divine names to avoid anthropomorphism can lead to negating God entirely.

The Batiniyya were a sect that reinterpreted the divine names, such as 'Creator,' as referring to secondary originated beings: the Intellect and the Soul. They thought this preserved God's transcendence. But Imam Maturidi refutes their view, showing that it ultimately empties God of all attributes and makes rational knowledge of Him impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Batiniyya claim
Read carefully how they divert the names to the Intellect and Soul, and their description of the world's manifestation.
Notice the terminology: al-mubdi' al-awwal wa al-thani.
2
Identify Maturidi's refutation
Notice the logical argument: it is impossible for an ignorant, powerless, or unwilling being to originate. Then see the consequence: denial of attributes leads to ta'til (negativism) and taqlid (blind imitation).
3
Reflect on the danger of overemphasis on transcendence
Consider how an excessive fear of anthropomorphism can backfire into negating God's attributes altogether.
Maturidi's refutation shows the importance of affirming attributes without comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الباطنية، وهم الذين يصرفون المذكور من الأسماء إلى المبدع الأول والثاني نحو العقل والنفس، ويجعلون كل العالم مبروزاً في العقل، تستمد منه النفس فيمد الهيولى. يقولون: كان العقل بالإبداع والإبداع علّته، مبروز فيه كل شيء يكون . ومحال أن يُبرزه بالإبداع من لم يعلم ما يكون، أو من لا يقدر على أن يُبرزه، أو من لا يريد أن يكون مبروزاً ؛ فيخرج الإبداع منه خروج فعل ذي طبع من حيث لا يشعر به ولا يعلمه ولا يوصف بالقدرة [عليه ] ؛ فيكون الله تعالى عنده - في نفي الصفات عنه والأسماء كراهية التشبيه - يصير في حد التعطيل، ويصير بحيث لا شيء عليه بدليل، ويحصل القول منه التقليد، وذلك بعيد . والله الموفق.
And the Batiniyya said—they are those who divert the mentioned names to the first and second originate (al-mubdiʿ al-awwal wa al-thānī) such as the Intellect and the Soul, and they make the whole world manifested in the Intellect, from which the Soul derives and then extends to the Hyle. They say: the Intellect was by origination (ibdāʿ) and origination is its cause, and everything that will be is manifested in it. And it is impossible that one who does not know what will be, or who is not able to bring it forth, or who does not will it to be manifested, should manifest it by origination; thus origination would issue from him like the act of a natural thing without him being aware of it or knowing it or being described as having power over it. So God Almighty— in their denial of attributes and names from Him, disliking anthropomorphism — becomes in the category of pure negativism, and becomes such that nothing is proven about Him by evidence, and speech from Him becomes imitation; and that is far. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of the Batiniyya and refutes it. The Batiniyya reinterpret divine names as referring to a first and second originated being: the Intellect (العقل) and the Soul (النفس). They claim that the entire world is manifested in the Intellect, which procures from it through the Soul to the Hyle. They hold that the Intellect came into being by 'origination' (الإبداع), and origination is its cause, and everything that will be is already contained in it. Maturidi then refutes this: it is impossible for someone who does not know what will be, or lacks power, or does not will the manifestation, to bring it forth. If they deny that God knows, wills, and has power over the act of origination, then His action would be like a natural process without awareness or power. Thus, by denying attributes and names to avoid anthropomorphism, they fall into pure negativism (التعطيل), where nothing can be proven about God, and they end up with mere imitation. Maturidi declares this far from the truth.

Key Terms
الباطنية
المبدع
العقل
النفس
الإبداع
الهيولى
التعطيل
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying all divine names and attributes prevents anthropomorphism and preserves God's transcendence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such denial leads to pure negativism (ta'til), making God unknowable and indistinguishable from nothing, and thus contradicts the purpose of tawhid.

Checkpoint
What do the Batiniyya reinterpret the divine names as?
A) The Intellect and the Soul
B) The Preserved Tablet and the Pen
C) The Throne and the Footstool
D) The highest and lowest spheres

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Batiniyya by showing that denying divine attributes under the guise of avoiding anthropomorphism actually leads to complete negativism, undermining rational knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in trying to protect Allah's majesty, ended up denying a truth about Him—like shying away from affirming His attributes out of fear of anthropomorphism? What would it mean for you to repent from that subtle negativism?
?
The Batiniyya's system reduced God to a passive, unknowing force. Search your heart: is there any area where you treat Allah as distant or uninvolved, as if He does not know, will, or act? How can you replace that with active trust in His perfect attributes?
?
Maturidi says their position leads to 'blind imitation' (taqlid). In what aspects of your faith are you relying on inherited beliefs rather than evidence from revelation and reason? Are you willing to examine them honestly, even if it challenges your comfort?

Four Erroneous Views on Knowing God through Self-Knowledge
Maturidi enumerates four deviant views on deriving knowledge of God from self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four erroneous ways that various sects have attempted to derive knowledge of God from self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is a path to God, but some sects take it in wrong directions.

Imam Maturidi begins a discussion on a well-known principle: "Whoever knows himself knows his Lord." However, different groups have misapplied this principle, leading to incorrect conclusions. In this passage, Maturidi presents four such erroneous views—from the Dualists, Jews, Anthropomorphists, and Jahm—without yet providing the correct answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: the agreed principle is stated first, then the disagreements.
The Arabic uses technical terms for each sect.
2
Identify each sect and their argument
For each of the four groups, note what aspect of self-knowledge they misuse to reach their conclusion about God.
The Dualists focus on good and evil; the Jews on composition; the Anthropomorphists on bodiliness; Jahm on originatedness.
3
Notice the common thread
All four groups start from self-knowledge but draw unwarranted conclusions by projecting creaturely limitations onto God.
4
Reflect on why these views are wrong
Consider: what is the error in each reasoning? Maturidi will later provide the correct approach, but for now, recognize that these are presented as deviant.
This is a preview of errors; do not assume Maturidi endorses them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التوحيد [معرفة الرب]
فإن قال قائل : أجمع العقلاء [على] أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة . فقالت الثنوية : لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيرته واحد جزء . وقالت المشبهة : هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم.
وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن ، و [ عرف أنه ] شيء، جسم، عالم، سميع ، بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الاسم فهو حدث، وربّه الذي أنشأه لا يُحتمل أن يكون حدثا .
Issue on Tawhid: Knowledge of the Lord.
If it is said: The rational people have agreed that whoever knows himself knows his Lord; but they disagreed regarding the aspect of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knows that his self includes good and evil, he knows that for each aspect of it there is a lord. The Jews made Him (God) one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body; because in the witnessed world, the knowledge of the self is of the body. And Jahm said: When he knows that he came to be after not being, and he knows that he is a thing, a body, knowing, hearing, seeing, he knows that everything to which that name applies is originated, and his Lord who originated him cannot be originated.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: the issue of Tawhid concerning knowledge of the Lord (ma'rifat al-rabb). It cites the well-known dictum that 'whoever knows himself knows his Lord,' noting that rational people (uqala) generally agree on this statement but differ on how to derive knowledge of God from self-knowledge. The passage then presents four erroneous views: (1) The Dualists (thunawiyya, likely Manichaeans) argue that since the self contains both good and evil, there must be two lords, one for each aspect. (2) The Jews (possibly a reference to anthropomorphic conceptions) are said to have made God 'one part,' implying composition. (3) The Anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) assert that God is a body because in the empirical world self-knowledge is knowledge of a body. (4) Jahm (Jahm ibn Safwan) argues that one knows oneself as originated (hadith) and possessing attributes like being a thing, body, knowing, etc.; thus everything with such names is originated, so the Creator cannot be originated. The passage ends here; Maturidi's own correct response is not included in the provided pages.

Key Terms

ثنوية
يهود
مشبهة
جهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These sectarian views are correct or endorsed by Maturidi.
Correction: Maturidi presents these only as deviant positions; he will refute them later.

Checkpoint
Which group argued that since the self contains good and evil, there must be two lords?
A) The Jews.
B) The Dualists.
C) The Anthropomorphists.
D) Jahm.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'know yourself to know your Lord' is agreed upon, but its application has been distorted by various sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the good and evil within your own soul, do you ever silently accuse God of being divided, or do you trust that He alone creates both, and then hold yourself accountable for choosing wrongly?
?
Jahm used your very experience of being created, knowing, hearing, and seeing to argue that God is beyond all that—but do you live as if you are a mere proof, or do you truly feel your contingency and respond with constant need of Him?
?
If the agreed principle is 'whoever knows himself knows his Lord,' then how much of your self-knowledge today—your habits, fears, desires—have you actually turned into knowledge of God, or have you stopped at self-absorption?

The First Source of Doubt: Blind Following (Taqlid)
Maturidi enumerates the first of three sources of doubt: blind following.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi regards blind following (taqlid) as the primary obstacle to rational acceptance of truth and how it prevents reflection on evidence.

Core Idea
Blind following suffocates reason by substituting habit for evidence.

Maturidi has just presented compelling rational proofs for God's existence. Yet many people still fail to accept these truths. In this passage, he begins to diagnose why: the first and most powerful obstacle is taqlid—blind adherence to inherited beliefs. He explains how this psychological attachment overrides the natural inclination toward truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the rhythm of Maturidi's argument come through.
Notice that the passage begins with a statement about three sources of doubt, then details the first.
2
Identify the three aspects mentioned at the start
Maturidi says doubt confronts people from 'three aspects' (أوجه ثلاثة). The passage only elaborates on the first. Keep in mind that two more are to follow in later passages.
3
Focus on the first aspect: taqlid
Taqlid is defined as following those to whom the soul is accustomed and inclined. Notice how Maturidi describes its effects: abandoning reflection on proofs and turning to wishful thinking.
4
Examine the motivations behind taqlid
Maturidi lists four psychological motivations: trust in authorities, desire for companionship, false hope of guidance, and expectation of favors. Each shows how taqlid is sustained by emotional and social factors.
These motivations are not exhaustive but represent common reasons people cling to tradition.
5
Reflect on the outcome of taqlid
The passage concludes that these habits eventually overcome a person. Consider how this outcome blocks rational acceptance of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرأيت الشبهة اعترضت البشر من بعد ما بينا مما يجب أن يكون به دفع الشبهة لمن تصح نفسه، من أوجه ثلاثة. أحدها التقليد بمن ألفت نفسه به ومالت إليه؛ فترك التفكر في الأدلة وأقبل على أماني النفس ثقة بهم أو رغبة في صحبتهم والوصول بهم إلى شهوات النفس، أو إنهاما لآرائهم أن تهيئ بهم إلى رشد، أو إنهاما لإنعامهم وغيره من أسباب السعادة؛ حتى يبلغ به العياد بشره النفس وسوء عاداتها.
So I saw that doubt confronts people after what we have explained, which must be repelled for one whose soul is sound, from three aspects. One of them is blind following of those to whom the soul has become accustomed and inclined; so they abandon reflection on proofs and turn to the wishes of the soul, trusting in them, or desiring their company and attaining through them the lusts of the soul, or imagining that their opinions will guide them to rectitude, or imagining their favors and other means of happiness; until their habit of evil and bad customs overcome them.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage identifies the first of three sources of doubt that prevent people from accepting the truth of divine existence. Maturidi states that after presenting the clear proofs, he observed that doubt arises from three aspects. The first is taqlid (blind following). People become accustomed to following certain leaders or traditions and become attached to them. As a result, they abandon thinking about evidence and instead follow their own wishful thinking. They do this either out of trust in those authorities, or to gain their companionship and fulfill their desires, or in the false belief that those authorities will guide them aright, or in hope of receiving favors from them. Over time, this habit of evil and bad customs becomes ingrained, and they are overcome by it. Thus taqlid is a major barrier to rational acceptance of the truth.

Key Terms
شبهة
تقليد
أماني النفس
العياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi condemns all forms of following others in matters of religion.
Correction: Maturidi specifically criticizes blind following that prevents critical thought and examination of evidence. Following learned scholars after understanding their reasoning is not condemned.

Checkpoint
What is the first source of doubt that Maturidi identifies?
A) Sensory limitations
B) Blind following (taqlid)
C) Philosophical arguments
D) Social pressure

Key Takeaway
Blind following is the first barrier to rational acceptance of truth because it replaces evidence with habit and wishful thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a religious figure or teacher you admire. Do you follow them because their arguments are sound, or because you are accustomed to their company or seek their approval? How can you ensure your loyalty is to the truth and not to the person?
?
Maturidi says blind following turns people away from reflection on proofs. Is there any aspect of your practice of Islam where you have avoided deep thought, relying instead on 'what everyone does'? What is one step you can take today to break that habit?
