The Importance of Deep Reflection in Refuting Dualism
Maturidi opens refutation by attributing error to lack of deep reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi begins his refutation of dualistic sects by emphasizing the necessity of deep reflection on previously established proofs.

Core Idea
Deep reflection on God's unity is the key to avoiding dualistic errors.

Imam Maturidi opens his refutation of dualism not with a direct argument, but with a methodological point: error stems from insufficient reflection. He urges the reader to consider that if the dualist sects had truly pondered the proofs already presented, they would not have fallen into their mistaken beliefs. This lesson examines that opening statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that the sentence is incomplete; it sets up a larger argument.
The text ends mid-sentence. The full refutation continues on the next page.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Maturidi uses the conditional 'if' (لو) to imply that the dualist sects did not reflect deeply, and that is why they erred.
This is a rhetorical technique that places the burden on the reader to reflect.
3
Connect to previous lessons
The phrase 'what was previously mentioned' refers to earlier proofs of God's unity and wisdom. Maturidi assumes the reader is familiar with those arguments.
4
Appreciate the rhetorical technique
The broken-off sentence builds anticipation for the detailed refutation that follows. It shows that Maturidi first addresses the root cause: lack of deep thought.
This methodological opening is a hallmark of scholarly kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ولو أمعن هؤلاء الفرق النظر فيما تقدم من ذكر
The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: {And if these sects had delved deeply into reflection on what was previously mentioned...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This is the beginning of Imam Maturidi's refutation of the dualistic and other deviant sects described in the previous lessons. He states that if these groups had carefully pondered the arguments already presented (presumably the proofs for the unity and wisdom of God mentioned earlier in the book), they would recognize the error of their positions. The passage is cut off, but it sets the stage for a detailed critique that follows on the next pages. The use of 'ولو أمعن' (if they had delved deeply) indicates a conditional rhetorical argument: since they did not reflect properly, they fell into error.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Dualist Arguments for Two Origins: Good and Evil
Maturidi presents the arguments of dualist sects for two origins of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main arguments dualist sects used to posit two eternal origins for good and evil.

Core Idea
Dualists argue that a single good God cannot be the source of evil, so good and evil must have separate origins.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the arguments of dualist groups such as the Manichaeans and Zoroastrians. They observe that the world contains both good and evil, and since a wise and merciful God would not create evil, they conclude that there must be two opposing eternal principles. This lesson outlines their reasoning and the sects that adopted it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the gist of the dualist arguments.
Notice the flow from the first two theories to the detailed moral argument.
2
Identify the three dualist theories
Look for the three explanations dualists give for the mixture of good and evil: (1) two distinct origins mixing, (2) one origin splitting into two, (3) the moral intuition argument.
The third argument is given the most detail.
3
Examine the moral argument in depth
Focus on the third argument based on human custom: a wise and merciful being only does good and avoids causing harm without benefit. Since God is wise and merciful, He cannot be the source of evil.
This is the core of the dualist case.
4
Connect to the named sects
See how the arguments lead to the doctrines of the Thanawiyya (light and darkness), the Magians (Allah and Shaytan), and the Dahriyya (materialists).
Maturidi will refute these arguments in the following lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضار. لكن منهم من يقول: كانا متباينين فامتزجا على ما مر بيانه. وعلى قول أصحاب الهيولى والطينة يجيء أن يكون كانا واحداً فتفرقا؛ إذ هو الأصل، فصار أصلاً للشر والخير، فبالتفرق عمل كل عمله. على أن عامة هؤلاء يجعلون كون العالم بالطبيعة لا بالفعل. والثالث الاعتبار بالمعاني، فقالوا: إنا نجد العالم اشتمل على نفع وضرر، وعلى خير وشر. ثم في العرف أن فاعل الخير محمود، ومن ينفع غيره فهو رحيم حكيم؛ وأن فاعل الشر مذموم، ومن يضر غيره فهو قاس سفيه. لم يجز أن يجيء من الله الذي هو حكيم رحيم فعل الشر أو الضرر بأحد، ومثله في الشاهد، ولا له السفه والقساوة. وهذا مما ينتفع به أو يدفع الضرر عن نفسه، فكيف لمن لا ينتفع بشيء ولا يضره شيء؟ على قولهم: إن الحكيم فيمن يجر بفعله النفع له لا الضرر. فأما من يضر غيره بلا نفع له فليس هو بحكيم. فقالوا: هذا باختلاف الأصل الذي منه العالم، ليرجع كل موجود فيه إلى أصله من خير أو شر؛ أو كان واحدا فيه الجوهران فتفرقا، فكان من كل ما يكون من مثله؛ أو بما اعترضت فيه الأعراض اختلف. الشاهد. فرجع إلى هذا قول الدهرية المنكرة للصانع والمثبتة جميعا لعدد، فسمت الثنوية الخير بجوهره نورا والشر ظلمة، والمجوس سموا الخير الله، والشر الشيطان.
And harmful. But some of them say: They were two distinct things then they mixed, as its explanation has passed. And according to the proponents of matter and clay, it comes that they were one then separated, for it is the origin, so it became an origin for evil and good, and by separation each did its work. Indeed, the majority of these make the existence of the world by nature not by action. And the third is consideration by meanings, so they said: We find that the world includes benefit and harm, and good and evil. Then in custom, the doer of good is praised, and whoever benefits others is merciful and wise; and the doer of evil is blamed, and whoever harms others is harsh and foolish. It is not permissible that from God, Who is wise and merciful, should come the act of evil or harm to anyone, and likewise in witnessed reality, and He does not have foolishness and harshness. And this is something by which one benefits or repels harm from himself, so how about one who is not benefited by anything nor harmed by anything? According to their saying: The wise is he who by his action brings benefit to himself, not harm. As for one who harms others with no benefit to himself, he is not wise. So they said: This is due to the difference of the origin from which the world [came], so that every existing thing in it returns to its origin of good or evil; or it was one containing the two essences, then they separated, so from each came what is like it; or because of the accidents that intervened in it, it differed. The witness. And to this returns the saying of the Dahriyya who deny the Creator and the [Zoroastrians] who affirm a number [of gods]; so the Thanawiyya named good by its essence light and evil darkness, and the Magians named good 'Allah' and evil 'Shaytan'.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of dualist groups (such as the Manichaeans and Zoroastrians) who posit two eternal origins for the world—one good (light) and one evil (darkness). The author (Imam Maturidi) first outlines several dualist theories: some say the two origins were distinct then mixed; others say they were one then separated, with the separation making each act according to its nature. The majority of these groups, Maturidi notes, hold that the world's existence is by nature rather than by voluntary action. Then he presents their third argument, which is based on moral intuition (consideration by meanings): they observe that the world contains both benefit and harm, good and evil. In human custom, a doer of good is praised as merciful and wise, while a doer of evil is blamed as harsh and foolish. Since they assume God is wise and merciful, they conclude that it is impossible for God to do evil or harm others. They reason that a wise being seeks benefit for himself, not harm; one who harms without gain is not wise. Therefore, they insist that the world's mixture of good and evil must arise from two different origins—either a single origin that later split, or two origins that always were distinct. Maturidi then notes that this line of reasoning leads to the doctrines of the Dahriyya (materialists who deny a Creator) and the dualists (Thanawiyya) who identify good as light and evil as darkness, and the Magians who call good 'Allah' and evil 'Shaytan'. The passage thus sets up the dualist case, which Maturidi will refute in the following lesson.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dualists believed in two equal gods.
Correction: Dualists actually posit two original substances (جوهران) that are inherent opposites, not necessarily personal gods.

Checkpoint
According to the dualist argument presented, why can God not be the source of both good and evil?
A) Because God is weak and cannot do evil.
B) Because a wise and merciful being would not do evil or harm without benefit.
C) Because evil is stronger than good.
D) Because God created evil separately.

Key Takeaway
Dualists argue that the coexistence of good and evil in the world proves they must originate from two distinct principles, not from a single good God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a painful event, do you ever find yourself silently accusing God of being unfair or unloving? How does the dualists' argument — that a wise and merciful God cannot do evil — expose your own doubts? This week, when hardship strikes, consciously say 'Allah is wise and merciful even if I do not understand.' What would it cost you to trust that?
?
The dualists claim that a wise being only acts for his own benefit. But Allah is self-sufficient and benefits from nothing. Is there a part of you that serves God only because you expect something in return — Paradise, reward, or comfort? How can you purify your intention (niyyah) so that you worship Him solely because He is worthy, without any expectation of personal gain? Commit to one act of worship this week with the explicit intention that you seek only His pleasure, not any worldly or otherworldly gain.
?
The passage mentions that the Magians called good 'Allah' and evil 'Shaytan'. Have you ever attributed a success to your own intelligence or effort, and a failure to 'Satan' or external circumstances, subtly denying that everything comes from Allah? How can you train yourself to say 'Alhamdulillah' for both the good and the seemingly bad, affirming that there is no creator but Allah? This week, every time something goes 'wrong', pause and say, 'This is from Allah, and He is wise.'

The Epistemological Self-Defeat of Dualism
Maturidi exposes the epistemological contradictions of dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi shows that dualism's own premises make knowledge of good and evil impossible.

Core Idea
Dualism's own logic makes reliable moral knowledge impossible.

Maturidi begins his refutation by turning the dualists' own principles against them. If each essence only perceives its own kind, then a human being – a mixture of both – cannot reliably distinguish wisdom from folly. This leads to a fundamental skepticism that undermines their entire system.

Guided Path
1
Consider the opening remark
Maturidi begins by stating that if dualists reflected, they would realize their minds cannot grasp even human wisdom. This sets the tone for the refutation.
2
Identify the core premise
Dualists hold that the essence of good sees only good, and the essence of evil sees only evil. A human is a mixture of both essences.
This is key to the epistemological problem.
3
Trace the unreliability of statements
Because a human sees by both essences, he may see wisdom as folly and vice versa. His claim 'it is good' is unreliable because we don't know which essence is speaking.
Maturidi uses this to undermine the dualist's ability to make any moral claim.
4
Observe the consequences for hope and fear
Since neither essence has power to harm or benefit the other, there is no basis for hope or fear. The entire purpose of knowing wisdom and folly is lost.
This shows the practical failure of dualism.
5
Understand the nature argument
If each essence acts by nature (not choice), then knowledge of wisdom (which requires deliberate placement) is impossible. This leads to the essence of good being mixed with evil and evil dominating.
The argument culminates in the conclusion that dualism is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو أمعن هؤلاء الفرق النظر فيما تقدم من ذكر الأدلة لعلموا قصور عقولهم عن الوقوف على الحكمة البشرية فضلاً أن يحيطوا بحكمة الربوبية. مع ما فيها إليه صاروا في الاختيار منع لهم عن دعوى معرفة حقيقة الحكمة والسفه؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن لا يرون بجوهر الشر إلا الشر وبجوهر الخير إلا الخير. ثم لا يدرى فيما سموه سفهاً أو حكمةً أنه فعل الشر أو فعل الخير. وكل الإنسان عندهم مشوب من الأمرين، يرى بكل واحد خلاف ما يرى بالآخر، فلعله رأى الحكمة سفهاً والسفه حكمة. ثم لا يوثق بقوله: «إنه خير»، فهو من جوهر الظلمة كذب كله ومن جوهر النور صدق كله، فلا يدرى بأي جوهرين ينطق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للآخر قدرة على النفع، فانقطع موضع الرجاء والخوف جميعاً؛ فيذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه. ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع، فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا محال، والسفه بالطبع. والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه، والسفه وضع كل شيء في غير موضعه. ومحال وصف ذي طبع به، إذ هو اختيار. والنور عندهم لا يعلم ما السفه فيحذره، ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة، والجهل بمائية الشيء والوضع له شر؛ فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل، ثم القدرة والعجز، بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه عن نفسه، ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به، فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوباً بالشر، وجوهر خير فيه، فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير. والذي هو خير لم يعرف الشر والسفه، فكيف يعرف هذا الذي يولد عن جوهر الخير، بعد غلبة الشر عليه، الخير والشر؟ على أن كل ذي طبع مقهور، إذ لا يملك صرف ما يوجبه الطبع وإيجاب الخلاف، وفي ذلك إيجاب قاهر يجعل ذا شراً بالطبع وهذا خيراً. ولو رد ذا إلى اثنين كان فيهما ما في هذين؛ نحو التسخين والتبريد، إنه يكون بمن جعله كذلك. وفي ذلك إيجاب القول بالواحد. ومن يقول بأن كل واحد منهما خالق قادر، فإنه لا يخلو كل واحد منهما من أن يعلم الوجه الذي يمنع الآخر عن عمله ويقدر أو لا يعلم ولا يقدر عليه.
Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Had these sects deeply considered the [evidence] previously mentioned, they would have known the deficiency of their minds to grasp human wisdom, let alone encompass the wisdom of divinity. Besides, what they have turned to in choice prevents them from claiming knowledge of the reality of wisdom and folly; for it is their doctrine that they see by the essence of evil only evil and by the essence of good only good. Then it is not known, regarding what they call folly or wisdom, whether it is the act of evil or the act of good. And every human, according to them, is mixed from the two [essences]; he sees by each one opposite of what he sees by the other, so perhaps he saw wisdom as folly and folly as wisdom. Then his statement 'It is good' is not reliable; for if he is from the essence of darkness, it is all falsehood, and if from the essence of light, it is all truth; so it is not known by which of the two essences he speaks. And there is no power except with God. Then since neither of them has power over harm, nor does the other have power over benefit, then the place of hope and fear is cut off altogether; so the benefit of knowing wisdom and folly disappears. Then since each of the two essences acts by nature, then the occurrence of knowledge of wisdom is impossible, and folly [acts] by nature. Wisdom is placing everything in its proper place, and folly is placing everything in other than its proper place. And it is impossible to describe one with a nature by it, for it is choice. According to them, light does not know what folly is to avoid it, nor does darkness know what wisdom is, and ignorance of the essence of a thing and its placement is evil; so the essence of light, according to them, becomes that in which knowledge and ignorance are combined, then power and incapacity, since it cannot repel folly from itself nor prevent darkness from harming it; so the essence of good, according to them, becomes mixed with evil, and [contains] some good, so it follows from their statement that evil dominates good. And that which is good did not know evil and folly, so how can this one, who is born from the essence of good after the dominance of evil over it, know good and evil? Moreover, everything with a nature is compelled, for it cannot control the repulsion of what nature necessitates nor the necessitation of the opposite; and in that there is the necessitation of a Compeller who makes this evil by nature and that good. And if one were to trace this back to two [principles], there would be in them what is in these two; like heating and cooling, that is by the one who made them so. And in that is the necessitation of asserting the One. And whoever says that each of them is a creator, powerful, then each of them is either knowing the way to prevent the other from its action and able, or not knowing and not able.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Maturidi's refutation of dualism. He begins by stating that if these sects had truly reflected on the proofs already presented, they would realize their minds cannot grasp even human wisdom, let alone divine wisdom. Furthermore, their own position on choice (that each essence only produces its own kind) prevents them from claiming knowledge of wisdom and folly, since a human who is a mixture of both essences cannot reliably distinguish them—what one essence sees as wisdom, the other may see as folly. Hence, no statement from such a person can be trusted, because it is unknown which essence is speaking. This leads to the destruction of hope and fear, since neither essence has power to benefit or harm the other. He then argues that since each essence acts by nature (طب, not choice), it is impossible for it to have knowledge of wisdom or folly, as wisdom requires deliberate choice and placing things in their proper positions. Light, they claim, does not know folly to avoid it, and darkness does not know wisdom. Consequently, the essence of light itself becomes a mixture of knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity—so the good essence is tainted with evil, and evil dominates. Maturidi then argues that anything acting by nature is compelled, and this necessitates a Compeller who makes it that way, leading to the affirmation of a single Creator. If one posits two creators, each must either be able to prevent the other or not; if unable, they are not truly powerful; if able but does not act, that is evil. Thus, the dualist position is contradictory and ultimately affirms the oneness of God.

سفه
طبع
اختيار
جوهران
نور
ظلمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument attacks all forms of dualism based on nature.
Correction: It specifically targets the dualists' claim that each essence acts by nature (not choice) and the resulting epistemological impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that a person from the dualist viewpoint cannot be trusted when claiming something is good?
A) Because humans are inherently sinful.
B) Because the person is a mixture of both essences, so it is unknown which essence is speaking.
C) Because the person is always lying.
D) Because good and evil are subjective.

Key Takeaway
Dualism leads to epistemological skepticism because a human, being a mixture of two essences, cannot reliably distinguish good from evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on moral judgments every day. After reading this lesson, would you say your own judgments about good and evil come from a single trustworthy source, or are they a mix of conflicting impulses? What one change could you make to purify your moral epistemology?
?
Maturidi argues that if you are a mixture of two opposing essences, you cannot be sure which side is speaking. When you face a moral dilemma, do you ever feel like two voices within you pull opposite ways? How can you train yourself to distinguish the voice of truth from the voice of misguidance?
?
This lesson shows that without hope and fear (of divine reward and punishment), the very reason to know wisdom disappears. Where in your life have you lost the sense of hope or fear that makes moral knowledge meaningful? What steps will you take to revive that urgency?

The Necessity of a Single Compeller
Maturidi argues for a single Compeller from the nature of dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that everything acting by nature is compelled, necessitating a single Compeller, and that the dualist position collapses when examining the relationship between two supposed creators.

Core Idea
Whatever acts by nature is compelled, which points to a single Compeller behind all existence.

Maturidi now shifts to a cosmological argument. If each essence acts by nature and not choice, then it is compelled – it cannot do otherwise. This compulsion demands a Compeller who made it that way. Furthermore, the idea of two independent creators leads to contradictions: either they can prevent each other or not, undermining their divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the compulsion claim
Maturidi states that everything acting by nature is compelled – it cannot do otherwise. This is a direct consequence of the dualists' own view.
Compulsion implies a lack of free will in the essences.
2
Note the necessity of a Compeller
Since the essences are compelled, there must be something that made them that way – a Compeller who designates one as evil and another as good by nature.
This points to a single efficient cause.
3
Examine the two-creators dilemma
If one posits two creators, each can either prevent the other from acting or not. If not, they are not powerful; if they can but don't, that is evil. Both options contradict the claim of being creators.
This dilemma forces the conclusion that there is only one Creator.
4
Conclude with the affirmation of the One
The argument leads to the necessity of affirming the one true God, who is the sole Compeller and Creator.
This is the ultimate refutation of dualism.

?

?
If you truly believed that you are not self-sufficient but completely dependent on the One who made you, what would you change about your daily habits of reliance, worry, or pride? Will you commit to one concrete act of reliance on God alone this week?

Why Dualism Leads to Complete Skepticism
Maturidi enumerates the skeptical implications of dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that belief in two eternal principles (good and evil) destroys the possibility of certain knowledge and moral evaluation.

Core Idea
Dualism makes every human judgment unreliable.

Maturidi now turns to an epistemological refutation of dualism. He shows that if reality is fundamentally split between a principle of good and a principle of evil, then humans, being mixtures of both, cannot have trustworthy knowledge. Worse, the very categories of wisdom and foolishness become meaningless. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Maturidi's flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dualist claim
Maturidi states that dualists believe they perceive only evil through the essence of evil and only good through the essence of good. This sets up the problem.
Notice the key terms: جوهر الخير and جوهر الشر.
3
Understand the mixture problem
Humans are composed of both essences, so any judgment could be from either. This makes it impossible to distinguish wisdom from foolishness.
The dualist's own principle makes their knowledge unreliable.
4
See the consequences
Because of this uncertainty, statements like 'this is good' cannot be trusted. Furthermore, since neither essence has power over harm or benefit, hope and fear vanish.
This destroys the purpose of moral knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة لعلموا قصور عقولهم عن الوقوف على الحكمة البشرية فضلاً أن يحيطوا بحكمة الربوبية. مع ما فيها إليه صاروا في الاختيار منع لهم عن دعوى معرفة حقيقة الحكمة والسفه؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن لا يرون بجوهر الشر إلا الشر وبجوهر الخير إلا الخير. ثم لا يدرى فيما سموه سفها أو حكمةً أنه فعل الشر أو فعل الخير. وكل الإنسان عندهم مشوب من الأمرين، يرى بكل واحد خلاف ما يرى بالآخر، فلعله رأى الحكمة سفها والسفه حكمة. ثم لا يوثق بقوله: «إنه خير»، فهو من جوهر الظلمة كذب كله ومن جوهر النور صدق كله، فلا يدرى بأي جوهرين ينطق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للآخر قدرة على النفع، فانقطع موضع الرجاء والخوف جميعا؛ فيذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه.
The evidences, if they knew them, would teach the incapacity of their intellects to comprehend human wisdom, let alone encompass the wisdom of lordship. Along with what they turned to in choice prevented them from claiming knowledge of the reality of wisdom and foolishness; since their doctrine is that they only see by the essence of evil except evil and by the essence of good except good. Then it is not known in what they call foolishness or wisdom whether it is an act of evil or an act of good. Every human, in their view, is mixed from the two [principles]; he sees with each one contrary to what he sees with the other. So perhaps he saw wisdom as foolishness and foolishness as wisdom. Then his statement is not trusted [for example]: 'It is good', for from the essence of darkness it is all falsehood, and from the essence of light it is all truth, so it is not known by which of the two essences he speaks. And there is no power except with Allah. Then since neither of them has power over harm, nor the other power over benefit, the place of hope and fear is cut off entirely; so the benefit of knowing wisdom and foolishness disappears.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological refutation of dualism (the belief in two eternal principles of good/light and evil/darkness). Maturidi argues that if reality is fundamentally dualist, human knowledge becomes impossible. He begins by noting that the dualists' own claims show the limits of human reason. Their doctrine holds that one can only perceive good through the essence of good and evil through the essence of evil. But since humans are a mixture of both essences, any judgment about whether an action is wise or foolish is unreliable: a person might mistake wisdom for foolishness and vice versa. Moreover, a statement like 'This is good' cannot be trusted because if it originates from the essence of darkness, it is entirely false, and if from the essence of light, entirely true—but we cannot know which essence is speaking. This leads to complete skepticism. Furthermore, since neither principle has power over the other for harm or benefit, the basis for hope and fear (from reward and punishment) is removed, rendering the very distinction between wisdom and foolishness useless. Thus, dualism self-destructs by making all knowledge and moral evaluation impossible.

Key Terms
جوهر الخير
جوهر الشر
حكمة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is endorsing skepticism about knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi is not skeptical; he is showing that dualism leads to skepticism, which is an absurd consequence, thus refuting dualism.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that a dualist's statement cannot be trusted?
A) Because the dualist always lies.
B) Because it is unclear which essence (good or evil) is producing the statement.
C) Because the dualist is ignorant of philosophy.
D) Because human reason cannot know anything.

Key Takeaway
Dualism self-destructs because it makes all human knowledge and moral evaluation impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your life have you made a judgment about something being 'good' or 'bad' without realizing that your own mixed motives might be blinding you? How does this passage challenge you to examine the basis of your moral certainty?
?
This passage shows that if we do not anchor our knowledge in a single, all-encompassing truth (tawheed), we risk losing all reliability. What area of your life—your beliefs, your decisions, your relationships—might be built on shaky foundations because you haven't submitted them to Allah's oneness?
?
Maturidi says that dualism cuts off 'the place of hope and fear.' If you ever find yourself feeling indifferent to the Hereafter, could it be that you are unconsciously living as if there are two sources of power in the universe? Repent and re-center your hope and fear on Allah alone.

Nature and the Impossibility of Wisdom
Maturidi argues that if essences act by nature, wisdom is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that moral categories like wisdom require free choice and cannot be attributed to essences acting by nature.

Core Idea
Wisdom requires choice; nature cannot be wise or foolish.

Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that the two principles (light and darkness) act by nature. He defines wisdom and foolishness as acts of choice, then shows that if light and darkness acted by nature, they would lack the knowledge necessary to avoid evil. This leads to a logical contradiction: the supposed good essence becomes mixed with evil, and eventually evil overcomes good. Let's trace this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: if 'then' statements, definitions, and a scenario involving light and darkness.
Pay attention to the definitions of wisdom and foolishness.
2
Understand the definitions
Maturidi defines wisdom as 'placing everything in its proper place' and foolishness as 'placing everything not in its place.' Note that these are actions of choice, not nature.
This is the key premise for the later argument.
3
Trace the critique of dualist naturalism
Maturidi shows that if light does not know foolishness, it cannot avoid it; similarly, darkness does not know wisdom. Thus, both are ignorant of moral categories, making them incapable of wise action.
The argument uses the dualists' own definitions of light and darkness.
4
Follow the consequence: mixture and evil's dominance
Because light contains both knowledge/ignorance and power/impotence, it cannot prevent darkness from harming it. Thus the 'essence of good' becomes mixed with evil, and evil overpowers good. Maturidi then questions how a being generated from such a contaminated source can know good from evil.
This is the reductio ad absurdum of the dualist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع، فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا محال والسفه بالطبع. والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه والسفه وضع كل شيء في غير موضعه. ومحال وصف ذي طبع به، إذ هو اختيار. والنور عندهم لا يعلم ما السفه فيحذره، ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة والجهل بمائية الشيء وبالوضع له شر؛ فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل، ثم القدرة والعجز، بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه عن ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوبا بالشر، وجوهر خير فيه، فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير. والذي هو خير لم يعرف الشر والسفه، فكيف يعرف هذا الذي يُولد عن جوهر الخير، بعد غلبة الشر عليه، الخير والشر؟
Then since each of the two essences acts by nature, the occurrence of knowledge of wisdom is impossible, and foolishness is by nature. And wisdom is placing everything in its place, and foolishness is placing everything not in its place. It is impossible to describe one with nature by it, because it is choice. And light in their view does not know what foolishness is that it might avoid it, nor does darkness know wisdom; and ignorance of the quiddity of a thing and of its placement is evil. So the essence of light in their view becomes that in which knowledge and ignorance, then power and incapacity, gathered, such that it cannot repel foolishness from nor prevent darkness from harming it. So the essence of good in their view becomes mixed with evil, and an essence has good in it; so it follows from their statement that evil overcomes good. And that which is good did not know evil and foolishness, so how can this [being] that is generated from the essence of good, after evil overcomes it, know good and evil?
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that the two principles (light and darkness) act by nature. Maturidi defines wisdom as 'placing everything in its proper place' and foolishness as its opposite. Since these are acts of choice, they cannot be attributed to a nature that acts necessarily. Moreover, if light does not know foolishness, it cannot avoid it; similarly, darkness does not know wisdom. Thus, the light essence would be a mix of knowledge and ignorance, power and impotence—unable to prevent darkness from harming it. Consequently, the supposed good principle becomes morally contaminated, and evil overcomes good. Maturidi then asks: if the source (light) did not know evil, how can a being generated from light after evil overcame it know good and evil? This argument shows that dualist naturalism eliminates the very conditions for moral knowledge and action.

Key Terms
طبع
اختيار
حكمة
جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is denying natural causality altogether.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying natural causality; he is arguing that moral categories like wisdom require free choice, which cannot exist in a purely natural, necessary system. Natural causality can exist, but it cannot ground moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say it is impossible for something acting by nature to be described as wise?
A) Because nature never errs.
B) Because wisdom requires choice, but nature necessitates fixed outcomes.
C) Because wisdom is a natural attribute.
D) Because nature is always foolish.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that moral categories such as wisdom and foolishness require free choice, and thus cannot be applied to beings that act by nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you act more by 'nature'—habit, impulse, or compulsion—or by genuine 'choice' based on wisdom? When was the last time you deliberately placed something (a word, a deed, a priority) in its proper place, and when did you fail?
?
Maturidi says light does not know foolishness and thus cannot avoid it. What foolishness are you unaware of in yourself? What 'darkness' in your soul are you ignorant of, and what would it take to see it before it harms you?
?
If the source of good in your life is contaminated with evil because of ignorance, how can you claim to know good and evil? Are you willing to confront the possibility that your own moral judgments are compromised by a deeper ignorance of your own nature? What step will you take this week to seek true wisdom from the only source that knows both good and evil without mixture?

Nature Implies a Single Coercer
Maturidi argues from natural compulsion to a single coercer and refutes dualism via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dual argument that natural compulsion necessitates a single omnipotent agent and his dilemma refuting dualist claims of two independent creators.

Core Idea
Nature's compulsion reveals a single all-powerful Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument from the very nature of things. He observes that everything with a fixed nature is compelled and cannot change itself, pointing to a single determiner. He then challenges dualists by showing that positing two independent powerful principles leads to contradiction. This lesson unpacks that reasoning, guiding you through the proof and the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall flow, then read the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the argument from nature and the dilemma for dualists.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the first argument
Maturidi begins by stating that everything with a nature is compelled (majbur). Why? Because it cannot change its own necessitated effects. This implies the existence of a coercer (qahir) who assigns natures.
The analogy of heating and cooling shows opposites come from one source.
3
Understand the dualist dilemma
Maturidi then presents a dilemma for those who claim two independent creators. Each must either know how to prevent the other and be able, or not. If not, the light principle is ignorant and powerless, invalidating the reason for positing two. If yes but does not act, it becomes evil for allowing harm.
Both horns destroy the dualist position.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how both parts together affirm tawhid. The first part shows the necessity of a single determiner; the second shows the impossibility of two independent agents.
This is a classic Maturidi proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن كل ذي طبع مقهور، إذ لا يملك صرف ما يوجبه الطبع وإيجاب الخلاف، وفي ذلك إيجاب قاهر يجعل ذا شراً بالطبع وهذا خيرًا. ولو ردّ ذا إلى اثنين كان فيهما ما في هذين؛ نحو التسخين والتبريد، إنه يكون بمن جعله كذلك. وفي ذلك إيجاب القول بالواحد. ومن يقول بأن كل واحد منهما خالق قادر، فإنه لا يخلو كل واحد منهما من أن يعلم الوجه الذي يمنع الآخر عن عمله ويقدر أو لا يعلم ولا يقدر عليه. فإن لم يعلم ولم يقدر اجتمع في النور الجهل والعجز، وفي ذلك بطلان السبب الذي له قالوا باثنين؛ وإن علم وقدر ثم لم يعمل في المنع لحقه وصف الشر.
Moreover, everything with a nature is compelled, as it does not have the power to divert what nature necessitates and to necessitate the opposite. And in that is the requiring of an overpowerer who makes this one evil by nature and that good. And if this were referred to two, there would be in them what is in these two; like heating and cooling, it is from the one who made it so. And in that is the requiring of the statement of the One. And one who says that each of them is a creator powerful, then each of them cannot be free from either knowing the way that prevents the other from its action and being able [to prevent], or not knowing and not being able. If he does not know and is not able, then ignorance and incapacity join in the light, and in that is the invalidation of the reason for which they said two; and if he knows and is able and then does not act to prevent, the description of evil attaches to him.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage contains two linked arguments. First, Maturidi argues that everything with a nature is compelled (majbur) because it cannot change its own nature. This implies the existence of a coercer (qahir) who assigns different natures to different things. If one posits two such coercers, the same reasoning applies: they themselves would be subject to some higher source. The analogy of heating and cooling shows that such opposites come from a single agent. Thus, one must affirm the One (al-wahid). Second, Maturidi presents a dilemma for dualists who say both principles are creators and powerful. Each must either know how to prevent the other and be able to do so, or not. If it does not know and cannot, then the light principle is ignorant and impotent, which negates the reason for positing two (since it is incomplete). If it knows and can but does not act, then it becomes evil for allowing harm. Either way, the dualist position fails.

Key Terms
طبع
قهور
قاهر
واحد
جهل
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is arguing that all natural things are directly compelled by God without any secondary causes.
Correction: Maturidi is using the dualists' own assumptions about nature to show that nature implies a single necessary being. He is not denying secondary causality; rather, he is demonstrating that the existence of fixed natural properties requires a single determiner.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma Maturidi poses for the dualist claim that each principle is a powerful creator?
A) Either they are both good, or both evil.
B) Either each knows how to prevent the other and does so, or it does not.
C) Either they are both eternal, or both created.
D) Either they act by nature or by choice.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the very existence of natural compulsion and the impossibility of two independent powerful agents require belief in a single, all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own nature—your habits, desires, and tendencies—do you truly feel that you are compelled? How does this realization affect your reliance on something beyond yourself to change what you cannot change on your own?
?
Maturidi argues that a creator who can prevent harm but does not act becomes evil. In your daily life, when you have the ability to stop a wrong or speak truth but remain silent, what does that reveal about your own character? Will you commit today to act when you can prevent harm?
?
The passage shows that any system of multiple independent creators collapses into absurdity. How does this challenge your own heart's tendency to treat created things—like money, status, or relationships—as independent sources of control in your life? Will you repent and acknowledge the sole sovereignty of the one true Creator?

The Moral Dilemma of Light's Enmity
Maturidi argues that light's relation to darkness entails evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dilemma argument against dualism and how he defends his position against the objection from admixture of defects.

Core Idea
Both loving and hating the enemy are evil — the dualist cannot escape this dilemma.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid tackles dualism with a clever moral dilemma. It assumes the dualists believe in a good light principle and an evil darkness principle. Maturidi asks: what is the light's attitude towards darkness? Two possibilities lead to the same conclusion — evil. The dualist may object, but Maturidi's response turns the objection back on itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the flow of the argument before analyzing details.
The passage is short but dense. Read carefully.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that Maturidi presents two options for light's relation to darkness: either it does not oppose it (and loves it) or it opposes it (and hates it). Both are claimed to be evil.
The first horn: no enmity → love → evil. Second horn: enmity → hatred → evil.
3
Understand the dualist's objection
The dualist might say that the second horn (enmity/hatred) is only evil in the witnessed world because of defects, not in the divine realm. Maturidi responds that the same logic would apply to all his criticisms of wisdom in creation.
This objection and response is the heart of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why Maturidi's argument is effective: it forces the dualist into a contradiction no matter what relationship is chosen.
This demonstrates the use of dilemma in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخلو النور من أن يعادي الظلمة أو لا، ويحب تشاغلها أو لا. فإن كان لا يعادي ويحب فذلك شر، لأن ترك عداوة العدو والمحبة له شر. وإن كان يعاديه ويبغضه فالعداوة والبغض في المعروف من الشاهد شر. فإن قال: ذلك في الشاهد لشوب الآفات، فمثله في جميع ما أنكر من الحكمة في خلق النوعين. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The light cannot be free from either enmity towards darkness or not, and loves its occupation or not. If it does not show enmity and loves, that is evil, because leaving enmity of the enemy and loving him is evil. And if it shows enmity and hates it, then enmity and hatred in what is known in the witnessed world are evil. If he says: That is in the witnessed [world] due to admixture of defects, then the same applies to all that he denied of wisdom in creating the two types. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a moral argument against dualism. The light principle, if it is entirely good, must have a definite relationship to the darkness principle. Maturidi considers two possibilities: either light does not oppose darkness and even loves it—this would be evil because one should oppose an enemy. Or light opposes darkness and hates it—but in the witnessed world, enmity and hatred are considered evil. The dualist might object that such moral judgments in the witnessed world are tainted by the admixture of darkness. But Maturidi retorts that the same admixture would apply to any moral evaluation, including the dualist's own denial of wisdom in creation. Thus, the dualist cannot consistently use this objection without undermining his own position.

Key Terms
عداوة
محبة
شاهد
آفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is claiming that God has hatred or enmity towards darkness.
Correction: Maturidi is not speaking about God but about the dualist's light principle. He is showing the moral untenability of the dualist's position using a dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma regarding light's attitude towards darkness?
A) Light shows enmity and hatred, which is evil.
B) Light does not show enmity and loves, which is evil.
C) Light is indifferent, which is evil.
D) Both A and B are presented as problematic.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses a dilemma to show that the dualist's light principle is morally self-contradictory, and the objection from 'admixture of defects' does not rescue it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider someone you view as an enemy, does your attitude toward them ever mirror the dilemma Maturidi describes—either loving them (which feels wrong) or hating them (which also feels wrong)? How does this lesson force you to reexamine the consistency of your own moral judgments about others?
?
Maturidi shows that the dualist's 'light' cannot escape being evil in either scenario. What does this tell you about your own attempts to separate 'pure goodness' from the messy reality of your life? Where do you compartmentalize your actions or beliefs to avoid facing contradictions, and what would it cost you to integrate them?
?
The dualist objects that moral judgments in the witnessed world are tainted by defects, but Maturidi turns the argument back on him. Is there a belief you hold that you excuse as 'only applying in this imperfect world'? What if that same excuse undermines the very foundation of that belief?

The Impossibility of Explaining Human Change with Two Principles
Maturidi enumerates observable changes and shows each attribution to dualist principles fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the fact of human change from ignorance to knowledge, and from sin to regret, to undermine the dualist theory of two eternal principles.

Core Idea
Observed change shows that two principles cannot be the source of all good and evil.

In this passage, Maturidi begins by listing several undeniable examples of change in the witnessed world: a person becomes knowledgeable after being ignorant, does good after doing harm, feels regret after sin, and corrects false beliefs. These changes are real and observable. They pose a problem for dualists who claim that light is the source of all good and darkness the source of all evil. Maturidi then considers three ways the dualist might try to attribute these changes to the two principles, showing each way leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of changes
Identify the specific examples Maturidi gives (ignorance→knowledge, harm→beneficence, sin→regret, etc.). Notice they are opposites.
2
Consider the three attributions
Maturidi presents three options: (1) all changes from light, (2) negative from darkness and positive from light, (3) all from darkness. For each, understand the problem that arises.
3
Why these refute dualism
Each attribution leads to a logical impossibility: either light is source of evil, or light is tainted by association with evil, or darkness becomes a source of good.
This shows the dualist cannot consistently assign moral opposites to two separate principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد، وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة، وبالنَّدَم بعد ذنب، وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل، وكذلك باعتقاد شيء حقا بعد أن اعتقده باطلاً، للوجود في الشاهد. فإما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء، فيبطل قوله بالاثنين لهذا الوجه؛ أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظلمة، والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور، فيكون ذلك كذبًا وتحزبا واهتماما؛ وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة، فقد أثبته للنور؛ ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه. وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر. وأيضا إن النور لا يخلو من أن يهتم للشر الذي يَحِلّ بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا. فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله: هو كله لذة وسرور، وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله في فعل الشر والضرر: «إنه القسوة والشدة، لا الرحمة». وذلك معناه في القول باثنين. ثم يقال له: هل يريد النور التحرك بعد السكون أو لا، ويريد شيئًا ثم يبدو له، ويحب أمرًا ثم يبغضه؟ ونكلم في هذا بمثل الذي ذكرت في الفصل الأول. والله الموفق. فإن زعمت الثنوية في جميع ما عارضنا من اختلاف الأحوال وتضادّها: أن ذلك كذلك في الشاهد لشوائب الآفات من الظلمة في جوهر النور فيرى الشيء بغير صورته، وبها يقع التواتر للعلم بالأشياء
Moreover, it is necessary to acknowledge knowledge after ignorance in the witnessed [world], and beneficence after harm, and regret after sin, and acknowledgment of harm after reason, and likewise believing something true after believing it false, due to existence in the witnessed [world]. So either we make both matters from light, then ignorance, harm, sin, foolishness and everything come from it, so his statement of two is invalidated for this reason; or we make harm, foolishness, ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgment, beneficence, regret from light, then that would be falsehood, partisanship, and concern; and all that in his view is from the act of darkness, so he has affirmed it for light; then acknowledgment of what was not is falsehood and foolishness. Or they are from darkness, so good and evil come from it. Also, light cannot be free from either being concerned for the evil that befalls its friends and grieving over it or not. If it is concerned and grieves, his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalidated; if it does not grieve, his statement regarding the act of evil and harm: 'It is harshness and severity, not mercy' is invalidated. And that is the meaning of the statement of two. Then it is said to him: Does light want movement after rest or not, and does it want something then it appears otherwise to him, and does it love a matter then hate it? And we speak about this by the like of what I mentioned in the first section. And Allah is the Guide. If the dualists claim in all that we have objected to of differing states and their contradiction: that that is so in the witnessed [world] due to admixtures of defects from darkness in the essence of light, so that the thing is seen in other than its form, and by it occurs tawatur for knowledge of things
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation using observed human change. Maturidi notes that in the witnessed world, people change from ignorance to knowledge, from harming to beneficence, from sin to regret, and from belief in falsehood to truth. These changes involve opposite states. If all such changes come from the light principle, then light would be the source of evil, negating dualism. If the negative states come from darkness and the positive from light, then the positive states (like regret) would be attributed to light, but regret implies prior sin, which is from darkness; thus light would be associated with sin indirectly. Moreover, claiming that the positive acknowledgment (e.g., 'I was wrong') comes from light is false because the acknowledgment pertains to a past reality that was not; thus it is deceitful. If both come from darkness, then darkness produces good and evil, so it is not purely evil. Maturidi then considers the emotional response of light to evil affecting its friends. If light grieves, it contradicts its nature of pure joy; if it does not grieve, it lacks mercy. Finally, he questions whether light can change its will (wanting movement after rest, changing decisions). Such changes would imply imperfection. The dualist might object that all these contradictions in the witnessed world are due to admixture of darkness, but Maturidi implies that this objection fails because it undermines the basis of knowledge (tawatur). The passage powerfully demonstrates that the observed world of change and moral progress requires a single, transcendent agent, not two opposed principles.

Key Terms
شاهد
ندم
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that observable moral change can be explained by two separate principles, one good and one evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that no matter how you assign the changes to the two principles, you end up with contradictions—either the good principle causes evil, or it is implicated in evil, or the evil principle causes good. Thus dualism fails to account for a unified moral order.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with attributing the change from ignorance to knowledge entirely to the light principle?
A) Light cannot change.
B) It would make light the source of ignorance as well since ignorance preceded knowledge.
C) Knowledge is not a good thing.
D) Ignorance is also from light.

Key Takeaway
Observing real human change from bad to good states shows that a single source must be responsible for both the initial state and the change, which defeats the dualist separation of good and evil principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a change from ignorance to knowledge about your faith, do you attribute that change to Allah alone, or do you think it came from your own effort or another source? How does the dualist's failure to explain such change challenge your own tendency to attribute good and bad to different sources in your life?
?
Consider a time you felt regret after a sin. According to Maturidi, that regret (a good state) implies that the prior sin and the regret come from the same source. Do you truly believe that both your sin and your repentance are from Allah? How does that belief affect your view of your own failures and your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
The dualist might say that good and evil come from different principles. In your daily life, do you sometimes act as if good deeds are from you and bad deeds are from Satan or circumstance, missing the fact that all is from Allah? How can this lesson change your perspective on your own moral struggles?

Emotional and Volitional Inconsistencies in Dualism
Maturidi poses dilemmas about light's emotions and will, then presents the dualist's admixture objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi further refutes dualism by examining whether the light principle can experience concern or grief over evil, and whether it can change its will, exposing internal contradictions.

Core Idea
A perfect principle cannot both be all-joy and yet concerned about evil, nor can it change its mind.

Having shown the failure of attributing observed changes to two principles, Maturidi turns to the emotional and volitional implications. He asks: does the light principle feel concern or grief when evil befalls its friends? If it does, then it cannot be pure joy and pleasure. If it does not, then it lacks mercy, contradicting its claimed attributes. Similarly, he questions whether light can change its will—desiring movement after rest, or loving something then hating it. Such changes imply imperfection, which contradicts the dualist's assertion that light is perfect and unchanging. Finally, Maturidi anticipates the dualist's objection that these apparent contradictions in the witnessed world are due to admixture of darkness, but he implies this defense fails because it undermines the reliability of empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the emotional dilemma
Maturidi forces a choice: either light cares about evil afflicting its friends (and thus feels grief, contradicting its nature of pure joy) or it does not (and thus lacks mercy, contradicting its nature as source of mercy).
This is a classic refutation by dichotomy.
2
Examine the volitional dilemma
If light can change its will (wanting movement after rest, etc.), then it is subject to change, which is imperfection. If it cannot, then it is rigid and cannot respond to events.
Maturidi says this is similar to the earlier argument.
3
Consider the dualist's escape attempt
The dualist might claim that all these contradictions arise because of admixture of darkness with light in the witnessed world. Maturidi implies this undermines the basis of reliable knowledge (tawatur).

Source Text

?

The Dilemma of Light and Darkness
Imam Maturidi presents a dilemma about light's relation to darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses a rational dilemma to refute the dualist claim that light and darkness are two eternal principles.

Core Idea
A rational dilemma shows that dualism's two principles cannot both be good.

Imam Maturidi here confronts the dualist belief in two eternal principles, light and darkness. He constructs a dilemma that forces the dualist into a contradiction: either light opposes darkness or does not, and both outcomes lead to evil. This lesson unpacks that argument and its refutation of an anticipated objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: two alternatives, each leading to a problematic conclusion.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the conditional phrases.
2
Identify the two horns
The dilemma presents two possibilities: light either opposes darkness or it does not. Why is each considered evil? Trace the reasoning for each.
3
Examine the dualist's objection
The dualist might respond that this reasoning applies only in the defective witnessed world. Note how the author counters this by extending the logic back to the original claim.
This shows the argument is not dependent on the analogy being perfect.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why this dilemma undermines the claim that light is a purely good principle. What does it imply about the coherence of dualism?
The key point is that both options lead to evil, so light cannot be essentially good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخلو النور من أن يعادي الظلمة أو لا، ويجب تشاغلها أو لا . فإن كان لا يعادي ويحب فذلك شر، لأن ترك عداوة العدو والمحبة له شر. وإن كان يعاديه ويبغضه فالعداوة والبغض في المعروف من الشاهد شر. فإن قال : ذلك في الشاهد لشوب الآفات، فمثله في جميع ما أنكر من الحكمة في خلق النوعين. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Light cannot be free from either opposing darkness or not, and either occupying itself with it or not. If it does not oppose and loves it, that is evil, because abandoning enmity of the enemy and loving him is evil. And if it opposes and hates it, then enmity and hatred in what is known in the witnessed world is evil. If he says: That is in the witnessed world due to the admixture of defects, then the same applies to everything you deny of wisdom in the creation of the two kinds. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma argument against the Dualist claim that light and darkness are two eternal principles, one good and one evil. Imam Maturidi argues that light must either oppose darkness or not. If it does not oppose and loves darkness, then that is evil because loving an enemy is evil. If it opposes and hates it, then that opposition and hatred in the witnessed world is also considered evil. The Dualist might respond that this is only in the witnessed world due to defects (shu'ub al-afat). The author counters that the same objection applies to all their denial of wisdom in creating two kinds. Thus, the dilemma shows the incoherence of the Dualist position. The key assumption is that in the witnessed world, both scenarios lead to evil, so the principle of light cannot be purely good.

Key Terms
النور (light)
الظلمة (darkness)
الشاهد (witnessed world)
شوب الآفات (admixture of defects)
العداوة (enmity)
البغض (hatred)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on assuming the witnessed world is a perfect analogy for the divine realm.
Correction: The author anticipates that objection and neutralizes it by showing the same logic applies to the dualist's own denial of wisdom, so the analogy is irrelevant.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma about light's relation to darkness?
A) Light must either oppose darkness or not oppose it.
B) Light must either be eternal or created.
C) Light must be good or evil.
D) Light must be self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that the dualist claim of two eternal principles cannot account for moral goodness without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your relationships, do you tend to either passively accept wrongdoing (loving darkness) or aggressively oppose it with hatred? Both are evil from a dualist perspective. How will you seek a tawhid-based balance that combines truth with mercy?
?
If you believe that everything is from Allah, then why do you sometimes react to evil as if it has independent power? This lesson shows that no second principle can exist. Will you commit to seeing every trial as from Allah Alone and respond accordingly?

The Witnessed World and the Three Options for Dualism
Maturidi enumerates three impossible options for Dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses empirical observations of change to argue against Dualism by showing that all three possible assignments of good and evil to light or darkness lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Change in the world proves that light and darkness cannot be entirely separate sources.

Imam Maturidi begins by noting that in our world we see transitions: from ignorance to knowledge, from harm to beneficence, from sin to regret. These opposite states must have a source. The Dualist claims light is wholly good and darkness wholly evil. But if we try to assign these transitions to their sources, we run into contradictions, as the passage shows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the empirical observations at the beginning.
2
Identify the three options
The passage presents three ways to assign good and evil to light and darkness. Find each one and note the contradiction it leads to.
Option 1: both from light → light produces evil. Option 2: some from light, some from darkness → light attributed with falsehood. Option 3: both from darkness → darkness produces good.
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why each option is impossible. This shows the flaw in Dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد، وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة، وبالنَّدَم بعد ذنب، وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل، وكذلك باعتقاد شيء حقا بعد أن اعتقده باطلاً، للوجود في الشاهد . فإما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء، فيبطل قوله بالاثنين لهذا الوجه؛ أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظمة، والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور، فيكون ذلك كذبًا وتحزبا واهتماما"؛ وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة، فقد أثبته للنور؛ ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه. وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر. وأيضا إن النور لا يخلو من أن يهتم للشر [الذي ] يَحِلّ بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا . فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور ، وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله في فعل الشر والضرر: «إنه القسوة والشدة، لا الرحمة». وذلك معناه في القول باثنين . ثم " يقال له : [ هل يريد النور] التحرك بعد السكون أو لا، ويريد شيئًا ثم يبدو له "، ويحب أمرًا ثم يبغضه؟ ونكلم في هذا بمثل الذي ذكرت في الفصل الأول. والله الموفق.
Moreover, it is necessary to acknowledge knowledge after ignorance in the witnessed world, and beneficence after harm, and regret after sin, and acknowledgement of harm after reason, and likewise believing something as true after believing it false, due to their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both matters from light, in which case ignorance, harm, sin, folly, and everything come from it, thus his statement of dualism becomes invalid in this respect; or we make harm, folly, and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgement, beneficence, and regret from light, but that would be falsehood, partisanship, and concern; and all that according to him is from the act of darkness, so he has attributed it to light; then acknowledgement of what was not is falsehood and folly. Or both may be from darkness, in which case good and evil come from it. Also, light cannot be free from either being concerned about the evil that befalls its friends and grieving over it or not. If it is concerned and grieves, then his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalid; and if it does not grieve, then his statement about doing evil and harm—'it is harshness and severity, not mercy'—is invalid. And that is the meaning of the statement of dualism. Then it is said to him: Does light intend movement after stillness or not, and does it intend something then it appears to him, and loves something then hates it? And we discuss this in the same way as mentioned in the first chapter. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a further argument against Dualism. Imam Maturidi observes that in the witnessed world, we see transitions from ignorance to knowledge, from harm to beneficence, from sin to regret, etc. These opposite states exist. The Dualist must assign them to either light or darkness. If both come from light, then light is the source of evil, contradicting its pure goodness. If some come from light and some from darkness, then light would be attributed with falsehood and partisanship, which is inconsistent. If both come from darkness, then darkness produces good as well. The author also argues that light must either care about evil befalling its friends or not; either way contradicts the Dualist's description of light as pure joy or pure harshness. The author then questions whether light can intend movement after stillness, etc., using similar reasoning as before. The argument systematically shows the impossibility of attributing all good to light and all evil to darkness given the observed intermingling of states.

Key Terms
العلم بعد الجهل (knowledge after ignorance)
الإحسان بعد الإساءة (beneficence after harm)
الندم بعد ذنب (regret after sin)
التحزب (partisanship)
الاهتمام (concern)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Dualist can simply choose one of the options without contradiction.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that each option contradicts the Dualist's own premises about light and darkness.

Checkpoint
What happens if both knowledge and ignorance are said to come from light?
A) Light would be source of evil as well.
B) Light would be purely good.
C) Darkness would be source of good.
D) Dualism would be confirmed.

Key Takeaway
The observed transitions in the world force the Dualist into impossible choices, undermining the claim that light and darkness are separate sources of good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden shift from ignorance to understanding—whether in faith or in daily life—do you recognize that only Allah, the One Creator, brings about that change? Or do you subtly attribute it to your own effort or mere chance? This lesson shows that such transitions cannot be explained by two rival powers. Will you train yourself to see every change in your soul as a sign of tawheed and thank Allah for it?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that some good in your life came from your own doing, while some evil came from external circumstances or other people? The argument here forces you to see that attributing good and evil to separate sources is illogical. Will you repent of any hidden dualism in your heart and affirm that every good and every trial is from Allah alone?
?
The passage proves that light cannot be entirely good if it produces ignorance, and darkness cannot be entirely evil if it produces good. This means the ultimate source of everything must be one God, who alone is perfect. How does this truth challenge the way you react to evil in the world? Will you submit to His wisdom even when you do not understand?

Does Light Care? The Dilemma of Concern and Intent
Maturidi presents a dilemma and further questioning to refute Dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses a dilemma about light's attitude toward evil and its ability to intend change to further dismantle Dualism.

Core Idea
If light is unconcerned about evil, it contradicts its mercy; if concerned, it contradicts its joy.

Continuing his argument against Dualism, Imam Maturidi presents a dilemma: if light is concerned about evil befalling its friends, it would not be all pleasure and joy; if it is not concerned, then its description as harsh and severe is undermined. He then questions whether light can intend movement after stillness, using similar reasoning as before.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the dilemma about light being concerned or not.
2
Analyze the contradiction
See how each horn of the dilemma contradicts a Dualist claim about light.
3
Consider the further questioning
The author then asks whether light can intend movement after stillness, etc. Understand how these questions follow the same logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد، وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة، وبالنَّدَم بعد ذنب، وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل، وكذلك باعتقاد شيء حقا بعد أن اعتقده باطلاً، للوجود في الشاهد . فإما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء، فيبطل قوله بالاثنين لهذا الوجه؛ أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظمة، والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور، فيكون ذلك كذبًا وتحزبا واهتماما"؛ وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة، فقد أثبته للنور؛ ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه. وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر. وأيضا إن النور لا يخلو من أن يهتم للشر [الذي ] يَحِلّ بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا . فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور ، وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله في فعل الشر والضرر: «إنه القسوة والشدة، لا الرحمة». وذلك معناه في القول باثنين . ثم " يقال له : [ هل يريد النور] التحرك بعد السكون أو لا، ويريد شيئًا ثم يبدو له "، ويحب أمرًا ثم يبغضه؟ ونكلم في هذا بمثل الذي ذكرت في الفصل الأول. والله الموفق.
Moreover, it is necessary to acknowledge knowledge after ignorance in the witnessed world, and beneficence after harm, and regret after sin, and acknowledgement of harm after reason, and likewise believing something as true after believing it false, due to their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both matters from light, in which case ignorance, harm, sin, folly, and everything come from it, thus his statement of dualism becomes invalid in this respect; or we make harm, folly, and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgement, beneficence, and regret from light, but that would be falsehood, partisanship, and concern; and all that according to him is from the act of darkness, so he has attributed it to light; then acknowledgement of what was not is falsehood and folly. Or both may be from darkness, in which case good and evil come from it. Also, light cannot be free from either being concerned about the evil that befalls its friends and grieving over it or not. If it is concerned and grieves, then his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalid; and if it does not grieve, then his statement about doing evil and harm—'it is harshness and severity, not mercy'—is invalid. And that is the meaning of the statement of dualism. Then it is said to him: Does light intend movement after stillness or not, and does it intend something then it appears to him, and loves something then hates it? And we discuss this in the same way as mentioned in the first chapter. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a further argument against Dualism. Imam Maturidi observes that in the witnessed world, we see transitions from ignorance to knowledge, from harm to beneficence, from sin to regret, etc. These opposite states exist. The Dualist must assign them to either light or darkness. If both come from light, then light is the source of evil, contradicting its pure goodness. If some come from light and some from darkness, then light would be attributed with falsehood and partisanship, which is inconsistent. If both come from darkness, then darkness produces good as well. The author also argues that light must either care about evil befalling its friends or not; either way contradicts the Dualist's description of light as pure joy or pure harshness. The author then questions whether light can intend movement after stillness, etc., using similar reasoning as before. The argument systematically shows the impossibility of attributing all good to light and all evil to darkness given the observed intermingling of states.

Key Terms
الاهتمام (concern)
التحزب (partisanship)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Dualist can say light is both joyful and harsh in different contexts.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that the same light cannot be both all-joyful and harsh-severe if it is concerned or not concerned about evil, because each attribute excludes the other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if light is concerned about evil befalling its friends, what does that invalidate?
A) Light is all pleasure and joy.
B) Light is harsh and severe.
C) Light is merciful.
D) Light is all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma about light's concern for evil shows that the Dualist cannot consistently describe light's nature, and further questions about change deepen the contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or suffering in the world, do you ever find yourself attributing contradictory qualities to Allah—seeing Him as both merciful and harsh? How does this lesson challenge you to reconcile His attributes, and what will you do the next time such a thought arises?
?
The Dualists claimed light is all joy, yet also the source of harshness. Have you ever described Allah in a way that contradicts His perfection? Reflect on a specific instance and commit to correcting your understanding today.
?
The argument asks whether light can change its mind. How does the belief that Allah is unchanging affect your trust in His promises? In what area of your life do you need to rely on His immutability this week?
