Classification of the Dahriyya into Three Groups
Maturidi classifies the Dahriyya into three groups regarding their views on separation and union of primordial elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main classifications of the Dahriyya according to Maturidi and understand the fourth additional view.

Core Idea
Maturidi maps the Materialists' errors into four distinct views about the origin of the world.

Imam Maturidi, in his refutation of the Materialists, first classifies their various doctrines. Understanding this classification is essential because it reveals the internal inconsistencies Maturidi will later critique. This passage presents the three groups (and an implied fourth) that arise from their conjectures about separation and union of primordial elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very condensed style.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage explicitly mentions three groups based on the order of separation and union. Write them down as you read.
3
Note the additional fourth view
After the three, Maturidi adds 'then there exists a statement of no separation or union'. Identify this fourth view and its proponents.
This fourth view is separate from the initial three.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Maturidi begins with this classification. Consider that by grouping these views, he is preparing to refute them by showing their contradictions.
This connects to the larger argument of the treatise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رجع اختلاف الدهرية إلى ثلاثة إلى تباين ثم الاجتماع وذلك قول الزنادقة والثنوية ومن يقول بالنور والظلمة وإلى اجتماع ثم التباين وذلك قول من يقول بالطينة والهيولى وإلى الجهل بهما على القول بالقدم ويشبه أن يكون هذا قول أصحاب الطبائع على أنه لم يظهر ذلك من قولهم ثم يوجد قول بعدم التفرق أو الاجتماع ويرون ما عليه العالم عليهما وعلى ذلك قول من يقول بقدم الأعيان مع حوادث لا أول لها
Then the disagreement of the Materialists returns to three: to separation then union, and that is the statement of the heretics, the Dualists, and those who say with light and darkness; and to union then separation, and that is the statement of those who say with clay and hyle; and to ignorance of them both upon the statement of pre-eternity, and it resembles that this is the statement of the proponents of natures, although it did not appear from their statement. Then there exists a statement of no separation or union, and they see that upon which the world is as being upon them both; and upon that is the statement of those who say with the pre-eternity of entities together with events that have no beginning.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage classifies the Materialists (Dahriyya) into three main groups based on their views on the primordial state of the world. The first group believes in an initial separation (tbayun) followed by union (ijtima'): this includes the heretics (zanadiqa), Dualists, and those who posit light and darkness as principles. The second group believes in initial union followed by separation: this includes those who posit clay (tina) and hyle (hayula) as principles. The third group claims ignorance of both separation and union while affirming pre-eternity, which seems to be the view of the proponents of natures (taba'i'), though it was not explicitly stated by them. Maturidi then adds a fourth view that denies both separation and union entirely, seeing the world's state as being upon both (i.e., the world is both separated and united eternally), which is the view of those who affirm the pre-eternity of entities along with beginningless events.

Key Terms
الدهرية (Materialists)
التباين (separation)
الاجتماع (union)
القدم (pre-eternity)
الهيولى (hyle)
الطينة (clay)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this is merely historical reporting, but Maturidi is setting up the refutation that follows by showing the internal inconsistencies of these views.
Correction: This classification is not just descriptive; it is a strategic preamble to refutation. Maturidi categorises these views to later demonstrate their logical flaws.

Checkpoint
How many main groups does Maturidi attribute to the Dahriyya in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's classification of the Dahriyya into four views on the primordial state sets the stage for their refutation by exposing their internal contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi meticulously classifies the views of those he disagrees with before refuting them. When you encounter a belief you consider false, do you first seek to understand its precise formulation, or do you rely on a caricature? How might your refutations become more effective if you adopted his method? Commit to one area where you will practice this.
?
The Materialists claimed pre-eternity for the world. What things in your life do you treat as if they have always existed or are unchangeable? Could there be a beginning to what you assume is permanent? Let this lesson press you to examine whether you are clinging to a false 'pre-eternity' in your own worldview.
?
Maturidi identifies a group that claims ignorance of the primordial state. Is there an area of theology or personal belief where you are content to remain ignorant rather than seeking knowledge? What would it cost you to pursue that knowledge? Decide today to take one step toward clarity.

The Three Possible Causes
Maturidi classifies three possible causes and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible explanations for why two things may be separate or united: nature, choice, or an external maker.

Core Idea
Only three explanations exist: nature, choice, or an external maker.

Maturidi begins by setting up a foundational framework for analyzing the coexistence of opposites. He states that any two distinct things (like light and darkness) must be in a state of separation or union, and only three causes can account for this: inherent nature, voluntary choice, or an external agent. This classification is the key to refuting dualist and materialist claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage that lays out the three possibilities. Notice how Maturidi states that separation and union cannot go beyond these three.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify each category: by nature (tab‘), by choice (ikhtiyār), or by another (ākhar). Reflect on what each implies about the entities involved.
These categories are exhaustive for rational analysis.
3
Notice the refutation of the first cause
Maturidi then refutes the first possibility (by nature) using the argument of intensification. Read the refutation and see why nature cannot explain a constant state.
The example of natural motion illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالاختيار أو بآخر يجعلها كذلك، وكذلك المجتمع منه . ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا . (أ) فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب أن يزداد من ذلك فيما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن يزداد منه . ألا يرى أن كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركة ، وكذا يستحق. وكذلك كل جوهر كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وموضعه فوق، ومن بطبعه يَسفُل فمحال لهما الاجتماع أبدا. وكذا هذه العبرة بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذي يتحرك إلى اليسار، وفي ذلك بطلان ما قالوا. (ب) وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار فالقول بأن كانا على غير ما عليهما فاسد؛ لأنه لا دليل على تثبت خلاف لما عليه الشاهد أن يكون الذي اختياره التباين يقع معه اجتماع، أو الذي اختياره الاجتماع يقع معه تباين فبطل الاختيار مع فساد قولهم من بقاء كل بجوهر الآخر واحتباسه. وتحقيق ذلك أن احتباس الخير في الشر شر. ولما لو كان لهما الاختيار لكان لا يخلو كل واحد منهما من القدرة على منع الآخر عن فعله واختيار ذلك والعلم بكيفية ذلك، فإن لم يكن بطل معنى الاختيار وتحقق فيهما جميعا العجز والجهل ، وإن كان ذلك كذلك بطل الاختلاف عما كانا عليه، لما به يصل كلّ إلى ما يؤذيه ، ويضره . وبعد، فإن في تحقيق ذلك تجهيل كل واحد منهما الآخر وتعجيزه، وفي ذلك فساد القول. ولا قوة إلا بالله . (ج) وإن كان ذلك بآخر ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين وهما لا يخلوان منه، فلزم حدثهما، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالتوحيد بما أريد به نفيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومن جهل الأمرين جميعا فقد أقر أن لا قول هناك تكلم عليه، وأنه ممن لا يحتمل عقله البلوغ إلى العلم به. وإنما طريقه التقليد، فأشكل عليه الأمر لاختلاف ما أدى إليه؛ فإنما تكلم من عنده وظن أن الذي أدّاه إليه حق يظهر عند ذلك الحق. ثم إذ محال اجتماع الأمرين من حيث ما بينا من التناقض ثبت أن الحق لو كان فيما يقول به أهل الدهر فهو في أحد ذينك القولين، وقد بينا فسادهما جميعا. وبالله المعونة.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The foundation in that is that the two (things) in a state of separation cannot go beyond either being that way by nature, or by choice, or by another that makes them so, and likewise the united from them. Then separation and mixture do not go beyond what we have mentioned. (A) If they are that way by nature, then one would necessarily increase in that, such that what originally had separation or union would increase from it. Do you not see that everything moving by nature increases in motion, and likewise it deserves. And likewise, every substance by its nature rises and its place is above, and that which by nature descends, it is impossible for them to ever unite. And likewise is the analogy between one moving from the right side and another moving to the left, and in that is the invalidation of what they said. (B) And if that is by choice, then the saying that they are other than what they are is corrupt; because there is no proof establishing contrary to what is observed: that the one whose choice is separation should have union occur with it, or the one whose choice is union should have separation occur with it. Thus choice is invalidated along with the corruption of their saying about the survival of each by the substance of the other and its retention. The verification of that is that the retention of good in evil is evil. Because if they had choice, each of them would not be devoid of the ability to prevent the other from its action and to choose that and knowledge of how to do so. If that is not the case, then the meaning of choice is invalidated and both are realized as impotent and ignorant. And if that is the case, then the difference from what they were upon would be invalidated, because each can reach what harms and injures it. Moreover, in the verification of that lies the rendering ignorant and powerless of each other, and in that is the corruption of the saying. And there is no power except with Allah. (C) And if that is by another, then the temporality of separation and union is established, and they are not free from it, so their temporality is necessary, and in that is the necessity of affirming tawhid (divine unity) by what was intended to negate it. And there is no power except with Allah. And whoever is ignorant of both matters has acknowledged that there is no doctrine to be spoken about, and that he is among those whose intellect cannot attain knowledge of it. His path is only taqlid (blind following), and the matter becomes problematic for him due to the divergence of what led to it; so he speaks from his own opinion and thinks that what led him is true, but the truth appears only with that truth. Then, since the combination of the two matters is impossible from the angle of the contradiction we have shown, it is established that if the truth were in what the adherents of time (ahl al-dahr) say, it would be in one of those two doctrines, and we have shown the corruption of both of them. And with Allah is the help.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of three possible causes for the state of two things (e.g., light and darkness) being either separate or united. Maturidi states that they must be that way either by nature (tab‘), by choice (ikhtiyār), or by an external factor (ākhar). He then refutes each possibility in turn. (A) If by nature, then the property would necessarily intensify over time—like natural motion increasing—but the observed state remains constant, so this is false. (B) If by choice, then each agent would have power to choose and prevent the other, leading to contradiction (since both would simultaneously choose opposite states) and mutual frustration; moreover, the ability to prevent implies knowledge and power, which if absent negates choice and implies ignorance and impotence. Thus the doctrine is corrupt. (C) If by an external cause, then the state (separation or union) is temporally produced (ḥādith), but the two things themselves must be temporally produced as well, leading to the affirmation of tawhid (God's oneness) rather than its negation. Maturidi concludes that one who ignores both doctrines has no valid position and resorts to taqlid. Since the two opposing doctrines are both absurd, the truth—if the materialists' view were correct—would have to lie in one of them, but both have been refuted, so the materialist position is invalid. The passage is a systematic refutation of dualism and an indirect proof of tawhid.

Key Terms
الطبع
الاختيار
آخر
التباين
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many possible causes are there for two things being separate or united?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Infinite

Key Takeaway
There are only three rational possibilities for why things are as they are: nature, choice, or an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the order in your own life—your habits, relationships, even your daily routine—do you see them as products of nature (automatic), choice (your own decisions), or an external Maker's design? Which of these three do you rely on most, and does that reliance align with what you claim to believe about Allah? If you are honest, do you sometimes live as if nature or chance explains everything, ignoring the Hand that sustains every moment? What would change if you deliberately attributed every detail of your day to that external Maker?
?
Maturidi shows that if the world were by nature, it would intensify and become extreme—but you experience stability and balance. Does that stability humble you? When was the last time you truly thanked the One who keeps the sun in its place, your heart beating, and your thoughts coherent? If you believe all this is held together by Allah’s will, why do you still worry as if the world runs on its own? What one worry will you hand over to Him right now, acknowledging that He is the only true Cause?
?
The argument forces you to choose between nature, choice, or an external Maker. There is no neutral ground. If you say 'nature' or 'chance', then there is no purpose, no accountability, and no Judge. Is that truly what you live by? Or do you sense a deeper Hand? If you truly believe in an external Maker, then how must you live? Are you living in obedience to that Maker, or are you ignoring the implications? What step will you take today to submit to the One who made you, not by nature, but by His choice?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
7 min
Refutation of Choice as a Cause
Maturidi refutes the explanation by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why choice cannot explain the coexistence of opposite states like separation and union.

Core Idea
Choice fails because it leads to contradiction and mutual frustration.

After refuting nature, Maturidi tackles the second possibility: that the state of things is due to choice. This would mean that each entity voluntarily chooses to be separate or united. But this leads to absurd consequences, as he shows. The argument is a powerful demonstration that voluntary agents cannot produce a coherent, stable order without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the choice refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار' and focus on Maturidi's logical dismantling of the choice hypothesis.
2
Identify the key objection
Notice that Maturidi says there is no proof that things could be other than they are. He argues that if choice were the cause, we would see contradictory effects (e.g., one choosing separation yet union occurring).
3
Understand the implications of ability
Maturidi argues that if each had choice, each must have the power to prevent the other. If that power is absent, then they are impotent and ignorant, which negates choice.
This shows that choice cannot coexist with mutual opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالاختيار أو بآخر يجعلها كذلك، وكذلك المجتمع منه . ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا . (أ) فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب أن يزداد من ذلك فيما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن يزداد منه . ألا يرى أن كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركة ، وكذا يستحق. وكذلك كل جوهر كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وموضعه فوق، ومن بطبعه يَسفُل فمحال لهما الاجتماع أبدا. وكذا هذه العبرة بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذي يتحرك إلى اليسار، وفي ذلك بطلان ما قالوا. (ب) وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار فالقول بأن كانا على غير ما عليهما فاسد؛ لأنه لا دليل على تثبت خلاف لما عليه الشاهد أن يكون الذي اختياره التباين يقع معه اجتماع، أو الذي اختياره الاجتماع يقع معه تباين فبطل الاختيار مع فساد قولهم من بقاء كل بجوهر الآخر واحتباسه. وتحقيق ذلك أن احتباس الخير في الشر شر. ولما لو كان لهما الاختيار لكان لا يخلو كل واحد منهما من القدرة على منع الآخر عن فعله واختيار ذلك والعلم بكيفية ذلك، فإن لم يكن بطل معنى الاختيار وتحقق فيهما جميعا العجز والجهل ، وإن كان ذلك كذلك بطل الاختلاف عما كانا عليه، لما به يصل كلّ إلى ما يؤذيه ، ويضره . وبعد، فإن في تحقيق ذلك تجهيل كل واحد منهما الآخر وتعجيزه، وفي ذلك فساد القول. ولا قوة إلا بالله . (ج) وإن كان ذلك بآخر ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين وهما لا يخلوان منه، فلزم حدثهما، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالتوحيد بما أريد به نفيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومن جهل الأمرين جميعا فقد أقر أن لا قول هناك تكلم عليه، وأنه ممن لا يحتمل عقله البلوغ إلى العلم به. وإنما طريقه التقليد، فأشكل عليه الأمر لاختلاف ما أدى إليه؛ فإنما تكلم من عنده وظن أن الذي أدّاه إليه حق يظهر عند ذلك الحق. ثم إذ محال اجتماع الأمرين من حيث ما بينا من التناقض ثبت أن الحق لو كان فيما يقول به أهل الدهر فهو في أحد ذينك القولين، وقد بينا فسادهما جميعا. وبالله المعونة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if both entities had choice, they could voluntarily agree to be separate or united.
Correction: Maturidi shows that choice entails the ability to prevent the other; that leads to conflict, not harmony.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is necessary for choice to be meaningful?
A) Ability to prevent the other and knowledge of how to do so
B) Independent existence
C) Eternal existence
D) Inertness

Key Takeaway
Choice as a cause fails because it necessarily leads to mutual opposition and contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe evil or conflict in the world, do you ever subconsciously attribute it to some independent force or choice, forgetting that nothing escapes Allah's power? How does this lesson expose the impossibility of any real cause besides Allah?
?
If choice as a cause is invalidated, then your own choices are not ultimately independent. Are you willing to truly internalize that your actions are under Allah's control? What would that mean for how you approach your next sin or your next good deed?
?
The passage says 'the retention of good in evil is evil.' When you struggle to see wisdom in difficult situations, do you trust that Allah's plan is good, or do you drift toward a dualistic mindset? How can you actively combat that drift today?

intermediate

Refutation of External Cause and Conclusion
Maturidi refutes external cause and concludes with tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how positing an external cause leads to the affirmation of tawhid and the invalidity of dualism.

Core Idea
If an external cause produces the state, then the things themselves are created, proving tawhid.

Maturidi now turns to the third and final possibility: that the state of separation or union is caused by an external agent. This line of reasoning, ironically, leads directly to the conclusion that the two things themselves are temporally produced (ḥādith), which necessitates a single Creator—the very tawhid the dualists deny. He then wraps up by dismissing the position of those who claim ignorance and reaffirming the logical collapse of all alternative explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the external cause argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وإن كان ذلك بآخر' and see how Maturidi uses it to prove that separation and union are contingent, implying the temporality of the things themselves.
2
Follow the conclusion to tawhid
Notice that he says the temporality of separation/union necessitates the temporality of the things, and this forces acceptance of tawhid—the very concept the dualists wanted to negate.
3
Understand the final dismissal
Maturidi dismisses those who claim ignorance of both doctrines; they resort to blind following. He concludes that since both alternative doctrines have been refuted, the truth must lie in tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالاختيار أو بآخر يجعلها كذلك، وكذلك المجتمع منه . ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا . (أ) فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب أن يزداد من ذلك فيما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن يزداد منه . ألا يرى أن كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركة ، وكذا يستحق. وكذلك كل جوهر كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وموضعه فوق، ومن بطبعه يَسفُل فمحال لهما الاجتماع أبدا. وكذا هذه العبرة بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذي يتحرك إلى اليسار، وفي ذلك بطلان ما قالوا. Maturidi concludes that one who ignores both doctrines has no valid position and resorts to taqlid. Since the two opposing doctrines are both absurd, the truth—if the materialists' view were correct—would have to lie in one of them, but both have been refuted, so the materialist position is invalid. The passage is a systematic refutation of dualism and an indirect proof of tawhid.

Key Terms
آخر
التوحيد
الحدث
التباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an external cause exists, that cause could be multiple gods.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the temporality of the things points to a single Creator, not multiple, because the argument for temporality leads to tawhid.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say necessarily follows from positing an external cause for separation/union?
A) The state is eternal
B) The things themselves are temporally produced
C) The cause is also temporally produced
D) Nothing follows

Key Takeaway
Positing an external cause leads to the necessity of tawhid, proving the dualists wrong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on blind following (taqlid) for your belief in God, or have you personally examined the arguments that lead to tawhid? Where in your faith are you most tempted to just 'go along' without understanding?
?
When you encounter contradictory worldviews, like those of atheists or dualists, do you honestly engage with their arguments or dismiss them without thought? Are there doubts you avoid because they feel uncomfortable?
?
The passage says ignorance about the fundamental truth is not a valid excuse. Is there any area of your life where you prefer ignorance over seeking certainty about your Creator? If so, what would it cost you to pursue the truth with sincerity?

Refutation by Natural Disposition
Maturidi refutes the natural disposition argument for dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the natural properties of the two principles contradict their coexistence, refuting dualism.

Core Idea
Opposite natures cannot coexist, proving dualism impossible.

Imam Maturidi begins his refutation by considering the first possibility: that the two eternal principles (e.g., light and darkness) interact according to their natural dispositions. If one naturally rises and the other sinks, they cannot remain together. This simple observation from the physical world undermines the dualist claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the examples of movement and spatial positions.
The argument uses everyday experiences.
2
Identify the natural properties
Identify the two opposite natures described: one rises, one sinks; one goes right, one left.
3
Understand the implication
Recognize that if they are by nature opposite, they cannot come together, yet dualism claims they do. Thus the dualist position is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يزداد منه. ألا يرى أن كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركه، وكذا يستحق. وكذلك كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وموضعه فوق، ومن بطبعه يَسفُل فمحال لهما الاجتماع أبدا. وكذا هذه العبرة بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذي يتحرك إلى اليسار، وفي ذلك بطلان ما قالوا. بـ) وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار فالقول بأن كانا على غير ما عليهما فاسد؛ لأنه لا دليل على تثبت خلاف لما عليه الشاهد أن يكون الذي اختياره التباين يقع معه اجتماع، أو الذي اختياره الاجتماع يقع معه تباين فبطل الاختيار مع فساد قولهم من بقاء كل بجوهر الآخر واحتباسه. وتحقيق ذلك أن احتباس الخير في الشر شر. ولما لو كان لهما الاختيار لكان لا يخلو كل واحد منهما من القدرة على منع الآخر عن فعله واختيار ذلك والعلم بكيفية ذلك، فإن لم يكن بطل معنى الاختيار وتحقق فيهما جميعا العجز والجهل، وإن كان ذلك كذلك بطل الاختلاف عما كانا عليه، لما به يصل كلّ إلى ما يؤذيه ويضره. وبعد، فإن في تحقيق ذلك تجهيل كل واحد منهما الآخر وتعجيزه، وفي ذلك فساد القول. ولا قوة إلا بالله. جـ) وإن كان ذلك بآخر ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين وهما لا يخلوان منه، فلزم حدثهما، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالتوحيد بما أريد به نفيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ومن جهل الأمرين جميعاً فقد أقر أن لا قول هناك تكلم عليه، وأنه ممن لا يحتمل عقله البلوغ إلى العلم به. وإنما طريقه التقليد، فأشكل عليه الأمر لاختلاف ما أدى إليه؛ فإنما تكلم من عنده وظن أن الذي أدّاه إليه حق يظهر عند ذلك الحق. ثم إذ محال اجتماع الأمرين من حيث ما بينا من التناقض ثبت أن الحق لو كان فيما يقول به أهل الدهر فهو في أحد ذينك القولين، وقد بينا فسادهما جميعا. وبالله المعونة.
It increases from it. Do you not see that every mover by nature increases by its movement, and likewise it deserves. And likewise every substance by nature rises and its place is above, and that which by nature sinks, so it is impossible for them to come together ever. And likewise this analogy between one who moves from the right side and one who moves to the left, and in that is the falsity of what they said. (b) And if that is by choice, then the statement that they are other than what they are is corrupt; because there is no evidence to establish a contrary to what is witnessed: that one whose choice is separation, conjunction occurs with him, or one whose choice is conjunction, separation occurs with him; so choice is invalid along with the corruption of their statement about the survival of each in the substance of the other and its retention. The verification of that is that the retention of good in evil is evil. And if they had choice, each of them would not be devoid of the ability to prevent the other from his action and to choose that and knowledge of how that is; if not, then the meaning of choice is invalidated and impotence and ignorance are realized in both of them; and if that is so, then the difference from what they were is invalidated, because by it each attains what harms him. After that, in the verification of that is the rendering ignorant of each of the other and the rendering impotent of him, and in that is the corruption of the statement. There is no power except with Allah. (c) And if that is by another, then the occurrence of separation and divergence is established, and they are not free from it, so their occurrence is necessitated, and in that is the necessity of the statement of tawhid (monotheism) by which its negation was intended. There is no power except with Allah. And whoever is ignorant of both matters has acknowledged that there is no statement there to be spoken about, and that he is among those whose intellect cannot reach knowledge of it. His way is only taqlid (blind following), so the matter became difficult for him due to the difference of what he was led to; he only spoke from himself and thought that what he was led to is truth that appears at that truth. Then, since the conjunction of the two matters is impossible from the point of what we have shown of contradiction, it is established that if truth were in what the materialists (ahl al-dahr) say, then it is in one of those two statements, and we have shown the corruption of both of them. And with Allah is the assistance.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist position (likely the belief in two eternal principles like light and darkness) by examining three possible ways in which these two principles could relate to each other: (a) by their natural dispositions, (b) by their own choice, or (c) by some third cause. The author shows that each leads to contradiction, thus proving the impossibility of dualism and the necessity of tawhid. The argument proceeds by first considering if the two principles are by nature: if one naturally rises and the other sinks, they cannot remain together, yet observation shows they do come together, so their nature cannot account for the observed changes. Second, if they act by choice, then each would have the power to prevent the other, leading to mutual frustration and the attribution of impotence and ignorance, which is absurd. Third, if their relationship is caused by something else, then they themselves are contingent, which implies they are created, not eternal. The author concludes that whoever is ignorant of these arguments has no rational ground and merely follows tradition. Finally, since both possible statements (the two dualist positions) are shown to be false, the truth must lie with monotheism.

Key Terms
الطبع
الاجتماع
التباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism could be true if the two principles have different natures.
Correction: If they have opposite natures, they cannot coexist, so dualism fails.

Checkpoint
What does the author observe about things with opposite natural dispositions?
A) They can coexist with effort.
B) They cannot come together ever.
C) They merge into one.
D) They alternate positions.

Key Takeaway
Opposite natural dispositions cannot coexist, refuting the dualist claim of two eternal principles with opposite natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own inner conflicts. Do you harbor contradictory intentions in your heart—wanting both to obey Allah and follow your desires? Are these not like the rising and sinking substances that cannot coexist? What must you remove to find consistency in faith?
?
The dualists claimed two eternal sources of good and evil. Have you ever subtly acted as if there are two sources of authority in your life—Allah and your own whims? In what specific decision today did you listen to your whims over Allah's command?
?
The argument shows that opposite natures cannot come together. In your relationships, are you trying to hold together beliefs or practices that are fundamentally opposed to Islam? What false peace have you made between truth and falsehood?

Refutation by Choice
Maturidi refutes the choice-based relationship in dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the assumption of voluntary interaction between dualistic principles leads to absurdities like impotence and ignorance.

Core Idea
If the two principles act by choice, they would be able to prevent each other, leading to contradiction.

Having refuted the natural disposition argument, Imam Maturidi now considers the second possibility: that the two principles interact by their own choice. If they have free will, then each should be able to prevent the other's action. This leads to a situation where their intended outcomes are frustrated, implying impotence and ignorance, which is incompatible with their supposed divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the hypothetical about choice.
This argument is more complex than the first.
2
Identify the assumption
Assume the two principles act by choice. Then each would have the power to prevent the other.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If each can prevent the other, then neither can achieve its desired effect, leading to impotence and ignorance being attributed to both.
4
Recognize the absurdity
This contradicts their eternal and powerful nature, thus the choice-based model is false.

?

?
In what areas of your life do you act as if there are two equal forces at play, such as blaming your environment for your sins while taking credit for your virtues? How does this refutation call you to accept full responsibility before the One God?
?
If you truly believed that attributing choice to two conflicting principles leads to ignorance and impotence, how would that change the way you approach the struggle between your better nature and your lower self?

Refutation by Third Cause and Conclusion to Tawhid
Maturidi refutes the third-cause model and concludes with tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the assumption of a third cause leads to contingency and affirms monotheism.

Core Idea
If a third cause unites the two principles, they become contingent, proving monotheism.

Imam Maturidi now considers the final possibility: that the relationship between the two principles is due to a third cause. If they are brought together by another, then they are not self-sufficient and are contingent. This leads to the necessity of admitting their createdness, which in turn supports monotheism (tawhid). The author also concludes that the dualist positions are false, so the truth must be monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the logical steps.
2
Identify the third cause argument
If the two principles are united by a third cause, then they are not eternal and self-existent.
3
Understand the implication for tawhid
This leads to the necessity of admitting a single creator (Allah).
4
Follow the final conclusion
The author concludes that both dualist positions (by nature and by choice) have been refuted, so the truth is monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states: 'There is no power except with Allah.' In which areas of your life do you still rely on your own strength or on others instead of turning completely to Allah? Will you consciously invoke His power before your next decision?
?
The author says that those ignorant of these arguments resort to taqlid (blind following). Are you personally convinced of the rational basis of your belief in Allah, or do you rely on inherited faith? Which specific belief of yours would you struggle to prove from revelation and reason?
?
The dualists believed in two eternal principles. Do you ever act as if there are two sources of power in your life—Allah and your own planning? Where do you need to affirm monotheism more practically, especially in moments of fear or ambition?

Critique of Mu'tazila on God's Power
A marginal note criticizes Mu'tazila's restricted view of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that limits God's power over small acts and the refutation based on the notion of possibility.

Core Idea
Denying God's power over any act contradicts His attribute of being 'Powerful over everything'.

This lesson examines a critical note from the margins of a classical theological work. The note challenges the Mu'tazila claim that God's power does not extend to minor acts like those of a mosquito. It highlights the inconsistency between their verbal affirmation that God is powerful over everything and their actual restriction of that power to what is rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the sharp tone of the critique.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Find the two claims attributed to Mu'tazila: 1) God cannot create the act of a mosquito, and 2) those acts are under the power of something else.
3
Examine the contradiction
The note says they fail to realize their own claim that God is powerful over everything, as they limit it to what is possible in the intellect.
4
Understand the refutation
The note argues that it is meaningless to oppose such people with arguments about the impossible, because they only accept what is within the realm of possibility.
This shows a disagreement on the definition of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه وصاحب الكتاب ينتحل نخلة الاعتزال، ومذهبهم أن الله لا يقدر على خلق فعل بعوض فما فوقه من الجواهر، وفعل ذلك كله واقع تحت القدرة أو في ذلك لغيره قدرة. فأبوا تحقيق ما ادعوا من أنه قادر على كل شيء في أكثر الأشياء التي هي في حد الإمكان في العقول. فمعارضة أمثالهم بالخارج عن حد الإمكان في العقول لا معنى له . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
In it: The author adopts the palm tree of Mu'tazila, and their doctrine is that God is not able to create the act of a mosquito or what is above it among the atoms, and all of that act is under power or the power of another. So they refused to realize what they claimed: that He is powerful over everything in most things that are within the realm of possibility in the intellects. So opposing their likes with what is outside the realm of possibility in the intellects has no meaning. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage appears to be a marginal note criticizing the author (Maturidi) for adopting Mu'tazila views. It states that the Mu'tazila deny that God has power over the smallest acts (like a mosquito's act), claiming those acts are within the power of creatures. Then it says that they failed to substantiate their claim that God is powerful over all things, limiting it to what is possible in the intellect. The note concludes that it is meaningless to oppose such people with arguments about what is outside the realm of possibility. This is a brief refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine power.

Misconception: Some think that God's power is limited only to what is logically possible, and that He cannot create acts of creatures.
Correction: The note shows that the Mu'tazila hold this view, but it is criticized as contradictory to the claim that God is powerful over everything. The note implies that God's power is not limited by rational possibility.

The Mu'tazila's restriction of God's power to rationally possible things contradicts their own affirmation that He is powerful over everything.

Exposing the Evasion in the Question
Maturidi exposes the sophistry in the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the logical evasion in the question about God creating a like of Himself.

Core Idea
The questioner's evasion lies in asking about creating a being that cannot be created by definition.

Maturidi begins by showing that a careful reading of the earlier discussion reveals the questioner's sophistry. The question assumes that a 'like' of Allah could be created, but anything created cannot truly be like the eternal Creator. This lesson unpacks that evasion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi identifies the evasion in the question.
2
Identify the key points of the evasion
Maturidi argues that the questioner asks about creating a 'like' but such a being would have to be eternal and uncreated, which contradicts the very idea of creation. Find these points.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this evasion is significant for theology: it shows that the question is invalid from the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن تأمل ما ذكر عرف حَيْد السائل في المسؤول عن سنن القول فيما له احتمال التمكن في العقول، لأنه سأل : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ؟ ومن يكون مثله لا يكون جسما ولا عرضاً ولا محدثًا ولا محتملاً للفناء؛ لأنه إن كان على شيء من ذلك فلا يكون مثله، وبه سأل؛ فكيف يبقى ذلك الذي ذكر . وما ذكر هو أبعاض ما في كون ذلك نفيه ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقد قلنا على المعتزلة ما هو أوضح من ذلك. مع ما يلزمهم شيء آخر من وجه آخر، وهو أنهم يصفون الله بالقدرة على الكذب والسفه والظلم مما لو كان شيء من ذلك فيه لتبطل ربوبيته، ثم لم يجز فعله ذلك لذلك . فليقل : يقدر على خلق مثله ولكن لا يفعل. لأنه ليست الأعجوبة في جعل الحدث قديما، وما يحتمل الفناء غير فان، وما يقع عليه أثر الصنع غير واقع ذلك إلا بالأعجوبة في جعل القديم حديثًا والباقي فانيا والحكيم سفيها ؛ فإن استقامت القدرة على هذا - على إحالة الفعل - فمثله الأول على مذهبهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الإحالة على مذهبنا سهل، وهو أن الله جل جلاله محال دخوله تحت القدرة، فالقول
The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever ponders what I have mentioned will recognize the evasion of the questioner regarding what is asked concerning the course of speech about what is possible in the intellects. Because he asked: 'Can He create a like of Himself?' But one who is like Him would not be a body, nor an accident, nor originated, nor susceptible to annihilation; because if it were any of that, it would not be like Him. And with this he asked; so how can that which he mentioned remain? What he mentioned are parts of what constitute the negation of that. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have already said to the Mu'tazila what is clearer than this. Along with something else that is necessary for them from another aspect: that they describe God as having power over lying, foolishness, and injustice, such that if any of that were in Him, His lordship would be nullified; yet He does not do that because of that. So let it be said: He is able to create a like of Himself but He does not do it. Because the wonder is not in making the originated eternal, or what is susceptible to annihilation not annihilated, or what is subject to the effect of making not actualized, except in making the eternal originated, the remaining annihilated, and the wise foolish. If power is valid over this—over the impossibility of the act—then the first like is valid according to their doctrine. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the impossibility according to our doctrine is easy: that Allah, glorified be He, is impossible to fall under power; so the statement…
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the previous question and a refutation of the Mu'tazila. Maturidi first points out the evasion in the question: the questioner asks about creating a like of God, but such a being would have to be eternal and not body, accident, etc., which contradicts the very concept of creation. Then he shifts to criticize the Mu'tazila: they attribute to God power over sinful actions (lying, foolishness, injustice) but then say such actions are not done because they would negate His lordship. Maturidi argues that this is inconsistent: if they accept that God has power over impossible things (like creating a like), they should also accept that He can create a like but simply does not do it. He concludes that according to his own (Maturidi's) school, the impossibility is straightforward: God cannot be subject to power (i.e., He cannot be created), so the question is moot. The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
حَيْد
سائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can create a being exactly like Himself in all attributes.
Correction: Any created being is necessarily originated and contingent, whereas Allah is eternal and necessary. A true 'like' would be another eternal uncaused being, which is impossible to create.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the 'evasion' in the question?
A) The questioner avoids answering a previous question
B) The questioner asks about creating a being that cannot be created by definition
C) The questioner denies God's power
D) The questioner uses unclear language

Key Takeaway
The question about creating a like of God is self-contradictory because any created being cannot be truly like the uncreated Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's power out of intellectual pride, thinking you could imagine a limitation? How does Maturidi's exposure of this self-contradictory question humble your intellect before the divine? Will you commit to examining your assumptions before questioning Allah's attributes?
?
This passage shows that some questions about God are inherently flawed. Are there any questions you have wrestled with that might also be based on false assumptions about God's nature? What would it mean for you to submit your reason to revelation rather than relying on your own speculation?
?
Maturidi ends with 'there is no power except with Allah.' In your own life, where do you need to acknowledge that your doubts and questions are powerless without divine guidance? Will you actively turn to Allah for clarity and accept His perfection?

The Mu'tazila Inconsistency on God's Power
Maturidi criticizes Mu'tazila inconsistency and states his own position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila inconsistency regarding God's power over sinful actions.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they affirm God's power over sin yet deny He does it because it would negate His lordship.

Maturidi now turns to the Mu'tazila, pointing out a contradiction in their theology. They describe God as having power over lying, foolishness, and injustice, but then claim He would not do these because they would nullify His lordship. This lesson explores that criticism and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, and follow how Maturidi builds his critique of the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position

3
Understand Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi argues that if power is valid over the impossible (like making the eternal originated), then by the same logic, God could create a like of Himself but simply chooses not to. Notice the parallel he draws.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's own position
The passage ends with Maturidi's own view: that it is impossible for God to fall under power, so the question of creating a like is moot.
The text is incomplete here but the point is clear.

Refuting Mu'tazila on Divine Power Over Evil
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila by comparing their view on divine power over evil to the claim of creating a like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by arguing that their own reasoning about God's power over evil actions forces them to admit that God can create a like of Himself.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila’s own logic forces them to admit God could create a like.

This lesson examines a sharp argument from Imam Maturidi against the Mu'tazila. He points out an inconsistency: they claim God has power to do evil but never does, yet deny God can create a like of Himself. Maturidi shows that the same principle should apply to both cases, turning their own reasoning against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: Maturidi states his earlier argument, then adds a new one, drawing a comparison between God's power over evil and His power to create a like.
The passage uses the term 'wonder' (ʿujūba) which is a key concept.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold that God has power over evil actions (lying, foolishness, injustice) but never does them because that would invalidate His lordship. Recognize this as the premise Maturidi uses.
3
Understand Maturidi's argument by analogy
Maturidi argues: if the Mu'tazila can say God has power over evil but refrains, then they should also say God can create a like but refrains. The underlying principle is the same: power to do something that is impossible (due to divine attributes) but not actually doing it.
The 'wonder' is not in making the impossible possible, but in the fact that power is attributed to God over things that would be inconsistent with His nature.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazila's own view leads to the admission that God can create a like. This refutes their denial of that possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قلنا على المعتزلة ما هو أوضح من ذلك. مع ما يلزمهم [شيء آخر] من وجه آخر، وهو أنهم يصفون الله بالقدرة على الكذب والسفه والظلم مما لو كان شيء من ذلك [فيه] لتبطل ربوبيته، ثم لم يجز فعله ذلك لذلك . فليقل : يقدر على خلق مثله ولكن لا يفعل. لأنه ليست الأعجوبة في جعل الحدث قديما، وما يحتمل الفناء غير فان، وما يقع عليه أثر الصنع غير واقع ذلك إلا بالأعجوبة في جعل القديم حديثًا والباقي فانيا والحكيم سفيها ؛ فإن استقامت القدرة على هذا - على إحالة الفعل - فمثله الأول على مذهبهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
We have already said against the Mu'tazila what is clearer than that. Together with what necessitates them [something else] from another aspect, which is that they describe God with power over lying, foolishness, and injustice such that if anything of that were [in Him] His lordship would be invalidated, then He does not permit its action because of that. So let him say: He can create a like of Him but does not do it. Because the wonder is not in making the originated eternal, and what is subject to annihilation not perishing, and what is affected by the trace of making not occurring, except by the wonder in making the eternal originated, the lasting perishing, and the wise foolish; if power is consistent with this—upon the impossibility of the act—then His like is the first according to their school. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila (a rationalist theological school). The author states that he has already presented clearer arguments, but adds another: the Mu'tazila describe God as having power (qudra) to lie, act foolishly, and be unjust—but they say He never does so because it would invalidate His lordship (rubūbiyya). The author argues that by this logic, they should also say that God has power to create a like of Himself but does not do so for the same reason. The core point is that the 'wonder' (ʿujūba) would not be in making the originated eternal (which is impossible), but rather in making what is possible under power actually occur despite its impossibility. If the Mu'tazila accept that God can do evil but refrains, they should also accept that God can create a like but refrains. Thus their own principle supports the author's position.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
قدرة (power)
كذب (lying)
سفه (foolishness)
ظلم (injustice)
ربوبية (lordship)
أعجوبة (wonder)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe God's power is limited to only good actions, and that He cannot have power over evil or create a like.
Correction: Maturidi shows that they themselves admit God has power over evil (but does not act), so consistency demands they admit God also has power to create a like.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Mu'tazila say about God's power over evil actions?
A) God has no power over evil actions
B) God has power over evil actions but never does them
C) God does evil actions sometimes
D) Evil actions are not possible for God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes an inconsistency in Mu'tazila reasoning: if God can have power over evil but not act, He can also have power to create a like but not act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson forces you to confront the logical consequences of believing that God has power over evil but never acts on it. How do you personally reconcile God's absolute power with the existence of evil in the world? Does your heart find peace in the Maturidi approach, or do you sense a tension that you have been avoiding? Be honest with yourself: are there doubts you have suppressed about God's control over evil? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to entrust God's wisdom regarding evil that you don't understand.
?
The Mu'tazila were caught in an inconsistency because they held a view that logically led to an absurdity. Think about your own beliefs: Are there any doctrines you accept without examining their logical implications? Specifically, do you ever attribute to God a quality that, if fully realized, would contradict His perfection? This lesson calls you to test your own reasoning. Is there an area of your faith where you hold two contradictory ideas about God? What would it cost you to bring them into alignment with what you truly believe about His nature? Commit to identifying one such inconsistency this week and seeking truth through study.
?
Maturidi says the 'wonder' is in attributing power to God over acts that are impossible for His perfect nature ('the wise foolish'). Have you ever considered that God's inability to do evil is not a weakness but a perfection? How does that change the way you view your own limitations? Do you secretly wish God would be 'more powerful' in the sense of being able to do evil, even though you know He is good? Confess to yourself any hidden desire for a God who acts according to your own standards of power rather than His perfect nature. Today, pray that God would increase your trust in His wise goodness, even when His ways are mysterious.

Why Maturidi Cites Mu'tazilite Views
Maturidi explains his reason for citing Mu'tazilite views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rationale for discussing opposing views in the context of establishing tawhid.

Core Idea
Maturidi cites the Mu'tazilites not for their content, but to expose their insufficiency.

Maturidi begins by clarifying his intention in mentioning the sayings of the Mu'tazilites. Although he states he had no need to mention them, he does so to demonstrate that no one can establish tawhid or refute heretics using their principles. The true path to tawhid lies elsewhere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's direct tone.
2
Identify the purpose
Find where Maturidi states his reason for mentioning the Mu'tazilites. He says it is so that the contemplator may know something.
The key phrase is 'ليعلم المتأمل'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why simply knowing that tawhid cannot be established on Mu'tazilite principles is valuable. It steers one toward the correct approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما أردت بما ذكرت من أقاويل المعتزلة - وإن لم يكن لي إلى ذكرها حاجة - ليعلم المتأمل أن لا سبيل إلى إثبات التوحيد ودفع معارضات الملحدة على مذهبهم، وأن الحق من القول في التوحيد قول غيرهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم زعم أن كل قادر سبقت قدرة فعله فعله، فهو وصف من قدر بغير، وفعله بغيره، فهو يتحول من حال إلى حال، وتقبل ذاته الاستحالة والزوال. فأما الله سبحانه فبنفسه يقدر على الأشياء ويفعلها. فما يذكره في ذلك فاسد، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد بينا فيما تقدم بأبلغ من هذا. وبالله التوفيق. وفي هذا آية جعل ذاته عالمة، وقد بينا وهمه.
And I only intended by what I have mentioned of the sayings of the Mu'tazilites—even though I had no need to mention them—so that the contemplator may know that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the heretics according to their school, and that the truth in the statement about tawhid is the statement of others. And there is no power but in God. Then he claimed that every powerful [agent] whose power precedes his action is a description of one who has power by another, and his action by another, so he changes from state to state, and his essence admits transformation and passing away. As for God Almighty, He by Himself is powerful over things and does them. So what he mentions about that is corrupt, and there is no power but in God. And we have explained in what preceded more clearly than this. And with God is success. And in this is a sign that He made His essence knowing? and we have explained his error.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi first explains his purpose in citing the Mu'tazilite views: not because they are needed, but to show that tawhid cannot be established on their principles, and the correct view is that of other schools (i.e., Ahl al-Sunna). Then he critiques the concept that a powerful agent must have a power that precedes his action. That would imply the agent's power is from another and his action is from another, leading to change and contingency. God, however, is powerful by Himself and does not need a prior power. Therefore any other view is invalid. He also mentions a point about 'making His essence knowing', which has already been refuted.

Key Terms
توحيد (tawḥīd)
معتزلة (mu‘tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi includes Mu'tazilite views for completeness or debate.
Correction: Maturidi says he has no need to mention them; he does so only to show their inadequacy.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi mention the sayings of the Mu'tazilites?
A) To support their views.
B) To show that tawhid cannot be established on their principles.
C) To list all known opinions.
D) To confuse the reader.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi cites opposing views not because they are needed, but to demonstrate their failure to establish tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi mentions views he has no need for, purely to help the contemplator see the truth. When you encounter false beliefs in conversations or online, is your first impulse to correct them or to feel defensive? How can you adopt Maturidi's posture of calmly exposing error to guide others to the truth, without being drawn into unproductive debates? This week, pick one common misunderstanding about tawhid and prepare a brief, charitable explanation of why the correct view is more coherent—not to win an argument, but to help a seeker.
?
The passage ends with 'لا قوة إلا بالله'—acknowledging that no power exists except through God. In your own efforts to strengthen your faith or defend it against doubts, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on Allah's power? What would change if you started each attempt to understand tawhid with a conscious admission that you are powerless without Him? Commit to saying 'لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله' before your next study session.

Refuting the Concept of Prior Power for God
Maturidi refutes the concept of prior power and mentions a sign about God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's power is not preceded by a separate ability.

Core Idea
If power precedes action, the agent depends on another, implying change — impossible for God.

Maturidi now addresses a specific claim about divine power. Some argue that every powerful agent requires a power that precedes his action. Maturidi shows this entails contingency and change, which cannot apply to God. God is powerful by Himself, without any prior power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about prior power
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم زعم' until the end. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The passage contains a conditional argument.
2
Understand the claimed view
Identify the claim: every powerful agent whose power precedes his action. Maturidi will unpack the implications.
3
Follow Maturidi's critique
Notice the steps: if power precedes action, then the agent has power by another and acts by another, leading to change and passing away.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Contrast with God
Maturidi states that God is powerful by Himself and acts directly. Therefore the claimed view is corrupt.
The phrase 'بنفسه' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And I only intended by what I have mentioned of the sayings of the Mu'tazilites—even though I had no need to mention them—so that the contemplator may know that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the heretics according to their school, and that the truth in the statement about tawhid is the statement of others. And there is no power but in God. Then he claimed that every powerful [agent] whose power precedes his action is a description of one who has power by another, and his action by another, so he changes from state to state, and his essence admits transformation and passing away. And we have explained in what preceded more clearly than this. And with God is success. And in this is a sign that He made His essence knowing? and we have explained his error.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary

That would imply the agent's power is from another and his action is from another, leading to change and contingency. God, however, is powerful by Himself and does not need a prior power. Therefore any other view is invalid. He also mentions a point about 'making His essence knowing', which has already been refuted.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
استحالة (istiḥāla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is like human power, requiring a separate ability before each action.
Correction: Maturidi explains that this would imply change and contingency, which are impossible for God. God is powerful by Himself without any prior power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would it imply if God's power preceded His action?
A) God would be more powerful.
B) God's essence would be subject to change and passing away.
C) God would still be eternal.
D) It would prove His power.

Key Takeaway
God is powerful by Himself, without any power preceding His action; any claim to the contrary implies imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where God's help seems delayed, do you ever slip into thinking that God's power is 'out of reach' or needs to 'activate'? How does knowing that God is powerful and acts by Himself (بنفسه) challenge that? What will you consciously remind yourself of the next time you feel impatient?
?
If God's power does not precede His actions, then every action of God is immediate and direct. Are there prayers you have made where you doubted God's ability to answer because you thought He needed 'time' or 'means'? This lesson strips that excuse away. How will you change your dua habits starting today?
?
The text argues that prior power implies change and imperfection. Is there any area in your life where you subconsciously expect God to 'change His mind' or 'react' to your deeds? Reflect on your view of God's constancy. What specific step can you take this week to align your belief with this truth that God does not change?

Mutual Limitation and Boundaries Refute Dualism
Maturidi presents two arguments: mutual limitation and boundary contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that light and darkness cannot be eternal because they exhibit ignorance, weakness, and have boundaries that imply contingency.

Core Idea
Imperfection and boundaries prove that light and darkness are not divine.

Maturidi begins his refutation of dualism by pointing out that according to dualist accounts themselves, both light and darkness exhibit ignorance and weakness. Light falls into the bonds of darkness, and darkness is prevented from doing evil. Furthermore, each has a limit on one side but not others, implying contingency. This lesson examines these first two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument on mutual limitation
Read the opening lines where Maturidi notes that light is ignorant for falling into darkness's bonds, and darkness is ignorant for being prevented from its natural action. This shows both are imperfect.
Notice how Maturidi uses the dualists' own claims against them.
2
Analyze the boundary argument
Read the second part about each principle having a limit on one side. Understand the logical options: if being unbounded indicates eternity, then the bounded side implies contingency; if not, both sides are contingent.
This argument targets the idea of two eternal principles directly.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Maturidi ends this section by exalting Allah above such attributes. The limitations of light and darkness prove they cannot be God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا يبطل على من يقول بالظلمة والنور، لما جهل النور حيث وقع في وثاق الآخر، والظلمة حيث منعت عن عملها - وهو الشر - في الآخر، ومع ما تفرقت أجزاؤهما وتشتتت أحوالهما حتى عجز كل واحد منهما أن يظهر سلطانه ويستولي على ما له. جل ربنا وتعالى عن أن تكون هذه صفته.
وأيضا إن القول من أصحاب الاثنين قول بنهاية كل واحد منهما من جانب، وارتفاعها من سائر الجوانب؛ فإن كان الارتفاع دليل القدم لزم الحدث في وجه الحد، وإن لم يكن لزم الحدث في الكل. مع ما إن لم يقدر النور على تخليص جزئه المتناهي عن يد عدوه بالأجزاء التي لا تتناهى، ولا كانت تلك الأجزاء قدرت على حفظ ذلك الجزء من يديها قبل الوقوع في وثاقها، أنّى يقدر إذا أراد بعد الوقوع في وثاق الظلمة التخلص من قيده؟ وعلى قول من يجعل الحواس كلها للنور دون الظلمة والعلم كله، وكذلك جميع ما يليق بالرب، فهي إذا إله أعمى لا يبصر، عاجز لا يقدر، ضعيف لا يقوى، شر بالطبع لا بالقوة. فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن العدول عن سبيله والابتلاء بشبكة الثنوية، فإنه لا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما كل اثنين لا بد من انفراد كل بمكان إن كان جسما أو عرضا؛ فإن كان عرضا فمفارقته توجب تلفه، وإن كان جسما فإما أن يكون مكان كل واحد منهما من جوهره فلا يقوم في مضادة حاله كالمائي في البر والليلي الممنوع من البصر بالنهار. وإن كان من غير جوهره ألف الخير بالشر، والشر بالخير، وذلك ينقض معتمدهم في القول بالعدد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And this invalidates the saying of those who affirm darkness and light, because light is ignorant when it fell into the bonds of the other, and darkness is ignorant when it was prevented from its action – which is evil – in the other, and with that their parts separated and their states dispersed until each became incapable of manifesting its authority and prevailing over what belongs to it. Exalted is our Lord, far above having such attributes. And also, the saying of the proponents of two is a saying that each has a limit from one side and its removal from all other sides; if the removal is evidence of eternity, then contingency is necessitated on the side of limit, and if not, then contingency is necessitated in the whole. Moreover, if light cannot rescue its finite part from the hand of its enemy by means of infinite parts, and those parts were not able to preserve that part from its hands before it fell into its bonds, then how can it, when it desires after falling into the bonds of darkness, free itself from its shackles? According to the one who makes all senses belong to light and not darkness, and all knowledge, and likewise all that befits the Lord, then it is a blind god who does not see, incapable who does not have power, weak who does not have strength, evil by nature not by power. So we ask God to protect us from deviating from His path and from being afflicted with the snare of dualism, for there is no power except with God. Moreover, every pair must have each one separate in a place, whether it is a body or an accident; if it is an accident, its separation necessitates its annihilation, and if it is a body, then either the place of each is of its essence, so it does not stand in opposition to its state, like water on land and the nocturnal one prevented from sight by day. Or if it is not of its essence, it harmonizes good with evil and evil with good, and that invalidates their basis for asserting duality. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the dualist belief in two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi points out that according to dualist accounts, light fell into the bonds of darkness and cannot free itself, showing ignorance and weakness. Darkness too is prevented from doing evil in the realm of light, showing limitation. Both are thus imperfect. Moreover, if each has a limit on one side but not others, that limit implies contingency. Also, if light is said to have all power and knowledge, then its inability to rescue its trapped part shows it is blind and weak. Finally, any two distinct beings must occupy separate places; if they are bodies, they either have intrinsic places (leading to contradictions) or extrinsic places (allowing mixing of good and evil), which undermines dualism. Thus, the true God is one, perfect, and free from such defects.

Key Terms
نور
ظلمة
وثاق
حد
نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two eternal beings can exist without any limitation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that by their own accounts, light and darkness are limited, proving they are contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does light's falling into the bonds of darkness show it is not eternal?
A) Because it shows light is weak.
B) Because it shows light is evil.
C) Because it shows light is a body.
D) Because it shows light is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Both light and darkness exhibit ignorance and limitation, proving they cannot be eternal or divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, where do you see 'boundaries'—areas where you lack knowledge, power, or control? How does this passage challenge you to stop attributing divine qualities to yourself or others?
?
Have you ever placed your trust in something that seemed limitless but eventually showed its limits—a person, a system, or your own abilities? What did that reveal about your need for the truly Unlimited?
?
The argument says that even light, often associated with goodness, is ignorant. In what area of your life are you most confident you 'know' the truth, and how can this passage humble you to admit your limits before God?

Impotence and Spatial Separation Refute Dualism
Maturidi argues from light's impotence and spatial necessity against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments that if light has all power and knowledge it cannot be divine because it fails to free its trapped part, and that spatial separation between two eternal beings leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
A deity that cannot rescue its own part is no deity at all.

Maturidi continues by targeting the dualist claim that light possesses all senses and knowledge. He argues that if light is all-powerful, why can't it free its trapped part? Then he addresses the spatial implications of two eternal beings: they must occupy separate places, leading to contradictions. This lesson covers these final arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about light's inability to free its part
Read the passage starting with 'Moreover, if light cannot rescue its finite part...' Notice the logic: if light had infinite parts, why couldn't it save its finite part?
Maturidi uses a sorites-style argument to show impotence.
2
Analyze the implications for light's nature
Consider that if light has all senses and knowledge, its failure makes it blind and weak—contradicting divinity.
Maturidi then says such a being would be evil by nature.
3
Examine the spatial separation argument
Read the final part about every pair needing separate places. Understand the dilemma: if accidents, separation destroys them; if bodies, either intrinsic places cause contradictions or extrinsic places mix good and evil.
This argument attacks the core of dualism's possibility.

?

This passage shows that any being with limitations cannot be divine. Where do you still unconsciously rely on 'lesser gods'? What would it take to redirect that reliance entirely to Allah?
?
The dualist believed in a god of light that was weak and blind. Do you ever act as if Allah's power is limited by circumstances or people? How would your daily choices change if you truly internalized that no force can oppose Allah's will—not even your own shortcomings?
?
If you genuinely believed that good and evil cannot mix from two independent sources, how would this reshape your understanding of why trials and suffering exist? Can you identify a specific trial where you blamed 'bad luck' or 'the devil' instead of submitting to Allah's perfect wisdom? What step can you take today to replace that blame with trust?

Introduction to Refuting the Materialists (Dahriyya)
Maturidi introduces his method for refuting the Dahriyya by exposing their views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodological approach to refuting the Dahriyya by first exposing their doctrines.

Core Idea
Exposing false doctrines is itself a proof of their corruption.

Maturidi begins his refutation of the materialists by outlining a simple but powerful method: he will first present their views as reported by reliable sources. The very act of making their doctrines manifest, he argues, reveals their incoherence. This introduction sets the stage for the detailed arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the author states his method before giving any arguments.
The passage is a single sentence after the heading.
2
Identify the key claim about method
Maturidi says that manifesting their doctrines is one of the proofs of their corruption. Ask yourself why he considers exposition itself as evidence.
This is a methodological principle.
3
Distinguish agreement and disagreement
Note that all Dahriyya agree on the eternity of the world's clay (primordial matter) but differ on whether its form (craft) is eternal or created. This sets the field for refutation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding their internal splits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان فساد أقاويل الدهرية
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم نذكر أقاويل الدهرية على ما ذكره ابن شبيب وغيره لتظهر مذاهبهم، فإن ظهورها أحد أدلة فسادها، بعد أن يُعلم اتفاقهم في قدم طينة العالم واختلافهم في قدم الصنعة وحدثها. وهذا جملة مذاهبهم.
Exposition of the corruption of the sayings of the Dahriyya. Abu Mansur – may God have mercy on him – said: Then we mention the sayings of the Dahriyya according to what Ibn Shabib and others mentioned, so that their doctrines may become manifest, for their manifestation is one of the proofs of their corruption, after it is known that they agree on the eternity of the clay of the world but differ on the eternity of craft and its temporality. And this is the summary of their doctrines.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section in which the author will refute the materialists (Dahriyya), who believe in the eternity of matter. Maturidi states that he will present their views as reported by Ibn Shabib and others. The mere exposure of their doctrines, he argues, serves as evidence of their falsehood. He notes that all Dahriyya agree that the substance of the world is eternal, but they differ on whether the formation (craft) of the world is eternal or originated. This sets the stage for the subsequent detailed refutation.

Key Terms
الدهرية
طينة العالم
الصنعة
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is simply describing their views without criticism or evaluation.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the manifestation of their doctrines is 'one of the proofs of their corruption', so the exposition itself is part of the refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the one point on which all Dahriyya agree according to Maturidi?
A) The world is created.
B) The clay (matter) of the world is eternal.
C) The craft of the world is eternal.
D) There is one God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's method: exposing the doctrines of the Dahriyya is itself a proof of their falsehood, setting up the detailed refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi says that exposing false doctrines is itself a proof of their corruption. What false belief about yourself or your life are you afraid to expose to the light, because you sense it would crumble under honest scrutiny? Will you write it down today and examine it?
?
The Dahriyya agreed on one fundamental error (eternal clay) but disagreed on secondary matters. Where in your faith or worldview do you hold an unexamined foundational assumption that you have never questioned? What would change if you let the truth challenge that foundation?
?
The mere manifestation of the Dahriyya's views revealed their corruption. Think of a time you defended an idea or action without letting yourself truly see it. Can you now state it clearly, as Maturidi does for the Dahriyya, and accept that its falsehood may become obvious?
